AUTHOR iii
# About The Author
Karl Knopf, EdD
ISSA Director of Fitness for
the Physically Limited
Dr. Knopf is the co-author of the ISSA Senior
Fitness Certification course and the ISSA Exercise / Fitness Therapy Certification course. He began his teaching career at Foothill College in Los Altos, California in 1976 as the coordinator of Adapted Physical Education. The program has evolved into the largest in California. Dr. Knopf has been instrumental in developing programs for mature adults, persons with arthritis and chronic
pain, persons with disabilities, and the developmentally delayed. He has written three books: Water Workouts, Fitness Over Fifty, and his most recent, Senior Fitness for the ISSA. Dr. Knopf has been writing, consulting and speaking professionally throughout his career and is a frequent guest on television and radio talk shows. Since 1986 he has been a consultant with Stanford School of Medicine for older adult research projects and is a recent recipient of the respected NISOD, Excellence in Teaching Award. The ISSA is proud to have Dr. Knopf join our growing staff and lend his expertise in the field of Adaptive Physical Education and Older Adult Fitness.

PREFACESenior Fitness
PREFACE vii
America, as well as the rest of the world, is graying. Adults over the
age of fifty represent a large and important segment of the popula-tion, which is often under-served by the fitness community. Ifsomething is not done soon to keep this group fit for life, manyolder adults are going to ‚Äúrust out‚Äù before their time. As this groupmoves through middle age and into old age, if they are not proper-ly maintained and kept well, the medical costs to keep this groupalive will be enormous. Many of the causes for poor health in olderadults are the result of poor health decisions. This, coupled with theeffects of a sedentary lifestyle, makes the later years more challeng-ing. Many fifty-plus adults already have some chronic health issuesthat necessitate a lifestyle change in addition to corrective exercise.Fortunately for older adults, good choices and regular physicalactivity can remediate many of these health issues.
The medical system of today is not well equipped to handle long-
term, chronic conditions. The medical system is good at ‚Äúfixing‚Äùsick bodies but is not very good at preventing illness. While ourhealth care system does an excellent job of handling acute condi-tions, it often fails to keep ‚Äúfixed‚Äù patients motivated to follow awellness program to prevent further problems after they have beendischarged. The fitness educator of the future can play a pivotal rolein the health care system. The future looks good for fitness profes-sionals who know how to keep chronic health conditions fromadvancing. Also, the fitness educator who can work in partnershipwith the medical community to provide successful rehabilitationwill be highly sought after. An expert in the field of gerontologysaid, ‚ÄúMost things that get worse with age can be positively influ-enced with regular and prudent exercise.‚Äù With proper wellnessintervention, older adults can be healthier and fitter tomorrow thanthey were yesterday. Isn‚Äôt that the fountain of youth?
Unfortunately, what many of today‚Äôs fitness professionals lack is the
ability to address the unique needs and varied functional abilities of
older adults. Fitness professionals of the future should be able to
individualize instruction based on the health status of the client.While staying within their professional scope of practice, theyshould be able to provide a safe and effective fitness program forenhancing quality of life that is appropriate to the client‚Äôs goals.Most personal trainers in the gym are not trained to treat thisimportant segment of the population. The fitness educator of thefuture who can work together with older adult clients and theirtherapists to keep older individuals operating at an optimal capac-ity will be highly valued.
Many times when older adults wish to improve their fitness and
wellness, they go to a gym and sign up with a trainer who pushesthem to work too hard and too fast only to get hurt and drop out.Other older adults feel they can do it all themselves and try to pickup where they left off when they were younger. They end up usingoutdated methods and once again fail, get hurt and quit. What isurgently needed is a group of knowledgeable and compassionateinstructors who can assess the client‚Äôs age, health status, goals, andviiiSenior Fitness

lifestyle then incorporate all of these elements into a realistic fit-
ness program. An older adult cannot and should not be trained inthe same manner as a young person. This is where this bookcomes in. It is designed to give the reader the most practical andrealistic information available to train older adults to improvetheir functional fitness in order to increase their quality of life foras long as possible.
This book addresses the skills and techniques necessary for today‚Äôs
fitness educator to completely serve active older adults. Its focus isto provide the reader with the skill set necessary to be a respectedmember of the wellness community. The creed of the medical com-munity is to ‚Äúdo no harm,‚Äù which is also the motto of this book.Everything containedwithin this book is basedon sound science. Thebook does not go intodetail to explain this sci-ence, but there are manyexcellent texts thataddress this very well.Also the book is notdesigned to inform thereader how to work withthe frail elderly. Rather,its goal is to explain howto empower the olderadult to avoid becomingfrail and losing the abili-ty to function fully with-in the mainstream of life, to help the older individual continue tocarry groceries, walk the dog, climb stairs, reach for items on highshelves, and so forth. This book is appropriate for all fitness educa-tors. Group exercise teachers, personal trainers, health/wellnesseducators, fitness facility managers, therapists, and activity direc-tors will all benefit from the information presented.

‚Ä¢ Become knowledgeable about the needs of

‚Ä¢ Describe current trends regarding aging

sional of being able to work effectively with an
aging population
‚Ä¢ Explain the difference between chronological age

T oday, people are living healthier into old age with fewer disabili-
ties, and this trend is expected to continue. For older adults oftoday, life has never been so good. Life expectancy at birth hasincreased by 4.7 years for men and 3.5 years for women over the lasttwenty years of the twentieth century. Most older adults are living alifestyle that wasn‚Äôt imaginable twenty or thirty years ago. T oday‚Äôsolder adults are less disabled, and the rate of disability is on thedecline as well.
Of course, more can be done to postpone chronic health problems.
This is where the fitness education of older adults can play a pivotalrole. If the fitness educator of older adults could teach simple
lifestyle modifications such as increased exercise, weight control,
smoking cessation, and healthy eating habits, the risk of lifestyle ill-nesses like cancer, cerebrovascular accidents, heart disease, andosteoporosis could be reduced, thus saving society millions of dol-lars. In concert with health professionals, we as fitness educatorshave the means to reduce and postpone these chronic and oftenexpensive and disabling diseases. And the interventions we provideare relatively inexpensive, providing the client a good return oninvestment. This is not to say people will not still die, but thoseclients who engage in healthy behaviors can compress the timebetween the onset of disability and death.
Welcome to the profession of Senior Fitness. Older adults will
come to you for various reasons and with varied backgrounds.Some of your clients will be very able and fit, while others willbe frail and in need of therapeutic exercise. However, all will beentering ‚Äúsenescence,‚Äù a time of growing out of adulthood andinto old age. Senescence, much like adolescence, prepares theperson for the next stage of life, which in this case is ‚Äúelder-2Senior Fitness

sedentary: Sitting a lot;
not involved in physical
activity that could producesignificant fitness benefits.It Is Never Too Late to Make a Client Feel Great!
# Unit 1 3
Being Sedentary Can Be Hazardous
to Your Health
The following list includes information and facts regarding
physical inactivity.
‚Ä¢ Inactivity and poor diet cause at least 300,000
deaths a year in the United States.
‚Ä¢ Adults who are less active are at greater risk of
dying of heart disease and developing diabetes,
colon cancer, and high blood pressure.
‚Ä¢ More than sixty percent of U.S. adults do not
engage in the recommended amount of activity.
‚Ä¢ Approximately forty percent of U.S. adults are
not active at all.
‚Ä¢ Physical inactivity is more common among older
adults than it is among younger adults.
‚Ä¢ Inactivity increases with age. By age 75, about
one in three men and one in two women engagein no physical activity.
‚Ä¢ Regular physical activity has a strong correlation
to social support from family and friends.
‚Ä¢ People with disabilities are less likely to engage in
regular moderate physical activity, than peoplewith no physical disabilities; yet they have similarneeds to promote health and prevent lifestyle-related disease.
hood.‚Äù Elderhood offers a time for reflection that is most fully
appreciated after a life complete with happiness, sadness,health, and illness.
Some experts define the aging population in two segments‚Äîthe
young/old and the old/old. An important concept to know as atrainer of older adults is that not all older adults are the same.Generally, the arena of the young/old is the Kingdom of the Welland Healthy. These individuals are mobile and for the most parthealthy, even though some might have some health issues. Theold/old inhabit the Kingdom of the Frail and Sick. The old/old haveentered a time of loss‚Äîloss of health, loss of a spouse, loss offriends, loss of independence, loss of function, and many times lossof memory. The ‚Äúone size fits all‚Äù theory will not work with thispopulation. Whether young/old or old/old, however, your clientscan benefit from your compassion and knowledge.
We now know that exercise increases your odds of living longer. The
data clearly shows that people who are active and do have a heartattack have much better survival rates than unfit heart attack vic-tims. A classic study involved a group of Harvard alumni. It fol-lowed 16,000 men, aged thirty-five to seventy-four, for twelve tofifteen years and found that, all else being equal, the more thesemen participated in moderate to vigorous activity, the longer theylived. Those who were active could expect to add one to two yearsto their lives by the time they reached eighty years of age.
Y et another study, this one in the March 14, 2004, New England
Journal of Medicine, reached similar conclusions. This study foundthat men with the greatest capacity for exercise live the longest. Thefindings suggest that exercise capacity is a better predictor oflongevity than any other health marker. Dr. Walter Bortz, a StanfordUniversity professor, agrees with this and takes it a little further,suggesting that leg strength is the best predictor of whether a per-son can live independently.
We all know that physical and mental changes occur as we age.4Senior Fitness
Aging affects all systems of the body; however, the amount of the
effect varies by individual. Dr. Bortz believes that everything he wastaught in medical school about aging was wrong. T oday, medicaldoctors, along with physiologists and gerontologists, are redefiningthe aging process. We know that normal aging, healthy aging, andsickness as seen in older people, are not the same. It wasn‚Äôt too longago that we expected heart disease to occur in older men andwomen; now we are aware that heart disease is the result of anunhealthy lifestyle and is not a factor of aging.
If someone were to ask you to describe the typical, eighty-year-old
person, what would you think of? Hopefully, you would describe thisperson as vibrant and active. Unfortunately, many people woulddescribe an eighty-year-old person as frail, possibly using a walker,and most likely, sedentary. However, aging does not have to be syn-onymous with disability and infirmity. For decades we‚Äôve seen shriv-eled and non-energetic individuals among the older segment of the

population and have come to think
of this as the norm in our society.Studies like those previously men-tioned suggest otherwise, however,as do recent studies of older athletesin which researchers are findingthat these individuals don‚Äôt slowdown like their sedentary counter-parts. Their muscle mass doesn‚Äôtdecrease as much, and when suchdeterioration does occur, it‚Äôs not theresult of old age but, again, of dis-
use. According to a study conducted by Stanford University, long-time runners live longer and have less pain, disability, and diseasethan their sedentary peers. The runners in the study were leaner, hadfewer joint problems, and needed less medication than non-runners.
What we‚Äôre learning is that our clients need to be proactive partici-
pants in the aging process rather than passive observers. Many ofthe negative changes observed in aging were detected in the bed reststudies of the 1970‚Äôs. The changes noticed in the bed rest studies aresimilar to the changes seen in people who have a sedentary lifestyle.Some experts on aging believe that total bed rest for twenty-onedays is equivalent physically to thirty years of aging. Other scientistssuggest that one hour of exercise increases life by one hour. Fewpeople die strictly from old age. Rather, they die from the repercus-sions of diseases such as cancer and heart disease, so often found inolder, unhealthy people.
The expectation of a slow, steady decline with progressing years
does not hold true for those individuals who show interest in fitnessand a healthy lifestyle. We know that a very fit person of seventy,someone on a regular exercise program, can be compared to a mod-erately fit person of thirty who exercises only occasionally. Asremarkable as that may seem, more and more evidence supports thefact that chronological age has very little to do with aging and is6Senior Fitness

nothing to worry about. The stigma of slow decline associated with
aging need no longer be an issue for those committed to fitness andhealth. Ask yourself how old ‚Äúold‚Äù would be if you didn‚Äôt know howold ‚Äúold‚Äù was. Research suggests that there is a significant differencebetween chronological age and physiological age.
Unfortunately, many older adults believe it is too late to start exer-
cising and that their past abilities and present disabilities are pre-dictors of how fit or healthy they can become. This is false! Nomatter how well one maintains oneself, the physiological processwill eventually take its toll. However, people need to take to heartthe idea that proper exercise is an important ingredient in maxi-mizing physiological capacity. Regular daily activity has been a wayof life for virtually every person who has reached the age of onehundred in sound condition. Exercise training does not stop thebiological clock, but at the present time, it is the safest means avail-able of slowing it. Alex Lief, M.D., a Harvard Medical School pro-fessor of gerontology, has said that exercise is the closest thing we
It Is Never Too Late to Make a Client Feel Great!
# Unit 1 7

have to an ‚Äúanti-aging pill.‚Äù If a person wishes to age successfully,
they must commit to engaging in a program of systematic exercise.
Science supports the idea that exercise can slow down the aging
process, and researchers continue to be persuaded that fitness forolder adults is a must:
‚Ä¢ Dr. Herbert DeVries is convinced that men and
women in their seventies and eighties can achieve
levels of vigor usually associated with people thirtyyears younger.
‚Ä¢ Dr. Steven Blair, of the Institute for Aerobic
Research in Dallas, found that as much as one andone-half of all functional decline typically associat-ed with aging is the result of disease and can bereversed with proper exercise.
‚Ä¢ Dr. Walter Bortz, author and professor, said, ‚ÄúOur
aging is in our own hands. It is no one else‚Äôsresponsibility. If we depend on our doctors, ourfamilies, our government, we risk compromisingthe quality of our lives.‚Äù If we take charge of ourlives we can ensure that our future years are cre-ative and radiantly alive.
‚Ä¢ Dr. Spirduso believes that older adults who stay
engaged physically, socially, and mentally age bet-ter than those who do not.
‚Ä¢ Dr. Fries, of Stanford University, believes that older
people can age well with grace and wisdom, witand experience, energy and vitality.
The bottom line is that it is never too late to feel great!8Senior Fitness

# Unit 1 9
# Unit Summary
I. Individuals are living longer than ever before.
a. A longer lifespan makes the need to maintain
health well into old age significantly more impor-
tant in terms of both quality of life for the individ-ual and the economic interests of the individualand the larger community.
b. Fitness professionals carry the message of healthier
aging and prolonged independence to older adults.
II. While aging affects all systems of the body, healthy
aging differs significantly from ‚Äúnormal‚Äù aging.
a. Traditionally, aging has been synonymous with dis-
ability and infirmity.
b. New research shows that fit individuals defy the
stereotypes commonly associated with aging.

‚ÄúWe are under-exercised as a nation. We look instead of play. We
ride instead of walk. Our existence deprives us of the minimum of
physical activity essential for a healthy living. ‚Äù
‚ÄîJohn F. Kennedy
If we want our clients to age well, not only do we need to have
them be physically active, but we also need to educate them aboutthe most current information about wellness. Dr. C. Everett Koop,
former Surgeon General of the United States, said it well: ‚Äúno pre-
scription is more valuable than knowledge.‚Äù The purpose of thiscourse is to connect the knowledge known about healthy aging tothe practitioner in the field. We here at ISSA believe that, regard-less of age or disability, clients can be better tomorrow than theyare today if they take the first step to fitness by engaging in thehealthy practices described within this course.
# Age-Related Changes
Aging is inevitable and extremely desirable when compared to suchalternatives as death or being feeble and frail. As we have alreadydiscussed, Americans are growing older and living longer. In 1880,only 3 percent of the U.S. population was over sixty-five years old.In 1980, 11 percent of the population was sixty-five and older. Bythe year 2050, 20 percent of the population will be over sixty-fiveyears of age. Based on recent findings, a growing number ofresearchers believe that with healthy lifestyles we may be able to setback the biological clock or at least slow it by as much as forty years.Many experts believe that humans have the potential to liveuntil
one hundred ten or one hundred twenty years of age.
‚ÄúWalking is man‚Äôs best medicine. ‚Äù ‚ÄîHippocrates
Because the aging process is very individual, the speed and extent ofaging varies from person to person. Many of the characteristics weassociate with aging, such as osteoporosis, poor posture, and theshuffling gait associated with weak muscles, are effects of ‚Äúnormal‚Äùaging. Fortunately, the effects of healthy aging are very different.Many of the negative effects of aging are brought on in part by phys-ical inactivity and can be reversed with regular physical activity. Wemust dismiss the stereotype that senior citizens are frail, elderly peo-ple who are incapable of health and vitality. We must replace thesenegative images of sick old people with positive images of healthy,active older adults. We must challenge ourselves to replace the label12Senior Fitness
‚Äúsenior citizen‚Äù with other, more positive terms such as ‚Äúchronolog-
ically advanced.‚Äù Sadly, many clients allow their age to exclude themfrom becoming the healthiest and most fit people they can be, butage is not a barrier to wellness or fitness. While there is currently nomagic formula to be invented to reverse the aging process, currentresearch suggests that proper exercise may in fact extend one‚Äôs lifespan and that, without question, it will improve the quality of one‚Äôslife. Exercise can make your client walk taller, feel better, and livelonger. Physical fitness is important for everyone!
T echnically, we begin to age from the moment of conception. As the
body approaches the middle years, if it is not well cared for, theeffects of ‚Äúnormal aging‚Äù begin to become quite obvious. Variousorgans and systems begin to display deterioration. Some of theeffects of this show up as heart disease, arthritis, elevated bloodpressure, and low back problems. Additionally, hormone produc-tion decreases from both the ovaries and the glands, immune func-tion declines, and elasticity diminishes in muscles, skin, andarteries. Older adults must begin to accept the fact that the phrase‚Äúuse it or lose it‚Äù has real meaning if they are to maintain physicaland emotional independence. Many older adults fear losing theirindependence more than dying. Proper exercise can help older
Making Your Client Fit For Life
# Unit 2 13

adults prolong independence, as well as slow down the progression
of many common chronic conditions. It has been shown that lackof regular exercise is one of the factors that significantly increasesthe probability of hospitalization in people over age sixty-five.
While aging is inevitable, being
feeble and frail is not. Very often,unsuccessful aging is the result ofabuse, disuse, and misuse of ourbodies. This is much like the oldgrandfather clock in the livingroom that does not work. It does-n‚Äôt work because the springs haveeither lost their ability to recoil orbecause they are over-worn. Ormaybe somebody forgot to windit up. Which analogy best repre-sents your client‚Äôs body?
We are all aware that aging results in changes in each body system.
These changes may contribute to a variety of health problems expe-rienced by the older individual. However, many of the major dis-abilities associated with aging, such as loss of cardiovascular fitness,decrease of muscle mass, reduced bone mass, glucose intolerance,diminished immune function, and intellectual function loss, can bereversed or prevented by becoming involved in a regular and pru-dent comprehensive exercise program. While this may sound like afairly sweeping statement, more and more scientific studies supportthis conclusion.
EXERCISE, THE ANTI-AGING SOLUTION
While aging is a normal, human phenomenon, unfortunately, it issurrounded by myths that many people accept as fact. The conceptof old age carries with it biases that are deeply ingrained in our soci-14Senior Fitness

ety. Many people believe that health and vitality are synonymous
with youth and that sickness and frailty are associated with aging.Cultural myths may make our clients fearful and hopeless. If theybelieve these myths, it may diminish their motivation and thus theirzest for life!
There are places in the world, such as the Russian province of
Georgia and the state of Florida in the United States, where menand women live to one hundred years and beyond, while experi-encing health, vitality, and vigorous activity. It is projected that bythe year 2050 there will be approximately one million Americansover the age of one hundred. The fastest growing segment of thepopulation is the eighty-five plus group.
The following are some commonly held beliefs that are associated
with aging. How many of the following assertions do you thinkpeople believe?
1. Older people are frail and sickly.
2. Aging is synonymous with senility.3. Older people are less productive than younger people.4. Older people are resistant to change.5. Aging is a uniform process with similar outcomes for
all people.
Thankfully, these things are all false! Y et it is a sad fact of life that
most people in America ‚Äúrust out‚Äù before they ‚Äúwear out.‚Äù Y our job
as a trainer of older adults is to be an agent of change. Active peo-ple age at a rate of only 1/2 percent a year, as opposed to inactivepeople who age at a rate of 2 percent a year. While that does notsound like much, in reality, it takes an active person four years toage the same amount as an inactive person does in one year!Activity really is the anti-aging solution.
Making Your Client Fit For Life
# Unit 2 15
# Ageism
If you or your client believe the myths described earlier to be true,
then it will be difficult to motivate change. Many trainers are tryingto sell youth when in reality they should be selling wellness andfunctional fitness. We must dismiss the stereotype that senior citi-zens are frail, elderly people who are incapable of health and vitali-ty and replace our negative images of sick old people with positiveimages of healthy, active older adults. At the same time, we shouldnot try to make our clients feel as if they are ‚Äúbad‚Äù if they don‚Äôt look,act, or feel like thirty-year-olds. We should encourage our clients tofeel comfortable in their skin and not allow others, especially adver-tisers, to pressure them into feeling badly about their appearance.
Because aging is an individual thing and no one ages in exactly the
same way, the search for universal characteristics of aging may haveto be abandoned. T o prove this to yourself, think about going to ahigh school reunion. Some folks look much older than others with-in the same class. Biologists may have to be satisfied with justdescribing the characteristics of aging, some of which will be expe-rienced by most individuals at some point in their lives and some ofwhich may never be experienced by some people.16Senior Fitness

Aging is influenced by our environment, genetics, and lifestyle, and
its speed and extent vary from person to person.
According to gerontologist John Rowe, M.D., ‚ÄúThe impact of
chronological aging has been overemphasized and a substantialportion of the physical changes we consider natural to aging areactually due to lack of exercise, poor diet, and bad habits such assmoking and excessive drinking.‚Äù Based on the findings to which wehave already referred, a growing number of researchers believe thatwith healthy lifestyles we may be able to set back the biologicalclock or at least slow it by as much as forty years.
# Causes Of Premature Aging
Our age and health are influenced by how we live but, more impor-tantly, by how we have lived. The leading authorities on aging main-tain that what makes us age prematurely is misuse, disuse, andabuse of our bodies and not just the passage of time. More andmore research suggests that people can beat the aging game by stay-ing active or becoming active and by adopting healthy habits. In1984, Dr. Bortz did groundbreaking research when he compiled areview paper titled ‚ÄúDisuse and Aging‚Äù for the Journal of theAmerican Medical Association . He found that if you listed all the
changes in the human body that are ascribed to aging (changes inmuscles, bones, brain, cholesterol, blood pressure, sleep habits, sex-ual performance, and psychological inventory) and then comparedthe list to changes that occur in the human body due to physicalinactivity, there would be a striking similarity between the two lists.The near duplication of these lists shows that many of the bodilychanges we have always ascribed to the normal aging process are infact caused by disuse of the body.
Aging is an inevitable part of life for all species. Differences in human
rates of aging are based on many factors. We all know individuals intheir sixties who are invalids and others who are in their eighties whoare healthy, active, and alert. Among the factors which will influence

# Becoming Disabled

and more disabled. However, U.S. Surgeon Generals have estimatedthat close to 85 percent of our most dreaded diseases could be pre-vented with appropriate lifestyle changes. Exercise will allow peopleto not only survive, but to thrive! Age is no excuse for infirmity!

The Top Ten Benefits of Exercise
for Older Adults
1. Maintenance of a high level of physical and social
activity increases the quality of life, enhancing social
satisfaction.
2. Increased independence is enjoyed when fitness and
health are maintained. Most Americans fear infirmityand dependence more than death.
3. A person has more energy and can perform daily rou-
tines with greater ease.
4. Increased muscle tone and flexibility improve balance,
decreasing falls.
5. The more muscle tissue a person maintains, the high-
er his metabolism, making it easier to control weight.
6. Calories burned through exercise allow a person to
rake in more nutrients.
7. Exercise delays loss of bone mass.
8. Posture improves, decreasing backache and enhanc-
ing appearance.
9. Cardio-respiratory function is enhanced, improving
peripheral circulation and decreasing risk of athero-
sclerosis, high blood pressure, and other circulatoryproblems.
10. Using it keeps you from losing it!22Senior Fitness
The bottom line is that physical fitness is the key to both mental
and physical health for the older adult. If a person wishes to agesuccessfully, they must commit to engaging in a program of sys-tematic exercise.
# Unit Summary
I. Aging is inevitable, but various factors affect how each
individual ages.
II. Exercise has been proven to increase length and quali-
ty of life.
III. A positive mental attitude is key to healthy aging.
a. Fitness professionals must arm their older clients
with current information about wellness.
b. Both trainer and client must embrace the truth that
health is distinct from youth.
IV. Premature aging is as much the result of disuse and
abuse of the body as it is the passage of time.
a. All of the changes commonly associated with aging
are also associated with physical inactivity.
b. While aging is inevitable, as much as 70 percent of
the way an individual ages is connected to lifestyle
and is thus in his or her own hands.
V. Our bodies are programmed for longevity, and by
remaining or becoming active, we can improve boththe quality and quantity of years we have left.The purpose of exercise is not to make senior athletes out of all
older adults or even to extend their life span, but rather to offerolder adults the opportunity and capability to be able to do what-ever they wish to do. Whether they want to have adequate strengthto get out of a chair and go for a walk, or to play catch with theirgrandchildren, or even to go on a vacation that they always wantedto take, exercise can help them do it. (I have an older friend at thegym who told me the only reason she exercises is so that she is fitenough to take advantage of the over-seventy-five-years-of-age skifree program!)
The key is to design a fitness program that matches the client‚Äôs goals
not the trainer‚Äôs goals.

‚ÄúNORMAL‚Äù EFFECTS OF AGING
As mentioned before, many of the characteristics we associate with
aging, such as brittle bones, stooped posture, a shuffling gait, andweak muscles, are the effects of ‚Äútypical‚Äù aging, not healthy aging.Fortunately, the effects of healthy aging are very different. Later inthis manual, we will see the positive influence exercise has on manyof these effects. For now, though, let‚Äôs review some of the signs, orbiomarkers , commonly associated with aging.
‚Ä¢ Metabolism slows down as we age. (Calorie
requirements drop by 2 to 10 percent for each
decade over the age of thirty.)biomarker(s):  a sign or
symptom associated with aprocess or phenomenon, asin the process of aging.
metabolism: The total of
all the chemical and physi-
cal processes by which thebody builds and maintainsitself (anabolism) and bywhich it breaks down itssubstances for the produc-tion of energy (catabolism).
osteoporosis: Abnormal
loss of bony tissue resulting
in fragile porous bonesattributable to a lack of cal-cium; most common inpostmenopausal women.‚Ä¢ Muscle mass decreases.
‚Ä¢ Muscular strength is reduced.‚Ä¢ Body fat levels increase, likely due to decreased
activity.
‚Ä¢ There is decreased bone mass and increased bone
porosity, degeneration of the vertebral discs, and
osteoporosis .
‚Ä¢ The joints stiffen and the bone structure becomes
less firm, which often results in a loss of height and
a stooped posture. Breathing, urination, and defe-cation can be impaired by these changes.
‚Ä¢ Cardiac output (the amount of blood pumped with
each beat of the heart) declines, decreasing theefficiency of the heart as a pump.
‚Ä¢ The heart muscle loses strength. Simultaneously,
the hardening and shrinking of the arteries (arte-riosclerosis) make it more difficult for blood to flowfreely throughout the body. The body may com-
pensate with an increase in the
systolic blood
pressure .The increased workload placed on the
heart and circulatory system can lead to strokes
and heart attacks.
‚Ä¢ Blood cholesterol levels may increase.
‚Ä¢ There is an increased tendency towards hyperten-
sion in some individuals as a result of increased
peripheral vascular resistance.
‚Ä¢ Aerobic capacity (VO 2max) decreases.
‚Ä¢ The exchange of oxygen and carbon dioxide in the
lungs may become more difficult as the respiratorysystem of the older person becomes less efficient.This loss of efficiency may be a result of a weaken-ing of the muscles and changes in the joints of theribs and chest. The fibers of the lungs may losetheir elasticity, and the blood vessels may harden.The result is that it becomes more difficult for theolder person to breathe.26Senior Fitness
systolic blood pressure:Blood pressure during thecontraction of the heartmuscle.
Aerobic capacity (VO 2
max): The highest rate of
oxygen consumption of
which a person is capable.Also referred to as maximaloxygen uptake.
cataracts: An eye disease
that involves the clouding
or opacification of the nat-ural lens of the eye. ‚Ä¢ The gastrointestinal system can change leading to
different dietary demands, and the frequency ofurination increases.
‚Ä¢ Kidney filtration declines. The kidney filtration rate
of a seventy-five-year-old is 60 percent of the rateof that of a thirty-year-old.
‚Ä¢ There is degeneration of the nerve cell body as
reflected by a decreased number of cells and achange in cell size‚Äîthis is generally associated withdecreased nerve conduction velocity, resulting inslower reaction and movement times.
‚Ä¢ The nervous system is altered in part because of a
loss in the bulk of the brain. Brain weight in a sev-enty-five-year-old is about 92 percent of that of athirty-year-old.
‚Ä¢ Reflexes and reaction time are slowed.
‚Ä¢ Visual acuity diminishes. Less light reaches the reti-
na in an aging eye, and the lens often acquires a
yellow cast.
Cataracts are often found.
‚Ä¢ Taste and smell become less sensitive.
‚Ä¢ The skin changes in appearance, becoming darker,
more pigmented, and more vulnerable to bruising
and skin sores. Skin also loses elasticity.
‚Ä¢ The sensations of touch and pain are altered.
‚Ä¢ The net effect is often that the individual feels less
capable of mastering his or her environment and
becomes increasingly depressed and isolated.
Regular and prudent exercise can halt and maybe even reverse some
of these common characteristics of typical aging.
Biomarkers of Aging
# Unit 3 27
# Theories On Aging
Two major groups of theories are being advanced by scientists to
further our understanding of aging. One group of theories holdsthat aging follows a biological timetable, perhaps a continuation ofthe one that regulates childhood growth and development. Theother major group of theories emphasizes environmental assaultsto our systems that gradually cause things to go wrong. Becausethese theories of aging are not mutually exclusive, it seems unlikelythat one theory will ever be able to describe the process of aging inits entirety. A brief and very simplified summary of some of themajor theories being proposed follows.
LIFESPAN/METABOLIC TIME SPAN THEORY
Numerous theories exist about the causes of aging. Dr. Schneiderat the USC Andrus Gerontology Center states, ‚Äúif you think cancer
is complex, look at aging.‚Äù T oday,most experts agree that the agingprocess arises from a multitude ofparallel and interconnecting factors.One of these factors may be a theo-retical metabolic time limit. Thepremise is that the metabolic timelimit is preset and is passed from onegeneration to the next. For yearswe‚Äôve known that certain animalshave fairly predictable life spans.Elephants usually live approximatelyseventy years, horses about forty
years, and mice survive about two to three years. The human lifespan is not as predictable.
Human mortality is characterized according to three phases of the
life span. In the first phase, the longer you live the longer you areexpected to live. After the first few months and years of life, deathrates drop drastically. For example, the longer a newborn survives,the better are his or her chances for a normal life span. During the28Senior Fitness

second phase, between the ages of ten and forty, the death rate is
generally quite low. During stage three, the range between age fortyand one hundred, the individual has already lived at least severalyears longer than expected and the mortality rate climbs rapidly. Theaverage life expectancy of infants born in 1930 was sixty-two yearsfor girls and fifty-eight years for boys. T oday, those same females andmales, now in their mid-sixties, can anticipate living until ageseighty-four and eighty, respectively.
Some of that increased longevity is due simply to statistics: as you
get older, your life expectancy automatically expands, since the pos-sibility of dying young, which drags the average down, no longerapplies. I have a friend who understands this quite well. While at hisone hundred third birthday party, I asked if he thought he‚Äôd makeit to one hundred four. He said statistics show very few people diebetween one hundred three and one hundred four!
Lifespan or metabolic time span theory is only one of many in this
group. Some examples of theories in this group follow.
Biomarkers of Aging
# Unit 3 29

endocrine system:
System consisting of theglands and tissues thatrelease hormones. It workswith the nervous system in regulating metabolicactivities.
immune system:
Complex and integratedbody system of organs, tissues, cells, and cell prod-ucts such as antibodies that differentiates self fromnon-self and protects theorganism from potentiallypathogenic organisms orsubstances.Programmed and Metabolic Theory Examples
Programmed Senescence : Aging is the result of the sequential
switching on and off of certain genes, with senescence being
defined as the time when age-associated deficits are manifested.
Endocrine Theory: Biological clocks act through hormones to con-
trol the pace of aging.
Immunological Theory: A programmed decline in immune system
functions leads to an increased vulnerability to infectious diseaseresulting in greater age-related characteristics that influence death.
# Extrinsic And Error Theories
Many things we associate with aging, such as fragile bones, forget-fulness, and loss of strength, are brought about partially due toextrinsic factors like mental and/or physical inactivity. In otherwords each of us ages at our own pace. Many of the changes that weexpect with the passage of timecan be positively influenced withthe inclusion of sensible and regu-lar exercise. Failure to use ourmuscles and other bodily systemsregularly can result in prematureaging, either from disuse or fromdevelopment of disease.
In 1961, scientists at the Vistar
Institute of Anatomy and Biologyfound that certain human cellscalled fibroblasts have a finite
capacity to duplicate or divide. When cells from older adults werecompared to those of younger adults, whose do you think had thegreater capacity to divide? Y es‚Äîthe younger person‚Äôs. Anotherinteresting fact scientists discovered was that people with ‚ÄúWerner‚ÄôsSyndrome‚Äù (a disease that results in premature aging and ultimate-ly death) show a reduced capability to replicate their cells when30Senior Fitness
fibroblasts: Cells within
the structure of our con-
nective tissue that moveinto adjacent tissues to help repair damage andform scar tissue.
compared to others of the same age without the disease. Examples
of theories in the extrinsic group are presented below.
Error Theory Examples
W ear and T ear: Cells and tissues have vital parts that wear out.
Rate of Living : The greater the rate of oxygen basal metabolism, the
shorter the life span.Cross-linking: The accumulation of cross-linked proteins damages
cells and tissues, thereby slowing down bodily processes.
Free Radicals : Accumulated damage caused by oxygen radicals
causes cells and eventually organs to stop functioning.Error Catastrophe: Damage to mechanisms that synthesize protein
results in faulty proteins, which accumulate to a level that causescatastrophic damage to cells, tissues, and organs.
Somatic Mutation : Genetic mutations occur and accumulate with
increasing age, causing cells to deteriorate and malfunction.Aging and ways to circumvent aging have been fascinating topics
since the beginning of time. Even the most respected scientists andgerontologists don‚Äôt fully understand the mechanisms of aging.
In 2003, Dr. James Fries, a Stanford University physician, told the
U.S. Senate Committee of Appropriation that older adults couldimprove their quality of life and delay disabling conditions and ill-ness if they would adopt a healthy lifestyle, thus compressing theirperiod of morbidity. In support of his claim, researchers at theCooper Clinic found that regular exercise may cut hospital staysand doctor visits for serious or minor illness by almost 50 percent.We now know that regular exercise is not only the fountain ofyouth, it is also the foundation for successful aging. Proper healthstyle behaviors and sensible exercise would not only prevent diseaseand untimely death, but would improve the quality of life.

MEN‚ÄôS AND WOMEN‚ÄôS HEALTH ISSUES
Though life expectancy has increased for both men and women,
there continues to be a difference in longevity. Let‚Äôs examine someof the issues affecting this trend.
MEN‚ÄôS HEALTH ISSUES
Statistics reveal that, on the whole, women outlive men. Considerthe following:
‚Ä¢ For every 100 women that die between the ages of
45 and 65, 170 men die!
‚Ä¢ Men are 88% more likely to die of heart disease at
a younger age than women.
‚Ä¢ Men are 45% more likely to die from cancer than
women.
More and more research shows that exercise helps lower the risk ofmajor chronic disease and premature death. Researchers at theCooper Institute for Aerobics Research in Dallas studied nearly10,000 men (ages twenty to eighty-two). Among that group 3,000had a history of heart disease, stroke, cancer, high blood pressure,or diabetes. The least fit men in each group were classified as‚Äúunfit.‚Äù When researchers compared the fit group with the unfitgroup, the unfit group had a higher rate of death.32Senior Fitness

Thosemen who got in shape actually cut their mortality rate as signifi-cantly as smokers who gave up smoking cigarettes.
WOMEN‚ÄôS HEALTH ISSUES

Heart disease is currently the number one killer of American
women 65 years of age and older; breast cancer is number two.Presently, there is a large study underway, funded by the NationalInstitute of Health, at Stanford University and forty other centersnationwide. This study is looking at the health habits of womenwho are fifty years of age. About 628 million dollars are being spent,and most of the major health issues affecting women hopefully willbe known. # Unit 3 35
# Unit Summary
I. ‚ÄúNormal‚Äù aging includes such symptoms of physical
decline as decreases in muscle mass, cardiac output,
and aerobic capacity; an increase in blood cholesterollevels; degeneration of the nerve cells; an increase inbody fat; changes in the skin; and stiffening of thejoints and weakening of the bone structure.
II. Two major groups of theories on aging are being
advanced by scientists to help us understand the agingprocess.
a. Life Span/Metabolic Theory focuses on the idea of a
theoretical metabolic time limit. Examples of theo-
ries in this category are Programmed Senescence,Endocrine Theory, and Immunological Theory.
b. Extrinsic and Error Theory assumes that many of
the changes we associate with aging are broughton by extrinsic factors like mental and physicalinactivity. Examples of theories in this categoryfocus on such issues as Wear and Tear, Rate ofLiving, Cross-linking, Free Radicals, ErrorCatastrophe, and Somatic Mutation.
III. Men and women have historically had and continue to
have a difference in life expectancy. Statistics showthat women typically outlive men.
a. Men are 88% more likely to die of heart disease
than women and 45 percent more likely to die
from cancer than women.
b. Much of the early research on aging was directed
at men, but this is changing. Newer studies revealthat the number one killer of women is heart dis-ease, while breast cancer is number two.

‚Ä¢ Describe the common changes that occur with aging
in the various organs and systems of the bodyVI. Muscle

IX. Personality
The only thing that is absolutely known about aging is that every-
body does it.
Individuals age at widely varying rates. In fact, within the same per-
son, organs and organ systems show different rates of decline.However, some generalizations can be made based on data from theBaltimore Longitudinal Study of Aging. It is important to remem-ber, however, that these conditions will not apply to all people. Thefollowing section is designed to give you an idea of some of themost common changes that can occur with age in various organsand systems.
# Lungs
Vital capacity (VC) is the maximum amount of air that can bemoved out of the lungs during a single breath, following a maximalinspiration. The elasticity of the lungs directly affects vital capacity,vital capacity (VC):
Maximal breathing capaci-ty; the amount of air thatcan be expired after a max-imum inspiration; the maxi-mum total volume of thelungs, less the residual vol-ume. The usable portion ofthe lungs.
and age and inactivity affect elasticity. Vital capacity may decline by
about 40 percent between the ages of twenty and seventy.
Maximum oxygen uptake is also affected by age and is often used as
a measure of total body fitness. Maximum oxygen uptake, or VO 2
max, is the greatest amount of oxygen that can be utilized by theentire body (at a cellular level). However, this measure of oxygenutilization does not depend only on the lungs, but also on the heartand circulatory system.
Physical conditioning determines how well our bodies transport
oxygen. Age and inactivity can decrease the ability to transport andutilize oxygen effectively. Maximal oxygen consumption duringexercise declines in men by about 10 percent with each decade ofadult life; in women VO 2drops by about 7.5 percent per decade.
Using various treatments and medicines, a physician can improvean individual‚Äôs ability to transport oxygen and increase blood oxy-gen content by 5 to 10 percent, but that is only a fraction of whatcan be accomplished through regular exercise and increased phys-ical fitness. 38Senior Fitness

Inability to control the flowof urine and involuntaryurination.HEART
There is a decrease in elasticity and an
increase in stiffness of the arteries as weage, which contributes to an increase inblood pressure. The heart muscleweakens and cardiac output (the prod-uct of stroke volume and heart rate)
decreases. Exercise can reverse or lessenthis decline.
# Brain
With age, the brain loses some cells (neu-rons), and others become damaged.However, the brain adapts by increasingthe number of connections (synapses)between cells and by re-growing thebranch-like extensions (dendrites and
axons) that carry messages. Physical exer-
cise has been shown to improve motor skills and cognitive skills.New research also suggests that keeping the blood pressure withinthe ideal range may prevent dementia and Alzheimer‚Äôs.
# Kidneys
As a person ages, the kidneys graduallybecome less efficient at extractingwastes from the blood. This decrease inefficiency is partially attributed to thegeneral decrease in size of the kidneysthat occurs with age, and also thedecrease in blood or plasma flow to thekidneys. Bladder capacity declines andtissues atrophy, sometimes resulting inurinary incontinence, which can oftenbe managed with exercise and behav-ioral techniques.

# Unit 4 39

lean muscle mass: The
amount of muscular tissue
that the person has in rela-tion to total body mass.
sarcopenia: The degener-
ative loss of muscle mass
related to aging.
Type II fibers: The fast
twitch muscle fibers of the
body that are responsiblefor high levels of strength.BODY FAT
Body fat increases with age, commonly from a combination of
acquiring more storage fat from sedentary living and redistributionof existing stored fats. Women are more likely to store it in the lowerbody‚Äîhips and thighs. Men will tend to carry their stored fat in theabdominal area. An unhealthy change in body composition hasmany health risks associated with it, but these risks can be reducedwith physical activity.
# Muscle
Lean muscle mass is the amount of muscular tissue that the person
has in relation to total body mass. Sarcopenia is the term used to
describe the degenerative loss of muscle mass related to aging. Theaverage decline in muscle mass between the ages of thirty and sev-enty has been measured at 22 percent for women and 23 percent formen. Type II fibers, the muscle fibers responsible for high levels of
strength, have been shown in some studies to decrease as much as50 percent in men by the age of eighty. Strength training can pre-vent loss of muscle mass and help in regaining the lean mass anindividual may have already lost. 40Senior Fitness

# Sight
Difficulty focusing close up may begin
around age forty; the ability to distinguishfine details may begin to decline in one‚Äôsseventies. From age fifty on, there isincreased susceptibility to glare, greaterdifficulty in seeing at low levels of illumi-nation, and more difficulty in detecting
moving targets. Less than 15 percent of the population over eightyyears of age has 20/20 vision.
# Hearing
It becomes more difficult to hear higherfrequencies with age, with loss of hearingactually beginning as early as age twenty.Hearing declines more quickly in menthan in women. It is estimated that asmany as 65 percent of people reaching ageeighty have some significant hearing loss.
# Personality
After about age thirty, personalityis stable. Sudden changes in per-sonality sometimes suggest diseaseprocesses. Senility, dementia, andorganic brain syndromes are allconditions that affect the brainwith age and consequently canaffect the personality of the personafflicted. Alzheimer‚Äôs, a progres-sive type of dementia that impairsmemory, judgment, and personal-
ity, accounts for half of all reported cases of dementia. Many olderadults suffer from depression, due to the losses that often occur.Some studies have found that mild-to-moderate exercise decreases
Physiology of Aging
# Unit 4 41

the depression. Often a positive side effect of exercise is the social
aspects of it, in addition to the improvement in self-esteem.
The bottom line is that almost everything that gets worst with age
can be positively influenced with a regular dose of mild-to-moder-ate physical activity. 42Senior Fitness

# Unit Summary
I. Vital capacity and maximal oxygen uptake are both
affected by aging, often resulting in a decreased ability
to transport oxygen through the body.
II. Cardiac output has been shown to decline with age.
III. The aging brain loses some cells, and others become
damaged; however, adaptations offset some of theeffects of these losses.
IV. The kidneys become less efficient at extracting waste
from the body with age, and bladder capacity declinesand tissues atrophy.
V. Body fat increases with age.
VI. Muscle mass declines with age.
VII. Difficulty with focusing close up and distinguishing
fine details affect vision as one ages.
VIII. Hearing at high frequencies becomes more difficult
with age.
IX. Sudden changes in personality with age are sometimes
brought on by disease.

While no magic pill exists to stop aging or prevent disease and dis-
ability, exercise is an intervention that is inexpensive, has no sideeffects, and if taken regularly can prevent many ailments seen inolder people. It can even slow down the aging process.  Scientists aresuggesting that we need not have a steady decline starting in ourthirties but that we can live successfully until our eighties before ourbody systems start to slow down and break down.
hypokinetic disease:
A disease related to, orcaused by, insufficient activity and lack of regularexercise.In addition to having good
genes, eating a sound diet,and getting proper medicalcare, regular physical activityis essential to successfulaging. Some experts evensuggest that regular physicalactivity is more importantthan diet‚Äîpossibly moreimportant than healthy cho-lesterol levels‚Äîand thatbeing fit and overweight isbetter than not being fit andbeing of normal weight. Astudy conducted jointly bythe American College ofSports Medicine and theCenter for Disease Control
found that 12 percent of deaths in Americans are indirectly relatedto the lack of exercise or what we call hypokinetic disease . This
might be better known as ‚Äúcouch potato syndrome.‚Äù With all of theattention exercise is receiving, it is depressing that only one in fourAmericans gets enough of it.
Some people discount exercise because they believe longevity is all
in the genes. It is true that living a long and healthy life is depend-ent upon many factors including genetics. Genetic research hasshown that one‚Äôs genetic make-up plays a key part in overall healthand longevity. Studies of identical and non-identical twins showthat life spans of identical twins are remarkably similar (identicaltwins have the same genes). Identical twins died within two to fouryears of each other, whereas non-identical twins died an average ofseven years apart. Experts agree, however, that only 30% of longevi-ty is in the genes. That leaves 70% that is determined by healthhabits and environment.46Senior Fitness

The Journal of the American Medical Association stated that even a
minimal amount of exercise, such as a thirty-minute brisk walk two
times a week, is enough to protect the body against cardiovasculardisease and cancer. The research also indicates that people, whoexercise or are active, even in short duration, tend to live longer andbetter than those who do not exercise at all.
Preventive gerontology starts in childhood with well baby care,
good nutrition, regular medical check-ups, and proper exercise.However, to maintain good health, older adults need all of thesethings, plus social connectedness and functional exercise.
The motto we should teach our clients is: what you do today will
determine how you age tomorrow. The results of misusing the bodyare cumulative and many times take years to manifest themselves.
Hippocrates was well ahead of his time when he said, ‚ÄúSpeaking gen-
erally, all parts of the body which have function, if used in moderation
and exercised in labours to which each is accustomed, become therebyhealthy and well developed, and age slowly, but if unused and left idle,they become liable to disease, defective in growth, and age quickly.‚Äù
Unfortunately, it only took the medical profession and fitness com-
munity 2000 years to prove this.
Aging affects all the systems of the body. We now know that people
do not grow too old to exercise, but rather grow old from lack ofexercise. As we said earlier, exercise physiologists have proven atrained and fit seventy-year-old year old person is equivalent intheir oxygen carrying capacity to an untrained, unfit, sedentaryforty-year-old person. We also understand that various medicalinterventions can improve the body‚Äôs ability to transport oxygen byabout 5 to 10 percent, but that is only a fraction of what can beaccomplished through exercise and improved cardiopulmonary fit-ness. The specific benefits of exercise on various systems of thebody will be presented in this unit.

# Unit 5 47
collateral circulation: An
area of tissue or an organ
has a number of differentpathways for blood toreach it. This is often as aresult of anastamoses -branches formed betweenadjacent blood vessels.CARDIOVASCULAR SYSTEM
The health and efficiency of our cardiorespiratory system can limit
our functional abilities. Structural and anatomical changes takeplace that affect our ability to exercise. Some unfit older adultsexperience shortness of breath or fatigue easily when participatingin simple activities. Specifically, with normal aging and in theabsence of disease, we see a decrease in both the size of the heartand the ability of the heart to efficiently pump blood. Since heartvalves become more rigid and thick with age, the phrase ‚Äúhard-hearted old guy‚Äù is in fact accurate.
# Vascular Adaptations To Training
Within the last twenty years, studies have explained the linkbetween cardiovascular health and regular aerobic exercise. Inactivepeople are one and one-half to two times more likely to have a heartattack, and the chances of dying immediately after that heart attackare three times greater than for physically active people. Said anoth-er way, active people are less likely to have a heart attack, and if theydo, they are more likely to survive due to collateral circulation.
Collateral circulation occurs when auxiliary vessels increase circu-
lation for coronary arteries that have become narrowed or clogged,
like when the freeway is blocked and you take the back roads to getto your destination. Collateral circulation is the route the bloodtakes to keep your heart alive until the medical staff can unblock theroad. Exercise increases the development of collateral vessels.
# Blood Pressure
As we age, our blood pressure increases. The belief is that the arterywalls lose their elasticity with age and become hardened, a condi-tion that affects the ability of the blood to flow freely. Generally,blood pressure that is consistently 150/90 is reason for concern.Some researchers contend that a greater difference between theupper number and lower number of the blood pressure representsan increased risk of heart attack. Recent research also suggests a linkbetween high blood pressure and Alzheimer‚Äôs Disease.48Senior Fitness
# Heart Disease
Research shows that age alone does not wear out the heart. Disuse,
misuse, and disease do. In a comprehensive study involving womenwith heart disease who exercised, researchers found that moderateexercise, such as brisk walking several times a week, cut heart attackrisk by up to 50 percent. Another study that analyzed 73,000women enrolled in the Boston-based Nurse‚Äôs Health Study, foundthat physical activity reduced the risk of stroke by more than 40percent. This was one of the first strong suggestions that exerciseworks as well for women as it does for men.
An interesting study completed by Dr. Hervert DeVries found that
a group of older men, followed through forty-two weeks of vigor-ous conditioning improved the way their bodies consumed oxygenand how their lungs took in and used air. (The body‚Äôs ability to useoxygen efficiently is a sign of fitness.) Another landmark studywhich revealed that older adults can improve cardiovascular fitnessas they age was performed by Dr. Fred Kasch at San Diego StateUniversity. Dr. Kasch carefully observed fifteen men, aged forty-fiveto sixty-five, for more than twenty years, and what he found shouldbe reassuring to all of us. These men maintained a healthy body

# Respiratory System
As has already been mentioned, vital capacity is the maximum
amount of air that can be moved out of the lungs during a singlebreath, following a maximal inspiration. The elasticity of the lungsdirectly affects vital capacity. Age and inactivity affect elasticity, andvital capacity may decline by about 40 percent between ages twentyand seventy.
Although the breathing muscles can be strengthened with exercise,
most changes to the lungs are irreversible. Fortunately, though,because of the large reserve capacity of the lungs, these changes canbe tolerated quite well. Older adults can demonstrate improve-ments in maximal oxygen consumption similar to their youngercounterparts though it may take longer to achieve such enhancedfunction. An older adult participating in an aerobics program may50Senior Fitness
submaximal heart rate:
Equates to about 70 to 85percent of maximal heartrate (MHR).enjoy a number of improvements in cardiorespiratory fitness such
as a decrease in submaximal heart rate, a faster recovery heart rate,and a decrease in systolic blood pressure at rest and during exercise.And not only do good physiological benefits occur with regularexercise, but an older adult with respiratory dysfunction can expe-rience psychological and social benefits from a regular aerobic exer-cise program as well. These benefits include decreased anxiety andtension, improved self-confidence and self-esteem, and a sense ofwell-being.
The evidence is in: older people can improve heart and lung func-
tion. Remember, all it takes is a mild to moderate program, three tofour times per week, at a light intensity, which is 60 to 70 percent ofmaximum heart rate. It is important to remember that some indi-viduals may need to begin at an intensity below 60 percent of theirmaximum heart rate and start at a duration of ten minutes or lessand gradually increase to twenty or thirty minutes. Others mayhave a good aerobic capacity but lack the muscular endurance toperform the aerobic activities. This will require them to start slow-ly. The most important thing to do is move and move regularly.Remind your clients that it is not how hard they work but howoften they work. It is better for older adults to exercise a little everyday than to exercise a lot once or twice a week.

Respiratory System

# Skeletal System
Bone Density
It is projected by 2020 half of all Americans over fifty will be at risk
for a bone fracture. Some women begin to lose bone mineral massduring their thirties, and men begin to lose it at about age fifty.Women lose approximately 1 percent per year until menopause,when the pace accelerates to between 2 and 4 percent per year forfour to five years, then returns to about 1 percent per year. By theage of seventy, some women have a total loss of bone mass of near-ly 30 percent. Men, on the other hand, lose about 0.5 percent peryear and generally are not at risk for spontaneous fractures untilthey are in their eighties. This gradual, but continual decrease inbone mass in women and elderly men results in structural weaken-ing and poses a high risk of spontaneous fractures.
An estimated 1.3 million fractures occur each year because of
osteoporosis, and they cost billions of dollars to repair and rehabil-itate. Research shows that once an older woman has broken her hip,rarely will she return to her pre-fracture health status. Also, it hasbeen reported that older women who suffer from hip fractures havea 30 to 50 percent death rate in the first year after the fracture.Demographically, osteoporosis is more prominent in white, slenderwomen than in African-American women. It also appears that if aperson has a larger amount of bone mass when young, this will pro-vide protection from bone problems later in life.
The causes of osteoporosis are multi-factorial and therefore are dif-
ficult to isolate. Genetic and environmental factors interact to affectthe rate of bone loss and the integrity of the remaining structure.Metabolic disorders involving the digestive system, renal function,diabetes, and hormonal levels increase the rate of bone loss. Poorlifestyle habits such as insufficient calcium and vitamin D intake,physical inactivity, and smoking can further accelerate bone loss.52Senior Fitness
Physical activity is necessary for maintaining bone material content
and serves as a stimulus for increasing bone mass. Studies haveshown that immobility leads to bone loss and that the stress ofweight-bearing exercise builds up mineral mass.
Wolfe‚Äôs Law states that the robustness of a bone is in
direct proportion to the load placed upon it.
If we can prevent falls and lessen the negative impact of those thatoccur, we will be able to drastically improve the quality of life formany older adults. Decreased bone density accounts for manyinjuries in seniors, but that risk can be lessened with regular activi-ty. Research has shown that forty to fifty minutes of weight-bearingaerobic exercise performed three to four days per week appears toadequately maintain bone mass, thereby preventing osteoporosis inolder adults. Aerobic exercise without weight-bearing activity is not
as important to the treatment of osteoporosis as isometric orweight lifting exercise. Basically stated, weight-bearing aerobic exer-cise and weight training are the best ways to increase bone density.
‚Ä¢ Even women with bone densities near fracture
threshold levels can increase bone density with
appropriate exercise.
‚Ä¢ Bone density returns to pre-exercise levels when
women stop exercising.
‚Ä¢ Many nutritionists believe that avoiding soft
drinks can help maintain bone density becausea chemical contained in these drinks extracts
calcium from the bones.
Healthy Aging
# Unit 5 53
Bone Density Studies
The findings on hip fractures, first reported in the January 1991
issue of Epidemiology, indicate that women who actively exercisedaily are 40 percent less likely to fall. Active exercises identified inthe study were such activities as biking, tennis, jogging, vigorouswalking, dancing, and resistance training exercise programs. Lessvigorous activities were golfing, gardening, and household chores.Findings suggest that minimally strenuous activities do not sub-stantially reduce the risk of hip fractures.
An intervention study by Harry K. Genant, M.D., Director of
Osteoporosis Research at the University of California, San Francisco,demonstrated that increases in bone mineral density beyond theteen years is possible. In the study, sixty-three women who ranged inage from twenty to thirty-five years were randomly assigned to exer-cising and non-exercising groups. The exercisers engaged in aerobicsand weight training for one hour, three times per week. In a double-blind design, all participants received either calcium supplements upto a total of 1500 milligrams per day or a placebo.
After two years, the exercisers had increased bone mineral density
at all four sites measured: the spinal trabeculae, femoral neck, cal-caneus and trochanter. They also improved in physical fitness meas-ures. Calcium supplementation had no significant effect, perhapsbecause the women in the study already had a fairly high calciumintake, Genant said. The message, according to the researchers, isthat women in their twenties can continue to build bone with amodest investment of time and effort. Women can affect their bonemass in the third decade just by participating in recreational sportstwo to three times per week.
As mentioned earlier, research has shown that forty to fifty minutes
of aerobic, weight-bearing exercise performed three to four days perweek appears to adequately maintain bone mass, thereby preventingosteoporosis in older adults. Most of the research investigating theeffects of exercise on cardiovascular disorders and osteoporosis has54Senior Fitness
been done with aerobic training. It seems reasonable to assume that
similar effects may occur with resistance training.
After recently completing a year-long study on the use of exercise in
improving bone density in women over the age of fifty, Dr. StanleyJ. Birge, Clinical Director of Washington University‚Äôs program onaging at Jewish Hospital, concluded that exercise may be used byyounger women and older women to reverse the effects of osteo-porosis. Birge‚Äôs study included twenty-two women ages fifty to sev-enty-two, including control subjects. ‚ÄúEven those women with bonedensities near the fracture threshold could increase their bone den-sity with appropriate exercise,‚Äù Birge said. He found that bone den-sity went back to baseline levels if the women stopped exercising,but if they continued, it was maintained.
Other recent studies have found that while swimming does little to
improve bone mass, water exercise performed vigorously mayimprove bone mass some. Until further studies are done, though,the best bet if you are concerned about osteoporosis is to combineswimming or water exercise with some type of weight-bearingactivity such as walking or weight-lifting. The key is to expose thebones to stresses that slightly exceed what is normally encountered.

# Unit 5 55

hypertrophy: Increase in
both gross muscle size as
well as individual musclecell size resulting fromtraining (especially weighttraining); due to the adap-tive process whereby themuscles add more mito-chondria, sarcoplasm,myofibrils, interstitial sub-stances such as water, fat,satellite cells, etc. inresponse to highly specificforms of stress.MUSCULAR SYSTEM
By adolescence we are equipped with all the muscle cells we will
have. After that time, a muscle can either get bigger or smaller.When we build muscles ( hypertrophy ) we enlarge the muscle cell.
The opposite is the shrinking of the muscle cell or atrophy. By thetime most people are seventy years of age, they have lost about 25percent of their muscle mass due to disuse. In some cases the loss ofstrength is so severe a person cannot climb or descend stairs, or risefrom a chair without pushing up with their hands.
Strength training can prevent loss of muscle mass and help in
regaining the muscle mass you may have already lost. This is criti-cal for many older adults who lose about three to five ounces ofmuscle mass per year. According to one respected researcher, when
we strip away the fat and skin in anautopsy, we find very skinny old peoplewith regard to muscle mass.
Strength in men is maximal between
the ages of thirty to thirty-five yearsand remains relatively constant untilaround age fifty, after which time itdeclines. The rate of decline in muscu-lar strength with age appears to beslightly less in the upper extremities(small muscle groups) than in the back
and legs (large muscle groups.) Leg and back muscle strengthdeclines by 40 percent. Men who are eighty years old may exhibit a40 percent lower muscle mass than men who are thirty years old.Between middle age and old age, overall body weight drops 10 per-cent, and this is attributed to a decline in lean muscle mass.
Sarcopenia
‚ÄúFrailty‚Äù is used to describe the loss of muscle and strength oftenseen in older people because it‚Äôs a word with which most people arefamiliar. The problem is that ‚Äúfrailty‚Äù has more than one meaning.56Senior Fitness

Sarcopenia is a better term because it indicates both loss of muscle
and strength, as well as the decreased quality of muscle tissue oftenseen in older adults. It‚Äôs a word you are very likely to hear moreabout in the future.
We know that muscle-building exercises can improve strength in
older adults, but many questions remain about muscle loss andaging. Researchers want to know, for example, if factors other thana sedentary lifestyle contribute to muscle loss. Does age itself causechanges in the muscles of older people? Is muscle loss related tochanges in hormones or nutrition? These are the kinds of questionsscientists are examining now. The answers may lead to ways ofhelping us keep our strength as we age.
Strength and Muscle Facts
1. Men and women generally achieve maximum
strength between the ages of twenty and thirty.
2. After age thirty, muscular strength declines.
3. A 20 percent decline in muscle strength usually
occurs between the ages of forty and sixty-five
and is attributed to age and disuse.
4. Muscle mass has been found to decrease from 35
percent to 46 percent after the age of sixty.
5. Reduced muscle mass is a factor responsible for
age-associated loss of strength.
6. The loss of muscle volume may be due to
reduced fiber size, particularly in the fast twitch(Type II) fibers seen in power/explosive activities.
7. By age sixty-five, most muscle groups show a loss
of maximal force.
8. Men and women can improve strength with age.
Healthy Aging
# Unit 5 57
The Functional Aspects of Muscle Mass
and Strength
Dr. Herbert DeVries found that strength-training exercise has a
tremendous impact on the maintenance and development of mus-cle mass in the aging adult. The reductions in the musculoskeletalsystem seen in older adults result in reductions of muscle tone andsize which usually leads to an avoidance of heavy, physical activity,which leads to less strength, and a vicious circle of decline in func-tion and strength gets underway. Research has found that the majordeclines seen in muscle strength are not an expected outcome ofage. In a landmark study, ten frail men and women as old as nine-ty-six worked out with free weights, and within two months, par-ticipants had already boosted thigh muscle strength by an average58Senior Fitness
Figure 5-1 Reductions in the musculoskeletal system lead to reduced muscle tone and
size, which can result in an avoidance of physical activity. Decreased physical activity
reduces muscle tone and size even more, and a cycle of decline ensues.

of 174 percent, and muscle mass had increased by 9 percent. What
does this mean in real-life terms? Five volunteers who originallycould walk only one-fourth of a mile doubled their walking dis-tance. Two other volunteers who had previously needed canes towalk no longer needed them at all.
Tufts University scientist Miriam Nelson, Ph.D., maintains that,
‚ÄúOur muscles have the ability to grow and get stronger from birthuntil we die.‚Äù By making a lifelong habit of even minimal weight-bearing exercise and resistance training (for 15 minutes a day, twotimes per week) we can maintain as much as 94 percent of our mus-cle mass well into old age.
Recent research shows that increased strength leads to improved
balance and functional mobility among older people. Myths aboutthe inability of older people to increase their strength are just that.Nearly every senior patient can benefit from basic resistance andstrength training programs.
In the 1970‚Äôs, we heard most exercise physiologists say that aerobic
exercise was critical. Unfortunately, in their enthusiasm to makethat point, they failed to remind us that fit people also need to com-bine both strength training and stretching exercises in addition tocardiovascular conditioning. T o be truly ‚Äúphysically fit,‚Äù a personmust incorporate all components of exercise.
According to experts at Tufts University, strength training is the sin-
gle most critical step in retarding the aging process. ‚ÄúIf in fact wecan maintain muscle mass, many of these things we call ‚Äòbiomark-ers‚Äô of aging might actually be biomarkers of inactivity,‚Äù the study‚ÄôsDr. Evans says. Educators and physical trainers who work witholder adults on a daily basis have observed first hand that olderadults can increase their strength, and recent research shows thatimprovements in strength lead to functional improvements in theability to perform normal, daily activities more easily because,among other things, strength training improves balance and mobil-ity. All of this leads to the conclusion that a total fitness program

calcification: The process
in which calcium salts build
up in soft tissue, causing itto harden.includes not only flexibility and aerobic exercise but strength train-
