Body Composition and Muscle Mass
It is not uncommon for people to gain significant fat during mid-life, especially women. Body fat increases with age, commonly froma combination of acquiring more storage fat from sedentary livingand the redistribution of existing stored fat. This increase in bodyfat is of concern because of its possible relationship with diseaseand premature mortality. A study conducted at the HarvardUniversity School of Public Health, found that weight gains of aslittle as eleven to eighteen pounds in mid-life increased a woman‚Äôschances of having a heart attack by 25 percent. And as we know,heart disease is a major killer in both men and women.
Exercise is extremely important in managing body composition in
older adults. For decades, we‚Äôve seen shriveled and non-energeticolder people and have accepted that as the norm. We have also seensubstantially overweight older people and have accepted that as thenorm. The old excuse was, ‚ÄúOh, my metabolism has just sloweddown.‚Äù Scientists looking at metabolic rates in senior athletes havefound, however, that metabolism doesn‚Äôt really slow down thatmuch if the people stay active and if their muscle mass remains sim-ilar to when they were younger. The key factor to remember is thatfit, muscular people use more calories at rest than do sedentarypeople with little muscle mass. Exercise not only increases metabo-lism during exercise, but keeps it revved up for some time afterexercise. Also, muscle burns more calories at rest than fat, so themore muscles you have, the more calories you are burning.
Posture, Mobility, and Flexibility
The aging process as seen in the skeletal and muscular systems alsomanifests itself in areas where these two systems intersect. Poor pos-ture, for instance, contributes to major mobility problems in bothmen and women. Osteoporosis and fractures, along with vertebralligament calcification and muscle weakness, cause muscle imbal-60Senior Fitness
ances, which contribute to postural changes. Some posture changes
are so severe that they can eventually affect locomotion. Recent stud-ies indicate that women who had a pronounced ‚ÄúDowagers Hump‚Äùcould improve their posture by strengthening the upper back mus-cles and stretching the tight chest muscles. These types of exercisesalso simultaneously contribute to increasing bone mass.
People with arthritis can benefit from sensible strength training
because strength training adds stability to the joints, decreases pain,and in most cases increases mobility.
It is generally agreed that joints become less stable and mobile with
age. Aging is often associated with joint surface deterioration, jointstiffness, and loss of flexibility. In fact, some researchers believe thatosteoarthritis is a natural result of the aging process. It is difficult toseparate deterioration caused by aging from that caused by accu-mulated wear and tear. Trauma to joint cartilage results in the for-mation of scar tissue that causes the connective tissue to become
Healthy Aging
# Unit 5 61

kyphosis: A common con-
dition of a curvature of the
upper spine (causing ahumped posture). It can beeither the result of degener-ative diseases (such asarthritis), developmentalproblems, osteoporosiswith compression fracturesof the vertebrae, and/ortrauma.
osteoarthritis: A nonin-
flammatory joint disease of
older persons. The cartilagein the joint wears down,and there is bone growthat the edges of the joints.Results in pain and stiffness,especially after prolongedexercise.
range-of-motion:
Measurement of the extentto which a joint can gothrough all of its normalspectrum of movements.stiffer and less responsive. This build-up can result in a thickened
joint capsule that often contains debris, which in turn impairsrange of motion. Decreased range of motion in joints affects pos-ture, gait, balance and skeletal flexibility, increasing the potential forfalls and injury. The kyphosis that occurs with aging leads to
diminished stature and poor use of respiratory muscles. Breakdownof the joint cartilage leads to osteoarthritis and pain, reduced
mobility, and a diminished ability to accomplish daily activities.Osteoarthritis is generally referred to as the wear-and-tear disease.Osteoarthritis in the elderly principally shows up in those areasreceiving the most mechanical stress. It is unclear if the restrictedrange of motion found in the joints is principally the result of agingor of repeated trauma.
Range-of-motion exercises have been shown to increase flexibility
dramatically in a relatively short period of time. One study by
Munn showed increases in flexibility ranging from 8 percent in theshoulders to 48 percent in the ankles in only twelve weeks ofstretching and dance exercises. However, it is improbable that exer-cise can significantly undo damage to joints that have undergoneextensive degeneration. It is encouraging, though, that flexibilitycan still be increased in older adults aged seventy-two to ninety.Improvement over a two-year period showed an increase in flexi-bility of 11 percent from participation in flexibility classes three tofour times per week.
Opinions are varied in the fitness field concerning the relationship
between osteoarthritis and exercise, especially running. Animal andoccupational studies have demonstrated inconsistent results indetermining the effects of activity and the development ofosteoarthritis. Clinical studies have demonstrated that whenosteoarthritis has been found to exist, it seems to be related to bonymisalignment and prior traumatic injury, not to running. Somestudies have shown that running and regular athletic activity do notincrease the risk of osteoarthritis in those runners with normalanatomy and strength.62Senior Fitness

# Nervous System
As our bodies change, so do our brains. As years pass, the brain may
decrease in weight, and the interconnections among neurons maybe reduced. Exercise has a positive effect on both the physiologicaland psychological functions of the brain, however. Exercise and itseffect on mental capacity, mental flexibility, and general mentalhealth will be discussed in the following paragraphs.
# Mental Capacity
Dr. Marian Diamond of the University of California at Berkeleycontends there is mounting evidence that so long as disease doesnot intervene, the brain retains its capacity to grow new anatomicalconnections, to learn, and to function at high levels. As we age,mental capacity does not necessarily deteriorate, especially in peo-ple who continue to learn and think, who are well nourished, andwho exercise their bodies throughout their lives. What does thebrain need to function? It needs oxygen. How does it obtain oxy-gen? Oxygen is transported to the brain via the blood. The best way
Healthy Aging
# Unit 5 63
to move oxygen-rich blood to the brain is through exercise. Exercise
can be responsible for a quicker and more alert brain.
From the thirties onward, there is a slight mental slowdown. Aging
affects reaction time, which determines how quickly you step on thebrake while driving or how fast you come up with the answers in‚ÄúJeopardy.‚Äù But other functions, such as vocabulary building, getbetter throughout your fifties and sixties. It is true that some men-tal skills do begin to decline around age seventy, but this variesgreatly from individual to individual.
In the Baltimore Longitudinal Study of Aging, more than a quarter
of the volunteers over age seventy showed no decline in memory.Some continued to perform well on the cognitive tasks as theymoved into their eighties. The brain, like the heart, may do moreadapting and less declining with age than previously thought. Takeproblem solving, for example: when faced with a task, older peoplemay draw on different capacities to complete the task. Up to ageseventy, there is little or no decline in problem solving.64Senior Fitness

central nervous system:
System comprised of thebrain and spinal column.
psychomotor: Of, relat-
ing to, or characterizing
movements of the bodyassociated with mentalactivity.Exercise helps to maintain the integrity of the central nervous sys-
tem and psychomotor control, possibly due to increases in region-
al cerebral blood flow or elevated cerebral metabolism from
increased sensory stimulation and arousal. Hence, age-relateddecreases in reaction and movement times are minimized. Middle-aged exercisers, in other words, are blissfully unaware of what theyare not losing. A recent study found that older adults who did ball-room dancing, which combined learning dance steps with move-ment, lost less brain function. And the good news is that even lowintensity activities such as walking and biking can help.
# Mental Flexibility
Research has demonstrated that there is a strong associationbetween regular, vigorous exercise and successful aging. Some pre-liminary research even indicates that exercising can improve brainfunction in older people. The findings show that older adults whoare physically active have faster movement times than their younger,inactive counterparts. Robert Dustman, Chief of theNeuropsychology Laboratory at the Salt Lake City, Utah, Veteran‚ÄôsAdministration Hospital, found that aerobically fit individuals intheir fifties did as well as those in their twenties. These people hadmore mental flexibility and adjusted more quickly to changing tasksthan did people their same age who were ‚Äúcouch potatoes.‚Äù Dr.Dustman postulates that the improved circulation associated withexercise produces this effect.
Another study analyzed women aged fifty-seven to eighty-five who
started exercising regularly, and showed that they were able to gainback some of the mental speed they had previously lost. Still otherstudies showed a dramatic difference in mental acuity (I.Q.)between men in their sixties who exercised and men who did not,as well as improvements in older adults‚Äô ability to stay focused.
T o older adults, most of the mental benefits of exercise such as a
slower decline in memory or the ability to react more quickly whendriving are likely to go unnoticed. The slippage in memory and
Healthy Aging
# Unit 5 65
physical response time is gradual and natural. Scientists now say it
is not inevitable, however.
Arthur Kramer, a University of Illinois psychologist, measured how
quickly forty sedentary adults between the ages of sixty-three andeighty-two responded simultaneously to two differently pitchedtones by hitting buttons. Half the adults went through a ten-weekwater aerobics course, three times a week for forty-five minutes ofexercise, while the others all remained sedentary. The results pub-lished in the Journal of Psychology and Aging (1992), showed
improvement in the exercisers‚Äô speed at hitting laboratory buttons,while the non-exercisers showed no improvement. The regularexercisers responded more quickly upon hearing a tone than didthe sedentary group, even when the non-exercisers were younger bytwenty to fifty years. Normally, reaction time hits its peak whenpeople are in their twenties, begins a subtle decline in the thirties,and is on a downhill roll by the sixties. Dr. Kramer states, ‚ÄúWhatwe‚Äôre finding is that the decline in reaction time beginning in ourmid-thirties is not related to aging so much as to fitness level.‚ÄùAnother important benefit to point out is that with increased men-tal functioning comes increased mental health.
# Mental Health
Exercise can further boost quality of life by keeping people‚Äôs spiritsup as they age. Exercise not only keeps your body strong andhealthy, but can improve your mood as well. A team of Stanfordresearchers randomly assigned three-hundred-sixty people, agedfifty to sixty-five, either to an exercise group or to an inactive con-trol group. The study reported that people who exercised experi-enced improved mood and less depression. The ability to elevateone‚Äôs mood through physical activity is a breakthrough for olderadults, especially those for whom exercise may provide an alterna-tive to medication.66Senior Fitness
Benefits Seen in the Brain and Mental
Health with Exercise
‚Ä¢ Improved ability to perform daily living activities
‚Ä¢ Increased social interaction‚Ä¢ Decreased levels of anxiety‚Ä¢ Decreased levels of depression‚Ä¢ Improved motivation‚Ä¢ Improved self-esteem
In summary, statistics show that Americans are living longer. And
many older Americans don‚Äôt want to merely survive longer‚Äîtheywant to thrive, be active, and stay independent as they age. Contraryto popular misconceptions, only 5 percent of older adults live ininstitutional settings. The majority of older adults are considered tobe healthy, despite various health issues. For the most part, themajority of older adults are independent and doing relatively well.
There are certain age-related changes that can‚Äôt be modified with
the intervention of exercise and healthy choices, such as graying ofthe hair, but fortunately there are many characteristics common toaging that can be positively influenced with regular exercise andsmart health choices. The Journal of the American MedicalAssociation stated that even a minimal amount of exercise‚Äîa brisk
thirty-minute walk once a week‚Äîis enough to provide some pro-tection not only from cardiovascular disease and cancer, but alsofrom stress, anxiety, and depression. Needless to say, fit people feelmore able, and that translates into improved self-efficacy.

# Unit 5 67
# Unit Summary
I. With ‚Äúnormal‚Äù aging the size of the heart and its abili-
ty to pump blood effectively decrease. However, exer-
cise increases the development of collateral vesselswhich can help lessen the risk of heart attack andincrease the likelihood of survival when a heart attackdoes occur. Exercise also reduces the risk of stroke andimproves the body‚Äôs ability to use oxygen efficiently.
II. Age and inactivity affect the elasticity of the lungs;
however, older adults who exercise may see such ben-efits in cardio-respiratory fitness as a decrease inminute ventilation, an increase in vital capacity, and adecrease in respiratory exchange ratio.
III. The gradual but continual decrease in bone mass that
can occur with age results in structural weakening andraises the risk of spontaneous fractures. Regular activi-ty and especially weight bearing exercise can reducethis risk and even return bone density to pre-exerciselevels.
IV. A loss of muscle and strength is often seen in older
people; however, strength training can prevent thisloss and help in regaining muscle mass that may havealready been lost. Many experts regard strength train-ing as the single most important step one can take toretard the aging process. Maintaining muscle massallows individuals to manage body composition andhelps with posture, mobility, and flexibility as well.
V. The brain changes with age, often decreasing in
weight and losing interconnections among neurons.Exercise helps maintain the integrity of the centralnervous system and psychomotor control due toincreases in cerebral blood flow and elevated cerebralmetabolism. Further, exercise has been shown to havea positive effect on mental health by decreasing anxi-ety and levels of depression and improving motivationand self-esteem.68Senior Fitness

MYTHS OF FITNESSUNIT 6Senior Fitness
Facts and Myths of Fitness
# Unit 6 71
# Learning Objectivesunit Outline
I. Exercise Facts and Myths
II. Questions Commonly Asked by
Older Adult Students
After completing this unit you will be able to:
‚Ä¢ Identify many of the facts and myths surrounding
exercise and provide your clients with accurate infor-
mation to help them with achieve their fitness goals
‚Ä¢ Know many of the questions commonly asked by
older adult clients and have clear answers to thesequestions
This unit identifies many of the facts and myths about fitness, and
answers those questions that are most frequently asked by olderadults, and often younger ones, about exercising.
Myth 1: By exercising only a few minutes every day you
can become fit.
Fact: There are no easy ways to fitness. The only way to become
physically ‚Äúfit‚Äù is to exercise regularly. A total fitness program
involves the development of muscular strength and endurance,flexibility, balance, coordination, and cardiovascular endurance. Itis widely known that an individual must exercise regularly to main-tain and/or increase his or her fitness level.
Though less frequent and less intense programs have been shown to
improve different areas of fitness, an ideal program consists of thir-
ty to sixty minutes of aerobic exercise at target heart rate (THR)every other day coupled with strengthening exercises on alternate
days. This schedule would give the individual a six-day exercise reg-
imen during the week.
The aerobic portion of the workout should consist of activities that
use large muscle groups (i.e., legs), are continuous, and last a mini-mum of thirty minutes. However, recent research suggests that threeten-minute periods of exercise a day is equal to one thirty-minuteperiod of exercise. Examples of ae robic activities include brisk walk-
ing, bicycling, swimming, and cross-country skiing. Strength train-ing may include the use of free weights, machines, bands, or anyother device which is safe and provides adequate resistance.
Myth 2: Creams, pills, massages, herbal wraps, vibrating
belts, roller machines, and electrical exercise machines will melt fat.
Fact: Unfortunately, there is no magic pill, cream, or passive equip-
ment which is able to melt or metabolize fat. T o lose weight perma-
nently, you must make a lifestyle change that includes moderatephysical activity and sensible eating habits. Save your money.Exercise regularly, and eat properly.72Senior Fitness
Sample Aerobic Workout
Activity Time
Warm up and stretching 5 to 10 minutes
Aerobic activity 30 minutes
Cool down and stretching 5 to 10 minutes
Total 40 to 50 minutes
Myth 3: Doing twenty-five sit-ups and fifty side bends every
day will reduce the size of your stomach.
Fact: This is probably the greatest misconception in fitness. The
body does not have the ability to spot reduce or selectively choose
spots where fat can be lost. When you lose fat, you lose it from allover the body and not from one particular spot. There are only fourways to lose body fat:
1. Exercise aerobically at least three times each week
(aim to burn 3000 calories per week)
2. Limit the amount of food you eat
3. Surgically remove the fat4. Employ a combination of (1) and (2)
The best way to lose fat is to eat properly and exercise regularly, lim-
iting the weight loss to no more than two pounds per week to pre-vent the loss of lean body mass. Our bodies are composed of fat and
Facts and Myths of Fitness
# Unit 6 73

lean body mass. By losing too much weight too quickly, you are at
risk of losing more than just fat. T otal body weight is not important.Rather it‚Äôs what that weight is composed of that matters. For exam-ple, a one-hundred-pound fashion model may appear to be ‚Äúthin‚Äùbut may actually be considered obese (having excessive body fat);whereas a professional football player may weigh three hundredpounds and be considered lean (having very little body fat).
Myth 4: One only needs to warm
up before exercising on cold days.
Fact: Warming up is an essential part of every exercise session and
should always be included, not just on cold days. The purpose of the
warm-up is to prepare the body for exercise. This is accomplishedby beginning your exercise session at a very low level of intensityand gradually increasing it into a more vigorous workout. By slow-ly increasing your activity, you are allowing the blood to flow intothe muscles and raise muscular temperature. As the temperatureincreases, the muscle and connective tissue become more pliableand less susceptible to injury.
Cooling down after exercise is as valuable as warming up properly.
Stretching and slow walking allow the body to recover from exerciseand maintain suppleness. Train like an expensive racehorse: warmup before and cool down after your activity! It is also best to stretchat the end of each workout.
Myth 5: When you stop exercising, your
muscles turn into fat.
Fact: Muscle and fat are two completely different tissue types which
are not interchangeable. Everyone has a certain amount of fat and
muscle cells located throughout their bodies, which are establishedby adolescence. The size of these cells, not the number of cells,changes with diet and exercise. As we begin an exercise activity, ourmuscles become stronger and perhaps larger. Augment this withdieting, and you begin to lose body fat. As the percentage of body74Senior Fitness
fat is decreased, the size of the fat cells decreases which gives us the
appearance of being more muscular and toned.
When you stop exercising, the size of your muscle cells begin to
decrease, and if you continue eating the way you did while you wereactive, those excessive calories will be converted into fat. Therefore,when you stop exercising the muscle cells become smaller and thefat cells become larger, giving the impression that muscle is turninginto fat.
Myth 6: Taking vitamins alone will improve your
fitness and performance.
Fact: Not so! As long as humans have been competing in athletics,
they have been seeking the magic formula that would make them
run faster, throw further, and endure longer. There is still no scien-tific evidence to suggest that vitamins, enzymes, or any ergogenicaid in the absence of a proper diet and training program willenhance an athlete‚Äôs performance.
T o function properly, the body requires more than fifty different
nutrients daily. These are classified into six separate categories: (1)water, (2) carbohydrates, (3) protein, (4) lipids, commonly known
Facts and Myths of Fitness
# Unit 6 75
Fat Reservoir
Excess fat is stored
until the fat is usedas fuel.
Nucleus
Figure 6-1 Fat Cell
as fats, (5) minerals, and (6) vitamins. When you eat a well-bal-
anced diet that incorporates each of the four basic food groups anddrink plenty of water, you will only require vitamin or mineral sup-plements in special circumstances (such as illness).
Myth 7: The ‚Äúno pain, no gain‚Äù adage is a standard
everyone should follow when exercising.
Fact: For the highly trained athlete this may be an appropriate
maxim, but for the average individual it is not. Pain should be con-
sidered the body‚Äôs built-in warning system letting you know some-thing may be wrong. If you experience any pain, discontinue theexercise; however, learn to distinguish between pain and discom-fort. A better adage is ‚Äúno mild discomfort, no gain.‚Äù
Y ou should only exercise to a point which you can tolerate com-
fortably. Exercise should not and does not have to be painful. Beaware of your capabilities and exercise within your limitations. Ifyou exercise too strenuously, you may be setting yourself up forinjury. If your fitness goal is to maintain or increase health, it isunnecessary to push your body to its limits. For example, if you jog,select a pace at which you can carry on a conversation. Exerciseshould become a joyful lifetime commitment not an act of dailydrudgery. If possible, exercise with a partner and make it part ofyour daily routine‚Äîwalk at lunchtime, take the stairs instead of theelevator, or park your car several blocks from your workplace andwalk. Exercise should be fun!
Myth 8: Lifting weights will cause women to develop
large muscles.
Fact: Unless a woman spends a great deal of time working out in the
gym, like bodybuilders do, she does not need to worry about devel-
oping large muscles. Women simply do not possess the same levelof testosterone (the hormone partly responsible for muscularhypertrophy) as men; thus it is difficult for most women to increasetheir muscle size significantly.76Senior Fitness
Valsalva response: If the
glottis (the narrowest part
of the larynx is closed fol-lowing full inspiration andthe expiratory muscles arefully activated, the com-pressive forces of exhalationcan increase the intratho-racic pressure from 2 or 3mm Hg to upwards of 100mm Hg above atmosphericpressure. This forced exha-lation against a closed glot-tis is called the Valsalvamaneuver (named after theItalian anatomist who firstexplained the phenome-non) The intrathoracic pres-sure causes the veins tocompress and this in turnresults in significantlyreduced venous blood flowinto the heart and into thebrain. Dizziness, ‚Äúspots‚Äùbefore the eyes and black-out can ensue. This is onegood reason why thosewith cardiac problemsshould refrain from all-outstraining (as in isometriccontraction), and insteadshould engage in a morerhythmic type of weightlift-ing technique while breath-ing normally.QUESTIONS COMMONLY ASKED BY THEOLDER ADULT STUDENT
1. How should I breathe during exercise?
Avoid holding your breath because it can elevateyour blood pressure and induce a V alsalva
response. The most important thing is to breathecomfortably. Don‚Äôt concentrate on any particular‚Äúway‚Äù to breathe. Breathing is an automaticresponse. If you find yourself holding your breath,count out loud or talk to your partner while doingthe exercise. Remember to breathe freely andeffortlessly. Some trainers suggest you exhale witheffort and inhale with the eccentric phase (relax-ation) when lifting weights.
2. Are vitamin and mineral supplements necessary?
When eating a well-balanced diet and drinkingplenty of fluids, there are only certain circum-stances, such as illness, which require the need forvitamin or mineral supplements.
3. How can I eliminate cellulite?
Cellulite does not exist. It is a term ‚Äúinvented‚Äù by aFrench beautician. What we understand to be cel-lulite is nothing more than subcutaneous fat, andas previously mentioned, the only way to elimi-nate fat is to exercise regularly and eat properly.Cellulite looks like cottage cheese because of theway the connective tissue holds in the fat.
4. Why do some people gain weight when they
start exercising?
Muscle is heavier than fat! As you become involved
in an exercise program and eat properly, your body
Facts and Myths of Fitness
# Unit 6 77
becomes leaner and begins to consist of less body
fat. It‚Äôs important to understand that it‚Äôs not howmuch you weigh but what that weight consists ofthat matters. Be careful of Body Mass Index for-mulas because highly muscled people will be classi-fied as fat according to these standards.
5. What is the best time of the day to exercise?
Y ou are the best judge of when you should exer-cise. Early morning workouts can be dangerousbecause blood platelets are stickier and could trig-ger heart attacks. In general, though, listen to yourbody to determine what time of the day is best foryou. Just remember that it is important to usecommon sense. If you have arthritis, exercise whenyou have the least amount of stiffness and pain.On very hot or humid days, it‚Äôs best not to exerciseat noon or in the early afternoon. If you are exer-cising outdoors, consider your route, lighting, and78Senior Fitness

delayed onset muscle
soreness (DOMS): The
pain or discomfort oftenfelt 24 to 72 hours afterexercising and subsidesgenerally within two tothree days. This is morecommonly known as being‚Äòstiff‚Äô the morning after asporting activity.traffic. Try to exercise daily and in the same timeperiod each day. Make it part of your routine, and
you are more apt to stay with your program.
6. What should I wear when I exercise?
Y ou should wear clothing that is comfortable and
allows you the freedom to move easily and doesn‚Äôtinterfere with breathing or normal blood flow.Naturally, depending upon the activity, your attirewill vary.
Avoid rubberized outfits that restrict the body from
cooling properly. Comfortable shoes, which are wellfitting and appropriate for your activity, are a must.In addition, shoes should provide the proper trac-tion, as well as support, to prevent injuries.
7. How do I get rid of muscle cramps?
Muscle cramps can be brought on by inadequateblood flow, lack of essential electrolytes, or generalmuscle fatigue. T o help eliminate cramps, youshould first stretch the muscles which areinvolved. For example, cramps of the calf are com-mon. Eliminate these by gently pointing your toesback toward you and holding this position. Next,to increase blood flow to the area, try rubbing themuscle. This usually is helpful. Back off a little onyour workout intensity.
8. What causes muscle soreness (commonly
called ‚Äúdelayed onset muscle soreness‚Äù)?
Muscle soreness may be expected after beginning
an exercise program following a long period ofinactivity. During exercise the muscle and connec-tive tissue may be minutely torn causing spasmand fluid retention. This spasm can irritate nerveendings, thus causing pain. T o help prevent sore-
Facts and Myths of Fitness
# Unit 6 79
ness: warm-up and stretch properly, progress
slowly in your exercise program, and cool-downeach time you exercise. If muscle soreness shouldoccur, mild stretching and a low-intensity activitysuch as walking can help relieve discomfort.
9. Is it okay to drink water during exercise?
T o carry out its normal physiological processes thebody needs water among many other things. T oexercise safely you should consume water before,during, and after exercise. This ensures that thewater you have lost through sweat is replenished.This is especially important on warm and humiddays. Urine should be clear at least once per day.Generally, the darker the urine, the more waterintake is needed.
10. What should I do if I experience increased
pain after exercise?
Remember the two-hour rule: two hours after
exercise, you should not experience a significantincrease in pain. If you do, back off. If the pain isin the joints, re-evaluate your routine.80Senior Fitness

III. Special Precautions
to be Aware of with
Older Adult Clients
a. Movements to avoid
This section contains information on exercises and techniques that
may hurt or strain muscles, joints, or ligaments and are now con-sidered contraindicated for many adults. Where appropriate, alter-
native exercises or techniques have been provided.
It is important to remember that any exercise can become con-
traindicated if it is performed improperly or prescribed for thewrong reason. When selecting exercises for an individual, ask thefollowing questions:
1. What is the purpose of the exercise?
2. Is the exercise necessary for achieving this partici-
pant‚Äôs program goals?
3. Can the exercise be performed safely by this partic-
ipant without violating principles of good biome-
chanics? If not, what is an alternative exercise?contraindication: Any
circumstance or conditionthat makes a therapy, pro-cedure, or exercise inadvis-able.
hyperextension:
Extension of a limb or partbeyond the normal limit.
impingement: An
encroachment on the space
occupied by soft tissue,such as nerve or muscle. Inthis context, impingementrefers to nerve irritation(e.g., from pressure or fric-tion) associated with mus-cles.
supination: Assuming a
horizontal position facing
upward. In the case of thehand, it also means turningthe palm to face forward.The opposite of pronation(supine).CONTROVERSIAL EXERCISES
Certain exercises have been around so long that it is considered
irreverent to question their value. Good sense indicates that anyexercise can be considered detrimental if done incorrectly. Theimportant question you must ask when evaluating an exercise is‚ÄúWhat is the benefit-to-risk ratio?‚Äù Can exercise cause more harmthan good? Is there a safer, more effective way to exercise that bodypart without potentially harming another body part?
High-risk areas include the knees, shoulders, neck, low back, hips
and ankles. Special attention should be paid when implementingexercises that involve those areas.
# Knees
The action of the knee is to flex and extend. Movements that hyper-extend or rotate the tibia should be eliminated. It is a good idea to
keep ‚Äúsoft knees‚Äù when exercising. Also,you should avoid twisting the body withthe feet planted. Do not include any exer-cises that do not keep the knees over thefeet. The knees and toes should alwayspoint in the same direction. Always avoidoverflexion of the knee joint.
# Shoulders
Shoulder impingement is increasingly
becoming a concern for exercisers. Cautionshould be used when bringing the armsabove the head. Movements need to becontrolled and the hands should besupinated when raising the arms above
shoulder height. Shoulders should berelaxed when performing arm movements.84Senior Fitness

flexion: A movement
which moves the two ends
of a jointed body part clos-er to each other, as inbending the arm. The actof flexing or bending.
pronation: Assuming a
facedown position. Of the
hand, turning the palmbackward or downward. Ofthe foot, lowering the inner(medial) side of the foot soas to flatten the arch. Theopposite of supination.
gait: Pattern of walking or
locomotion.NECK
Neck problems can occur when activities
cause excessive hyperextension and rota-tion. Neck movements should never bedone quickly or in an unsafe fashion.
# Low Back
The majority of adults will experienceback pain at some time in their life. Thisis a result of the culmination of misuseand abuse. It is critical that the back beprotected when exercising and the lowback be stabilized. Any exercise that cre-ates hyperextension of the low backshould be eliminated. Caution should be
used when doing movements that cause lateral flexion of the spine
and obliques forward rotation of the upper body.
# Ankles And Feet
It is important to pay attention to the position that your client‚Äôs feetare in when they land and when moving to avoid supination andpronation . Check your client‚Äôs gait to make sure that a preexisting
problem will not inhibit proper form.
Exercises Older Adults Should Avoid
# Unit 7 85

# Exercises To Eliminate
1. Head circles or head-rolling exercises
Rationale: Head circles may strain the supporting ligaments
which maintain stability in the cervical spine (neck area). This
exercise also causes excessive compression of the intervertebraldisk and impingement of the vertebra. Because of the degen-erative changes that occur with aging (i.e., osteoporosis), theolder individual is moresusceptible to injuryand so should avoid this exercise.
Alternative: Slowly turn
the head from left to
right, or lean the headfrom side to side.Maintain proper align-ment of head, neck, and shoulders.
2. Standing toe touches
Rationale: This exercise can hyperextend the joint capsule
and stretch the ligaments of the knee. In addition, it causes
excessive pressure of the interverte-bral disk in the lower back, whichmay strain the soft tissue structures.Rounding of the upper back is discouraged.
Alternative: While sitting on the
floor or in a chair, extend one or
both legs out in front of the body,keeping the back straight and theknees slightly bent. Slowly bend for-ward and reach toward the toes.86Senior Fitness
Head Circles
Standing Toe Touches
ballistic movement: An
exercise or sports-related
movement in which part ofthe body is ‚Äúthrown‚Äù orbounced against the resist-ance of antagonist musclesor against the limits of ajoint. The latter, especially,is considered dangerous tothe integrity of ligamentsand tendons. High-impact,rapid, jerking movements.3. Bouncing during warm-up
or stretching exercises
Rationale: Bouncing or stretching ballistically does not
increase flexibility; rather it causes the stretched muscle tocontract and shorten which may induce strain or tear themuscle fibers.
Alternative: Slowly stretch to a point of mild discomfort, and
hold this position for 10 to 30 seconds.
4. Sit-ups with straight legs and
hands behind the head
Rationale: Doing straight-leg sit-ups does not work the
abdominal muscles but rather the hip flexor muscles. This
technique encourages a hyper-extended posture of the lowback ( lordosis ) and causes unnecessary strain to this area.
Holding the hands behind the head may contribute to a for-ward head position and kyphosis, as well as cause excessivepressure on the cervical area. In addition, having one‚Äôs feetheld during a sit-up is similar to doing straight leg sit-ups; itis not the abdominal muscles that are the prime movers butthe hip flexors. Many experts also recommend not usingabdominal or back extension machines in gyms.
Alternative: Lie flat on the floor, bend both knees, and bring
the heels as close as possible to the buttocks. Cross the arms
across the chest, slowly tuck the chin to the chest, and lift theshoulders off the floor. It is not necessary to pull farther thanhalfway up toward the knees.
5. Full squats or deep knee bends
Rationale: This kind of exercise creates excessive stretching of
the ligaments of the knee and may pinch the joint cartilage.Once the ligaments of the knee have been stretched, theycause instability of the knee joint.
Alternative: When squatting, do not go beyond the point at
which the thighs are parallel to the floor.
Exercises Older Adults Should Avoid
# Unit 7 87
lordosis: The forward
curving of the spine at the
neck (cervical spine) andlower back (lumbar spine).Often used to refer to anabnormally increased cur-vature of the lumbar spine.
6. Double leg lifts
Rationale: Like straight
leg sit-ups, double leg
lifts do not work the
abdominal area. Theyencourage a hyper-extended posture andcreate excessive pressurein the lumbar area.
Alternative: T o increase abdominal strength, perform sit-ups
or curl-ups with both knees bent, feet flat on the floor, arms
across the chest, and the heels as close to the body as possible.
7. Hurdler‚Äôs stretch
Rationale: By placing the leg in this bent position, excessive
stretching occurs to the ligaments of the knee which can causeunnecessary damage.
Alternative: Place the legs in front of the body and stretch
forward at the waist while keeping the back straight; or bend
one leg and place the bottom of the foot on the inside of thethigh and reach toward the toes. Y ou can also use the seatedstretches described in exercise two.
8. Trunk twist
Rationale: Twisting strains the lumbar
area and possibly the knee. T orquegenerated by the twisting action in achair causes considerable strain to thelower back.
Alternative: Lean left and right in a
sitting position.88Senior Fitness
Trunk Twist
Double Leg Lifts
9. Isometric exercises
Rationale: This type of exercise is contraindicated for
individuals with a history of cardiovascular disease,
especially high blood pressure. Isometric arm exercisesincrease blood pressure.
Alternative: T o minimize the risk of unnecessary high blood
pressure, exercise with a comfortable weight, breathe properly,
and perform the exercise through the entire motion allowableby the joint.
10. Hyper-extending or arching of back
Rationale: This compresses the disks of the lower back and
encourages the posture of lordosis (sway back).
Exercises Older Adults Should Avoid
# Unit 7 89
Prone Trunk Raise
Alternative: T o help strengthen the lower back extensors, sit
in a chair, bend forward, and have someone resist slightly on
your upper back when returning to an upright position.
Use caution when doing hypertension exercises, they may not
be appropriate for some people with low back problems.Always consult a physician or physical therapist before doingextension exercises.
11. Immediate rest after intense exercise
Rationale: Resting immediately after exercising prevents ade-
quate return of blood back to the heart.
Alternative: Continue with low level exercise, such as walking,
to encourage proper return of blood to the heart and thereby
preventing any ‚Äúpooling‚Äù effect which may occur in the lowerextremities.
12. Holding the breath during exercise
Rationale: This decreases the return of blood to the heart
which can result in dizziness or fainting.
Alternative: Breathe naturally; do not force inhalation or
exhalation.
# Special Precautions To Be Aware
# Of With Older Adult Clients
# Movements To Avoid
1. Hopping, jumping, jarring/twisting
This stresses the ankle, knee, hip and back and may cause fur-ther damage to bones in inner ear if structure is already weak-ened. Hopping increases impact stress threefold and maycause shin splints (pain in the lower front of leg). These exer-cises are not recommended for people with arthritis, osteo-porosis, or balance problems.
Alternative: Low impact exercises that keep one foot on the
floor at all times.
2. Bringing the knee to the chest more than 90
degrees and turning the leg inward
Following total hip replacement, or in case of shallow hip
socket, this can result in dislocation of hip.
Alternative: Standing or seated exercises that emphasize the
strengthening of the muscles of the lower body in order to
improve hip movement.90Senior Fitness
varicose veins: Twisted,
widened veins caused by
swollen or enlarged bloodvessels. The blood vesselshave enlarged due a weak-ening in the vein‚Äôs wall orvalves.3. Long periods of standing, especially with
little movement
This increases varicose veins.
Alternative: Keep moving slightly and do not stand for long
periods of time.
4. Locked joints or snapping limbs straight
These movements strain ligaments and cartilage by going
beyond the normal limits of the joint and are especially badfor people with arthritis.
Alternative: Straighten legs and arms slowly and smoothly.
5. Side and front lunges
This can result in a strained knee joint.Alternative: Keep the knee centered over the foot at 90-degree
angle while lunging to the side or front.
Exercises Older Adults Should Avoid
# Unit 7 91
Make sure the lead leg‚Äôs
knee does not protrudepast the toes.
6. Loud music
This can result in sensorineural hearing loss . Aerobic classes
have been found to have music at 110 decibels and above. Thesafe maximum range is 80 decibels. Damage to the hammerand stirrup in the inner ear has been linked to excessive jar-ring/bouncing, especially when compounded with loud music.
Alternative: Play music at lower volume. Do not place speak-
ers near students or the instructor.sensorineural hearing
loss: A type of hearing loss
in which the root cause liesin the vestibulocochlearnerve (Cranial nerve VIII),the inner ear, or centralprocessing centers of thebrain.
7. Straight arm circles and side arm
raises with palm down
This can result in excessive wear and tear on the shoulder
joint.
Alternative: Keep palm up whenever arm is raised/circled
above shoulder height.
8. Standing hamstring stretch
This may overstretch hamstrings, which can irritate the sciatic
nerve . Additionally, students with poor balance may fall while
performing this activity.Alternative: Seated hamstring and calf stretch.92Senior Fitness
sciatic nerve: The nerve
running along the buttocks,
posterior thigh and leg,originating primarily fromthe L5 and S1 nerve roots.
Safety Summary
‚Ä¢ Don‚Äôt bounce.
‚Ä¢ Don‚Äôt hyperextend joints.‚Ä¢ Don‚Äôt arch the back; maintain a neutral spine.‚Ä¢ Balance strength exercises with stretching exercises.‚Ä¢ Balance agonist and antagonist groups.‚Ä¢ Always protect the spine and joints.‚Ä¢ Trust your ability to distinguish the feel of a safe
stretch.
‚Ä¢ Know when to back off to avoid developing over-
use injuries.
‚Ä¢ Always center knees directly over feet.‚Ä¢ Always warm-up.
Exercises Older Adults Should Avoid
# Unit 7 93
# Unit Summary
I. There are some exercises that may hurt or strain mus-
cles, joints, or ligaments, and that should be avoided
when working with the older adult client.
II. High-risk areas include the knees, shoulders, neck, low
back, hips and ankles. Special attention should be paidwhen implementing exercises that involve those areas.
III. Replace head-rolling exercises, standing toe touches,
bouncing during warm-up / stretching, straight-legsitups, full squats, deep knee bends, double leg lifts,hurdler‚Äôs stretch, trunk twist, isometric exercises, andback hyper-extension with alternatives. Also, clientsshould avoid immediate rest after exercise and holdingtheir breath during exercise.
IV. Older clients should avoid hopping, jumping, jarring,
and twisting; bringing the knee to the chest more than90 degrees and turning the leg inward; long periods ofstanding with little movement; locked joints; snappingthe limbs straight; side lunges; lunges that extend theknee beyond the toe; loud music; straight arm circlesand side arm raises with palms down; and standinghamstring stretches.

# How To Teach Older Adults
Numerous principles affect an individual‚Äôs rate and amount of
learning. The teacher of adults must understand these principles ifhe or she wishes to be successful and effective. Following are a fewthings that almost always hold true and that the wise trainer woulddo well to keep in mind.
Law of Effect
People tend to accept and repeat those actions that are pleasant andsatisfying and avoid those which are not. In other words‚Äînothingsucceeds like success, so make every effort to see that your clientsachieve some level of success during each class.
Law of Primacy (The Impact of First Impressions)
First impressions are lasting. Make initial client meetings safe,meaningful, and clearly structured.
Law of Disuse
A skill not practiced or knowledgenot used will be forgotten quickly.Important skills and conceptsneed to be reviewed in order to beretained. Adults want to be able touse the knowledge and skills theyhave learned soon after the origi-nal teaching/learning experience.They are not willing to wait foryears to see the results.
Law of Vigor
A dramatic or exciting learning experience is more likely to beremembered than a routine or boring experience. Let your teachingcome alive. Use vivid examples, and participate with the partici-pants. Remember that humor is a useful teaching tool. If studentsare laughing, then you know they are listening. 96Senior Fitness

# Accommodating
# Learning-Style Preferences
Each individual has a preferential learning style involving a domi-
nant sensory channel. T o foster the learning experience, the instruc-tor needs to know the learning style of each participant.
The importance of trainers‚Äô interactions with clients cannot be
overemphasized. The primary function of a trainer is to hasten theparticipant‚Äôs cognitive, psychomotor, and affective development asthey relate to physical education. Trainers should be aware of thetypes of interactions that foster each client‚Äôs learning, motivation toexercise, and attendance at exercise sessions. The following sectiondescribes instructional strategies in the visual, auditory, and kines-thetic/tactile domains (adapted from McKenzie, 1984). If a partici-pant has a weak sensory channel‚Äîvisual, auditory, or kinesthetic/tactile‚Äîthen a multi-sensory approach is warranted. Thisapproach makes use of all of the senses to promote and reinforcethe learning of knowledge or skills.
VISUAL DOMAIN:
# The Client Learns Best By Watching
If a client learns best by watching, try these methods:
1. Demonstrate a skill whenever possible.
2. Tell the client what to look for in your
demonstrations.
3. When multiple individuals are present, be sure
everyone in the group can see you.
4. Maintain eye contact when speaking to the client.5. Use diagrams and videos.6. Use visual cues to enhance body awareness and
coordinated movement (e.g., footprints placed on
the floor, stickers placed on the body or equip-ment, pointing to a body part, arrows or lines onthe floor).
Effective Teaching
# Unit 8 97
AUDITORY DOMAIN:
# The Client Learns Best By Hearing
If a client learns best by listening, try these methods:
1. Whenever possible, provide the client with facts
or background information regarding exercises or procedures.
2. Verbally direct the individual to perform a task.
3. Allow individuals with hearing impairments to see
your mouth and hand gestures. Do not over exag-
gerate mouth movements.
4. Provide corrective skill feedback‚Äîthat is, informa-
tion regarding how to correct an inadequate per-formance. Information about results leads toincreased learning.
5. Provide positive skill feedback‚Äîthat is, tell the
client what they did correctly to reinforce the likeli-hood of the skill‚Äôs being correctly performed again.
6. Ask questions regarding discomfort during exercis-
es (e.g., pain during passive range-of-motion, painduring cardiovascular training). This informationalerts the trainer to signs of exercise distress andprovides feedback regarding his or her technique.The trainer should also ask questions regardingexercises or procedures for the purpose of testingthe participant‚Äôs knowledge.
7. Listen to the client‚Äôs questions, responses, or
attempts at conversation.
8. Utilize praise, vocal intonation, claps, gestures, and
expressions to activate or intensify motor perform-ances or foster appropriate behavior.
9. Use simple verbal cues to assist the individual in
better visualizing the movement.
10. Remember that a person cannot talk and listen at
the same time. A good teacher listens with bothears and eyes.98Senior Fitness
KINESTHETIC/TACTILE DOMAIN:
# The Client Learns Best By Doing
If a client learns best by the hands-on approach, try these methods.
Remember, though, always ask permission before touching a clientas part of instruction.
1. Manually guide a participant with apraxia (visual
impairment) through the desired motions.
2. Physically assist a participant whenever needed
(e.g., manual assistance should enable a participant
to reach full range of motion).
3. Tap the body part to facilitate movement in the
correct direction.
T o be a more effective trainer, utilize this information while teach-
ing your older client new skills. Y our attention to detail will berewarded in the form of attentive clients and a reputation for gen-uine care regarding the progress of your older clientele.
Effective Teaching
# Unit 8 99

# Behaviors Of Effective Teachers
An effective teacher holds and projects high expectations for his or
her students‚Äô success. If you believe that your clients can makeprogress and improve their quality of life, then they will come toshare those beliefs. Maximize opportunities for participants toengage in learning experiences. Learning experiences help solidifywhat you have taught them during your training; such experiencesmake plain information come to life and seem relevant.
Manage time and organize sessions
efficiently. Do your research andhomework on your own time. Meetyour client with answers to ques-tions raised during the last session.Pace the information you choose toshare in order to maximize partici-pant success. Set realistic goalsabout how long it will take you towork with a client. Work towardmastery of knowledge and skills bysystematically monitoring clientprogress and providing feedback.Be well prepared‚Äîknow the sub-ject, know the participant, and know the appropriate teaching style.Engage in active teaching with all participants whether individualsor groups of varying size. Maintain a high level of energy for eachclient regardless of how many times you may have presented thesame information that day. Clients are sensitive to differences in theenergy levels of their instructors.
Provide a supportive learning environment characterized by warmth
and personal support. Select equipment and activities that are appro-priate for each client‚Äôs development level. Remember that yourclient‚Äôs self-esteem is more important than any exercise or activity.Think of what clients can do, rather than what they cannot do. 100Senior Fitness

# Professional Conduct
As a trainer, it is imperative that you always conduct yourself in a
professional manner. Observing the following general rules ofbehavior will help you do this.
1. Be punctual.
2. Use appropriate language and terminology, and
be prepared to answer client questions.
3. Refrain from unnecessary chatting with fellow
trainers.
4. Give your full attention to your client.5. Wear appropriate clothing.6. Attune yourself to the older client‚Äôs interests and
motivating factors. (These can differ greatly from
younger generations.)
7. Maintain client confidentiality at all times.
8. If you must touch a person, ask permission, and
keep it appropriate.
We have covered a number of different aspects of effective teaching.
The following list summarizes some of these things‚Äîkey elementsin helping you enhance the education and motivation of your olderadult clients.
1. Give each participant a clear exercise objective for
each training session.
2. Provide concise exercise instructions and precise
exercise demonstrations.
3. Attentively supervise clients as they train.
4. Appropriately assist participants when necessary,
especially when trying new exercises.
Effective Teaching
# Unit 8 101
5. Focus on one task at a time.
6. Progress gradually from simple to more complex
exercise concepts and requirements.
7. Be generous with positive reinforcement, and
let individuals know their exercise efforts are
appreciated.
8. Provide specific feedback on exercise performance
to facilitate the learning process and correct errors.
9. Promote relevant communication by carefully and
frequently asking questions.
10. Welcome participants to the exercise session,
and thank them for successfully completing their workout.
11. To get maximum results from your students try the
sandwich approach: put a little positive reinforce-ment on the bottom, add corrective feedback inthe middle, and finish with a layer of positiveencouragement on the top.102Senior Fitness

# Getting The Older Adult Started
Sensible and regular exercise is an investment for life. Rehabilitation
specialists have a term called ‚Äúfunctional fitness.‚Äù They want us tokeep our legs and body fit so we can function as fully as possible foras long as possible.
Recently in Healthy People 2010, the United States Department of
Health and Human Services suggested the leading factor contribut-ing to premature disability and death is the sedentary life stylemany Americans live. Some older adults use injury, impairment, ordisability as a reason not to exercise.
One key to avoiding this situation is to take extra care to avoid
injury at the outset. Many people use outdated training techniquesor ill-fitted equipment, or they push themselves beyond reasonablelimits. These things can contribute to exercise-induced injuries andthen to stopping exercising altogether. Don‚Äôt let your clients becomepart of the dropout statistics. Encourage them to choose a sport oractivity that fits their body type and interests. If it requires specialequipment for safety or to play, they should make sure the equip-ment works and fits properly. Also explain the concept of crosstraining by mixing up activities. Whatever activity individualschoose, they should have fun doing it. If they can go outside, sug-gest that they try walking or resistance training outdoors.
Many individuals suffer from some type of impairment. How they
deal with that impairment will determine whether or not itbecomes a disability. An impairment is a pathological disorder suchas arthritis. If the impairment isn‚Äôt addressed, it can lead to a func-tional limitation that causes a dysfunction, such as not being able towalk. If the impairment continues to cause the limitation, theimpairment can become a full-blown handicap.
One common problem affecting older individuals is some sort of
limiting back disability. Specialists are now recommending trainingthe core muscles of the body‚Äîthe trunk‚Äîto help with this. As the
Effective Teaching
# Unit 8 103
core muscles get stronger, they can
protect the spine from stressfulmovements. The primary core mus-cles that need to be strengthened toprotect the spine are: the abdomi-nals, the obliques, the spinal exten-sors, and the trapezius. Other keymuscles that play a significant rolein controlling the pelvic region are
the gluteus maximus, hamstrings, quadriceps, hip flexors, and hipabductors. T o stay pain free, it‚Äôs important to work the core musclestogether to develop a well-balanced spine.
Some older clients will be very fit and not need rehabilitation. With
other individuals, though, medical care will be needed. In thesecases a combination of therapy and fitness is now being suggested.Advise your clients not to quit exercising because of an injury. First,they should get a diagnosis and evaluate what caused the injury.Some muscles may be too weak, or some may be too inflexible. Incases such as these, an appropriately geared exercise regimen wouldbe the key to healing as well as to achieving a condition of overallfitness tailored to prevent such injuries in the future.
All of us, but especially older adults, must work to strengthen our
bodies. By strengthening the muscles around the joints, we stabilizeand protect them. Stretching helps, too. Exercise often results inshortened muscles, a condition which makes us prone to muscularstrain injuries. The more time we train, the more time we shouldspend stretching.
Some clients may respond positively to programs consisting of
short, intermittent exercise. Y ou can inform folks in this group thatthree bouts of ten minutes each is equal to thirty minutes of nonstopexercise. For some older adults, knowing this type of work-out is anoption might make the difference between getting and staying fitand the all-too-rapid physical decline that accompanies inactivity.104Senior Fitness

Following are some points you can review with your older clients to
help them get into the frame-of-mind of wanting to maintain theirhealth and fitness for all aspects of life, athletic and otherwise.
1. Set reasonable goals.
2. Weigh yourself monthly. Most older adults suffer
from ‚Äúcreeping obesity‚Äù‚Äîfive extra pounds here
that don‚Äôt go away, followed by another fivepounds there....
3. Make your healthy lifestyle enjoyable. Exercise and
eat healthy foods most of the time, but don‚Äôtworry if you have to miss a workout or choose tohave a piece of cake once in a while.
4. Reward yourself for small accomplishments.
5. Be prepared for a fitness opportunity. Keep exercise
clothes in the car. If your schedule changes, use the
extra time to exercise.
6. Don‚Äôt start doing too much too soon. If you have
been away from fitness for a while, start back atabout one third of what you were doing beforeyou stopped. Don‚Äôt increase by more than 10 per-cent per week.
7. Listen to your body, and heed what it says. Learn
the difference between mild discomfort and pain.
8. Warm-up, train, and cool-down.
# Designing A Workoutfor The Older Adult
Improving your health and fitness does not have to hurt. T oo many
people are afraid to start a fitness program for fear they will hurtthemselves. Others who are fit may develop an injury and start backtoo soon which can lead to the frustration of re-injury. Othersremember how fit they were in their twenties and thirties andbecome discouraged thinking they can‚Äôt ever regain that level of fit-
Effective Teaching
# Unit 8 105
ness. Whatever one‚Äôs mindset, the first few steps toward health and
fitness can be the most difficult as well as the most important.
A comprehensive workout should include a warm-up, a workout, a
cool-down, and stretching, in that order. Begin the warm-up withfive to ten minutes of light cardiovascular work and follow with fiveto ten minutes stretching the shoulder joints, back, groin, and legs.See the suggested exercises later in this course, and choose several toperform during the workout. Choose exercises to address the spe-cific body area you wish to tone and shape. Pick at least two exer-cises for each body part. Vary the workout so it does not becomeboring. Try playing music or watching an exercise program or videowhile working away the calories. Finally, for the cool-down, stretchand perform the same exercises as done when warming up.
Most people know there is no perfect exercise. A comprehensive
therapeutic exercise program should address balance, neuromus-cular coordination, flexibility, cardiovascular fitness, muscularstrength, and endurance. However, if a disability is involved, it isimportant to address those parameters involved in improving106Senior Fitness

functional fitness (getting out of a chair, putting groceries away,
etc.). If you can include exercises that will allow clients to betterperform activities of daily living, you have an exercise programthat can make a difference. Proper exercise can lead to an improvedquality of life.
# Fitness And Stress
Good stress occurs when you try to run a little faster or lift a little
more weight. Bad stress occurs when you have pressure to meet adeadline or have to deal with a difficult situation. Bad stress can alsobe called distress. Distress affects our mental and physical health.This kind of stress is the perception that an event or circumstancewill challenge or exceed a person‚Äôs ability to cope. This might helpexplain why some events, like deadlines, can cause some people towork harder and paralyze others.
Major stressors in life can affect health. Some stress-producing
events include marriage, divorce, and death. Although most peopleagree that major upheavals such as those listed above cause a greatdeal of distress, a study at the University of California at Berkeleyfound that everyday aggravations can actually take the greatest toll.Researchers found the cumulative effects of distress, such as thehassles of too many responsibilities, constant interruptions, toomuch noise, or even trying to learn a new computer program, canfar outweigh the stress of a major event. While stress motivatessome people to work harder, its cumulative effects have been asso-ciated with colds, stomachaches, headaches, high blood pressure,and heart disease.
Exercise and play can help relieve stress and relax you. Some fitness
experts maintain that one exercise session will generate about anhour-and-a-half of relaxation. Try breathing deeply between exer-cises to slow down momentarily. Don‚Äôt forget to laugh and play inaddition to exercising, and encourage your clients to do so as well.
Working out should be fun. Some of us are so compulsive we
become aggravated and experience stress if we miss our normal
Effective Teaching
# Unit 8 107
workout routine because of vacation, an appointment, a visitor, or
some other reason. Remember, though, that having fun with fitnessis key. Among other things, it will keep you committed. Speed-play,intervals, and cross training add diversity to training. Some peopleare content running the same course or doing the same weight andswim routine every day. Other people find they need variety to staymotivated and committed to exercise. Do whatever it takes to keepexercise enjoyable, and you will stick with it and reap its rewards.
Here are some suggestions for varying your routine.
¬ÅIf you‚Äôre walking outside and see a tree in the dis-
tance, pick up your pace until you reach it. Oncethere, use the tree for support and do two sets ofthirty-second heel-cord stretches. Take off foranother tree, and at that tree do squats against thetree, say two sets for thirty seconds. Take off againuntil you find a strong tree that will allow you tohang from a limb and pull yourself up‚Äîat least try!This is a fun way to incorporate stretching andstrengthening into your daily walk. Be creative.
¬ÅIf you‚Äôre on vacation and you‚Äôre a regular swim-mer, you may find those hotel pools are too crowd-ed for a good lap-swim workout. Try going into thedeep end and doing water-running, jumping jacks,or treading water. Follow that workout with push-ups and press-ups at the edge of the pool.
¬ÅIf you‚Äôre at a hotel with no exercise room or equipment, try stairwell walking. Make sure the stairs are well lit. Climb up, then take the elevator back down.
¬ÅAt home, at the office, or in a hotel, use a hallwayto do one-quarter lunges down the wall.
¬ÅDo pushups against a sturdy, bolted park bench.
Remember: safety is key! Make sure unconventional equipment is
safe, and is strong enough and sturdy enough to support you.108Senior Fitness
# The Importance Of Balance
It is important to balance your workout mode. It is also important
to balance your muscle use and development. Many muscular-skeletal problems occur as the result of muscle imbalances. Watch amarathon or any type of running event. Evaluate the posture andphysique of the runners. Some may have well developed muscles intheir legs, but scrawny upper bodies. Check their posture. Manyhave round shoulders and a forward-leaning head. They are train-ing their legs and working out their heart, but their other musclesare out of shape.
Cross-training involves blending various fitness workouts to work
opposing muscle groups. A cross-training workout keeps the par-ticipant from getting bored with his or her workout. It allows dif-ferent muscles to be used to help a person better prepare forparticipating in unaccustomed activities without creating risk forinjury. By cross-training one can avoid overuse injuries.
Try to blend all of the major fitness ingredients into your routine. If
you are a swimmer, try to include weight-bearing activities. If you
Effective Teaching
# Unit 8 109

are a walker, try to combine strength training with your stretching
program to keep you in perfect balance. If you are a runner, stretchand stretch again. Spend time working opposing muscles fromthose that are working when you run.
Consider your physical balance, also. T otal fitness should include
stretching and strengthening, along with cardiovascular fitness andposture work. Mobility and agility training should address balanceskills along with fall-prevention activities.110Senior Fitness

# Unit Summary
I. Different principles affect the way
different individuals learn and the
amount of information they retain.Understanding these principles helpto make the fitness professional bet-ter able to motivate clients.
II. Individuals have preferential learn-
ing styles determined by a domi-nant sensory channel. Byunderstanding these different learn-ing styles, identifying which oneeach client employs, and incorporat-ing this knowledge into the devel-opment of the training session,trainers can enrich the learningexperience of each of their clients.
a. Clients who learn best by watch-
ing will benefit from having skills
demonstrated for them, from theuse of diagrams and videos, andfrom visual cues used to enhancebody awareness and executecoordinated movement.
b. Clients who learn best by listen-
ing benefit from verbal direction,from corrective skill feedback,and from a question-and-answerexchange of information withthe trainer.
c. Clients who learn best through
the hands-on approach will ben-efit from being physically assistedwhen needed and from havingthe body tapped to facilitatemovement in the right direction.III. Effective teachers maximize each
opportunity for their students tolearn and manage time and organizesessions efficiently.
IV. Getting older adults started with a fit-

a. Extra care must be taken to avoid
injury at the outset.
b. Any impairments must be
acknowledged and dealt with
properly so they don‚Äôt turn intofull-blown handicaps.
c. The amount of time spent train-
ing should be balanced by timespent stretching.
V. Exercise can help relieve stress and
promote relaxation. Some expertsmaintain that one exercise sessionwill generate about an hour and ahalf of relaxation. To enjoy the fullstress-reducing benefits of exercise,it is important to remember to havefun and vary the exercise routine.
