VI. Thoughts for Consideration
As a health professional, you can affect behavioral changes that will
improve your clients‚Äô lives. Strength training and cardiorespiratoryexercises are therapeutic modalities you bring to all your clients, butan older clientele may present different motivational challenges.
Using different approaches to behavior modification and under-
standing what motivates the older adult is crucial.
Optimal learning occurs when the teaching style matches the char-
acteristics of the learner. No single teaching style, strategy,approach, or method is a panacea for all instructional situations.Effective teachers learn to adapt their teaching styles to match theirstudents‚Äô various learning styles.
When working with older clients, educators are faced with the chal-
lenge of facilitating learning for a group of individuals who may pos-sess various learning styles as well as age-related disabling conditions.Understanding the learning process in these situations is crucial andmeans the difference between mediocre and effective programs.
# Knowledge As A Motivator
Knowledge is an important factor in intrinsic and extrinsic motiva-tion, yet doctors often fail to give their older clients the same ‚Äúpre-ventative‚Äù information they give to their younger patients. Surveysof primary care physicians and clinicians indicate that only thirty114Senior Fitness

percent provide counseling to their sedentary clients on the bene-
fits of physical activity. Information on how to reduce stress, lowercholesterol, control weight, and exercise is not wasted on an agingpopulation. As we have discussed in previous units, older adults arecapable of significantly improving their health. Older clients willlook to their health and fitness professional as a resource forimproving their knowledge.
Educating your older clients about health and fitness allows them to
understand and participate more completely in the training process.Knowledge can serve to build intrinsic motivation for exercising.Many exercisers have learned that staying on schedule and living ahealthy active lifestyle makes them feel good about themselves, andthat is a reward in itself. Feeling good about their healthy lifestyle,the way they look, and most importantly about the good care theyhave given themselves is something that will help your older clientsand continue to serve as intrinsic motivation for exercise.
Knowledge also can act as an extrinsic motivator. If the doctor tells
a patient his or her blood pressure has gone down since beginningregular exercise, that information serves as an extrinsic motivator tocontinue exercising. Positive reinforcement is a strong motivator,but negative reinforcement also motivates. If the doctor tells apatient that an inactive lifestyle has resulted in cardiovascular dis-ease or elevated cholesterol, that individual may feel compelled toexercise because of fear that the condition may worsen otherwise.
# A Different Era
Another important factor to recognize is that older adults have hadmany years, if not decades, to develop their bad habits; and it maytake some time to replace unhealthy habits with healthy ones. Someof your clients may have never been in a fitness center before begin-ning work with you, and many pieces of the equipment they willuse will be completely foreign to them. Most of your older clientsdid not have access to adequate physical education programs whenthey were younger; for example, some may have gained their only
Motivating the Older Adult
# Unit 9 115
physical training experience while serving in the Armed Forces.
Defining what motivates your clients and taking into considerationpast exposure to health and fitness is very important. Patience, tol-erance, and understanding are invaluable qualities for you as a fit-ness professional working with older adults.
Successful fitness professionals will be good at staying organized,
developing trust with their clientele, and listening. Being organizedinvolves responsibilities like keeping your client‚Äôs progress accurate-ly charted. Developing trust requires that you use safe techniquesthat do not cause injury, that you show up to all appointments ontime, ready to teach and train, and that you keep private informa-tion private. Listening to your clients provides you with sufficientinformation on how to appeal to them and increase their chances ofregular participation in exercise. Combining these qualities willenable you to move your clients closer to optimal health.
# Socialization
Many older adults enjoy working with other people their own age.Being around other older adults makes trying something new feelmore comfortable and safe. Encouraging social interaction is veryimportant and can be beneficial. The Surgeon General‚Äôs report onphysical activity and health states, ‚Äúphysical activity reduces symp-toms of anxiety and depression and fosters improvements in moodand feelings of well being.‚Äù Encourage your older clients to get toknow one another. It creates a support system for them. TheSurgeon General‚Äôs report also says ‚Äúsocial support from family andfriends has been consistently and positively linked to regular phys-ical activity.‚Äù When people start feeling better from their new activeand healthy lifestyle, they may encourage their friends to seek youto set them up with a fitness program.
As mentioned earlier, motivation can be both intrinsic and extrin-
sic. This means the motivation for exercise can come from externalfactors such as compliments, loved ones‚Äô urging, or trophies ofsome kind. Motivation can also come from internal factors: a desire116Senior Fitness
to be healthy, a desire to stay active, or a desire to become better at
a skill. Most people have a bit of both types of motivation that spurthem to achieve their goals.
# Goal Setting
Setting realistic goals is another important factor in motivation. Ifgoals are too lofty and can‚Äôt be met, it will be difficult to use goal-setting effectively in your motivation strategies. Using goals to stim-ulate progress in exercise programs is as effective with a seniorpopulation as it is with any other group. Goals come in differentforms, ranging from short-term to long-term, with each type serv-ing a different purpose. Short-term goals can be used to renew dailyresolve. Encourage your clients to choose an activity they wish toaccomplish each day. Attempting to walk five minutes longer on thetreadmill or eliminating a fatty food from the diet are examples ofshort-term goals. Achieving a goal is very motivating; by perform-ing the activity they set as their daily goal, individuals reinforce apositive behavioral trend. The healthy trend that develops is bene-ficial on the road to achieving long-term goals as well.
Motivating the Older Adult
# Unit 9 117

Long-term goals are often comprised of a number of short-term or
even mid-term goals. Usually six months to a year or more is con-sidered the time frame for a long-term goal. Within that time, yourclients may achieve many small victories on the road to accomplish-ing their long-term goals. Losing a significant amount of weight ordeveloping enough strength to trade in the walker for a cane areexamples of long-term goals. If a client is properly motivated andencouraged by setting and achieving goals, that client may make apermanent change that will improve his or her quality of life.
# Goal Orientation
Goal orientation is a key element in establishing strong motivation.Goal-orientation not only shows you where you are going, it alsoshows you how to get there. Beware, however, of allowing your goalsto impose limits on your clients‚Äô performance by making them‚Äúfinal destinations.‚Äù Instead, look at your goals as ‚Äústepping-stones‚Äùthat will put you in position to access your next stepping-stone! Inother words, the goal is not an end, but a means to another goal.Excellence is an ongoing process for the passionate, committedtrainer.
A goal can be seen as the bridge between wanting to achieve some-
thing and actually achieving it. The following conditions must bepresent in order for a sincere desire to be considered a goal.
‚Ä¢ A goal must be well defined. Vague wishes or
desires, such as ‚ÄúI want to be a great baseball play-
er‚Äù or ‚ÄúI want to become thinner‚Äù just don‚Äôt cut it.Instead, ‚ÄúI want to make the little league team thisyear‚Äù or ‚ÄúI want to weigh 165‚Äù are better defined,and as such, are more attainable.
‚Ä¢ A goal must be stated in writing. Busy schedules
and the various complexities of life have a way ofturning sincere desires into distant memories. Goalsmust be written down and put in a place wherethey will be seen often.
‚Ä¢ A goal must be stated in the positive. The subcon-
scious mind does not understand negatively stated118Senior Fitness
goals, so always use a positive frame of reference in
constructing your goals. Not ‚ÄúI won‚Äôt eat junk foodany more‚Äù but ‚ÄúI will eat healthy foods each day.‚ÄùBetter yet, ‚ÄúI enjoy eating healthy foods each day.‚ÄùNow the goal is an affirmation, which can be usedto precipitate action.
‚Ä¢ A goal must have a deadline for its completion. In
order to get really excited about a goal, there mustbe an expected time of completion. Otherwise,there will be no sense of urgency, and before youknow it, you are right back where you started, nocloser to your goal. Of course, unexpected prob-lems and circumstances often arise, and when thishappens, simply adjust the completion dateaccordingly.
‚Ä¢ A goal must have sincere emotional appeal. If it
does not, there will be no urgency or passion.Learn to prioritize the most important goals, andmake the distinction between a mere wish and animportant objective.
‚Ä¢ A goal must be difficult, yet realistic. If the goal is
too easy, e.g., ‚ÄúI will go to the gym tomorrow,‚Äù itwill be very unlikely that enough attention will befocused on it to get it done. Conversely, if it is toodifficult, the confidence of knowing it can be donewill be lacking. Set goals in such a way that with asustained, concentrated effort, you know you canget the job done.
# Excuses Or Barriers To Exercise
Older people give many reasons for not engaging in exercise. Some
of the reasons discussed in the book, Biomarkers , include embar-
rassment, humiliation, self-deprecation, and self-doubt. Whateverthe reason, it is important to get to the root of it so that you cancounter with better reasons to give exercise a chance.
Embarrassment and humiliation can stem from entering into unfa-
miliar territory. Entering a gym at age seventy and not knowinghow to properly use any of the equipment makes some people feel
Motivating the Older Adult
# Unit 9 119
inadequate. Many clients share a feeling that they are in a ‚Äúfish
bowl,‚Äù being watched by everyone in the facility. The awkward feel-ing of not knowing how to use the machines, coupled with thesense of being onstage, can be a strong deterrent. Remind yourclients that you will guide them through the training exercises andthat everyone else they see started at the beginning just as they arenow. Some clients may be embarrassed about being so ‚Äúout ofshape.‚Äù Assure your clients that within a relatively short period oftime, they will not be breathing as heavily or feeling so run downafter only a few minutes of exercise. T ell your clients that regularparticipation will not only get them through their training sessionsmore easily, but also will help them get through their daily activitieswith greater ease. Nothing succeeds like success! Build some ele-ment of success into every workout for your client.
Some older adults may make self-deprecating remarks to the effect
that they feel silly beginning an exercise program at such anadvanced age. Use this opportunity to praise your older clients forhaving the courage and wisdom to start taking better care of them-selves through exercise. Help them realize that it would be counter-productive for them not to partake in activities that can improvetheir quality of life.
Y ou may also find that some older adults experience self-doubt
because the social group they associate with does not support theirinterest in exercise. Encourage these clients to make new friendswhere they participate in the fitness program. Their old friends willstill be there to socialize with when they are finished exercising.Many times the increase in energy and feelings of well-being exud-ed by your client will spark an interest among their old friends tojoin in. Soon your clients may find themselves exercising with oldfriends and new.
Whatever the motivator may be, communication with your older
clients will provide insight into what will most help them. If youtake the time to get to know your clients, you will prolong the120Senior Fitness
Motivating the Older Adult
# Unit 9 121
amount of time they spend training with you. And the more time
they spend training, the greater the results they will achieve!
# Thoughts For Consideration
‚Ä¢ Exercise is a journey, not a destination! Enjoy
the journey!
‚Ä¢ If you always do what you‚Äôve always done, you‚Äôll
always get what you‚Äôve always gotten.
‚Ä¢ If not now, when?
‚Ä¢ It is never too late to feel great.‚Ä¢ Everything that gets worse with age is positively
influenced with exercise.
‚Ä¢ Growing older is a privilege not granted to everyone.

# Basic Assessment
Every exercise program should begin with an assessment of your
client‚Äôs physical fitness; unfortunately this process is often neglect -
ed. Each physical assessment should at least include the parameters of muscular strength and endurance, flexibility and cardiovascular endurance. Assessments provide a baseline from which to evaluate your progress. Assessments allow you to note the areas that need improvement, and from that information, develop a personalized program based on what is best for your client. Assessments also give you an opportunity to discuss what the results mean with your
client. This is a great time to reiterate the importance of physical fit -
ness and how improvements in test scores reflect on improvements
in health.
Assessment is the first step in developing an exercise program,
but be aware that not all assessment tools are suitable for all cli -
ents. Assessment should be done during the course of your initial consultation. You should discuss in depth any medical or chronic conditions that your client presents. This is also the appropriate time to request medical clearance from the client‚Äôs physician and, if necessary, inquire about parameters to work within for more seri -
ous conditions. If it is impossible for you to speak directly with the physician regarding your client‚Äôs medical condition, send a form to the doctor to complete and send back to you, or have the client take it to the doctor and bring it back to you. You may create your own or use the example provided. The form should include the doctor‚Äôs recommendation for exercise, any activities or exercises that would be contraindicated (and the reason why), and any other pertinent information relevant to designing a safe and effective training pro -
gram. This form should be completed in addition to the medical clearance release form.
Once the client returns the form to you, be sure to go over it with
him/her thoroughly. This will ensure that you are very familiar 126Senior Fitness

with the medical/health history of the client and also give you a
chance to discuss options. Once certified, these forms are available to you for download at issatrainer.com.
Once you reach the decision to have your client start exercising, his/
her physician may recommend a graded exercise test (GXT) before you have him/her exercising. This procedure commonly referred to as an exercise stress test. Such a recommendation from one‚Äôs phy -
sician may raise a number of questions in your mind regarding a GXT: What does a GXT involve? Why should your client undergo a GXT? Is it safe? What information does it provide? Understanding the answers to such questions and other relevant issues concerning graded exercise testing may help to alleviate much of the confusion and mystery that frequently exists concerning this increasingly utilized evaluative procedure. Certainly if your client has never engaged in an aerobic exercise program before, a better under -
standing of what an exercise stress test involves may lessen the likelihood of your client experiencing undue anxiety.
# What A Gxt Involves
A GXT can be administered in a variety of locations, including a physician‚Äôs office, a hospital, and a health and fitness facility. Regardless of where a GXT is administered, special equipment to handle any medical emergency that may arise and a sufficient number of appropriately trained professionals must be present. According to the guidelines established by the American Heart Association (AHA) and adopted by ISSA, all individuals who administer GXTs should be trained in cardiopulmonary resusci -
tation (CPR), and at least one of the technicians in the testing area should also be trained and certified in advanced cardiac life sup -
port. Also, in accordance with AHA guidelines, a physician should be on the premises (e.g., in the building) at all times.
Prior to the start of a GXT, a resting electrocardiogram (EKG)
is performed to check for cardiac abnormalities that exist in the absence of ‚Äústress‚Äù and to provide a basis for comparison with your physiological responses during the exercise session. Both resting Basic Assessment
# Unit 10 127
blood pressure and heart rate are assessed and monitored through -
out a GXT. Electrodes (sensors) are placed on the chest during a
resting EKG. These sensors remain in place during a GXT so that the rhythm and electrical activity of the heart can be monitored continuously throughout the GXT. At rest, an EKG may indicate that the heart is receiving adequate amounts of blood and oxygen, but when the body is stressed (i.e., during exercise), an EKG may reveal signs that your heart is receiving insufficient amounts of blood and oxygen.
A variety of modalities (e.g., treadmill, stationary cycle, stair climb -
ing machine, etc.) can be used to provide the exercise stress. The
motor-driven treadmill is the device most commonly used in the United States for graded exercise testing. In general, a GXT initially involves placing a relatively low level of exercise-induced stress on the cardiovascular system. Subsequent increases in work demand are then gradually incorporated into the exercise protocol the client is required to perform by raising either the speed or grade (incline) of the treadmill. Because the goal of a GXT is simply to determine the capability limits of the cardiovascular system, you need not worry about the treadmill reaching ‚Äúwarp‚Äù speed or being raised to a grade that simulates Mount Everest.
Two different types of GXTs can be administered. One is referred
to as a symptom-limited maximal GXT and the other as a submax -
imal GXT. The symptom-limited maximal GXT is the more com -
mon of the two types of GXTs and involves progressively increasing the intensity of the exercise until signs or symptoms of exertion intolerance are displayed. The submaximal GXT, on the other hand, progressively increases the level of exercise intensity until a predetermined end point (e.g., 85% of your maximum heart rate) is reached or until a sign or symptom of exertion intolerance occurs (before the end point has been reached).
Who should have a Stress Test?
The current guidelines of the AHA (for both male and female) indi -
cate that individuals under age 40 who undergo a normal physical examination, which indicates no symptoms of cardiovascular dis -128Senior Fitness
ease, no coronary risk factors, no physical findings (including mur -
murs and hypertension), they can be considered free of disease and
do not require a GXT before undertaking a new exercise regimen. On the other hand, if you have a client 40 years of age or older with an abnormal physical examination (murmur, etc.) or two or more coronary risk factors, he/she should have a GXT before embarking on a vigorous exercise program.
In guidelines recently revised by the American College of Sports
Medicine (and subsequently adopted by ISSA), a GXT is not rec -
ommended prior to initiating an exercise program if your client is an asymptomatic male 40 years or younger or female 50 years or younger. If your client is an older asymptomatic adult (males 40-plus, females 50-plus), you may have him/her begin a moderate exercise regimen (intensity 40% to 60% maximum oxygen uptake capacity, or VO2 max) without a GXT. A GXT is
recommended for all clients for whom you are planning a vigorous exercise program (intensity greater than 60% VO2 max).
The ACSM and ISSA use the same cri -
teria of two or more primary risk fac -
tors as the basis for recommending a GXT prior to an individual beginning an exercise program. Moderate exer -
cise is, however, permitted without a GXT for individuals without coronary artery disease (CAD) symptoms but with two or more CAD risk factors. Individuals with two or more risk factors, but without symptoms, must undergo a GXT only before vigorous exercise.
How Safe is a GXT?
Like most medical procedures, a GXT is not without some risk. The risk can be minimized by proper screening and by having the test administered by properly trained personnel. As stated previously, Basic Assessment
# Unit 10 129
Your 40+ male and 50+ female cli-
ents need a GXT if they also have one of the following:
 ‚Ä¢ physical findings of cardiovascular
problems (murmur, hypertension, etc.)
 ‚Ä¢ symptoms of coronary artery or  cardiovascular disease
 ‚Ä¢ two or more coronary risk factors
 ‚Ä¢ exercising above 60% VO 2 max
personnel involved in conducting a GXT should be familiar with
the equipment and trained to handle emergency situations should they arise. Although unfavorable events (e.g., heart attacks) are well publicized, they are extremely rare (less than one per every 500 symptom-limited maximal GXTs). The risk of dying is much lower (less than 1 in 10,000).
What Information Does a GXT Provide?
In the early stages of CAD, individuals usually do not exhibit symptoms of coronary insufficiency while either at rest or during non-strenuous activity. During exercise, however, signs or symp -130Senior Fitness

Basic Assessment
# Unit 10 131
toms of CAD often become evident, as the demands for oxygen
increase beyond what the diseased arteries can deliver. If the oxygen demand and supply imbalance is relatively large, it can cause angi -
nal chest pain‚Äîa hallmark sign of CAD. Smaller oxygen imbalanc -
es can cause changes in heart rate, blood pressure and heart rhythm and may elicit changes on the EKG. An abnormal EKG during a GXT is only suggestive that a cardiac problem is present since the results of such tests are not always accurate. Approximately 10% to 20% of GXTs result in false positives (i.e., the test erroneously indi -
cates that an individual has heart disease). Unfortunately, a higher proportion (20% to 40%) of false negative (i.e., the test incorrectly indicates that an individual is free of heart disease) GXT results occur. Accordingly, when evaluating test results, the physician must consider all of the following: how long and through what stage the individual exercised, what your heart rate and blood pressure responses, whether any rhythm disturbances of heart occurred and when they occurred, whether any EKG abnormalities appeared, and how he/she looked and felt.
After the GXT
Once your client has taken a GXT, you should discuss the results with his or her doctor. If no problems are discovered, the infor -
mation provided by the GXT should then be used to design an appropriate exercise program for your client‚Äôs specific goals, needs, interests and fitness level. Should your client‚Äôs physician feel that his/her stress test was not accurate or was inconclusive, he or she may recommend a thallium exercise stress test in order to improve the diagnostic accuracy of the evaluative procedure.
Remember, it is impossible to ‚Äúflunk‚Äù a GXT. If a problem is detect -
ed, it is much better to identify it in the controlled setting of a phy -
sician‚Äôs office, a hospital, an exercise science laboratory or a health
and fitness club than in your client‚Äôs own home. Finally, no matter who you are, or who your client is, both of you can benefit from participating in an exercise program based on the information obtained from a properly conducted GXT.
par QPhysical Activity Readiness Questionnaire
A Questionnaire for People Aged 15 to 69
Regular physical activity is fun and healthy, and increasingly more people are starting to become more active every day. Being more active is very
safe for most people. However, some people should check with their doctor before they start becoming much more physically active.
If you are planning to become much more physically active than you are now, start by answering the seven questions in the box below. If you
are between the ages of 15 and 69, the Physical Activity Readiness Questionnaire (PAR-Q) will tell you if you should check with your doctorbefore you start. If you are over 69 years of age and you are not used to being very active, check with your doctor.
Common sense is your best guide when you answer these questions. Please read the questions carefully and answer each one honestly: check
YES or NO.

INTENSITY OF EXERCISE:
# Target Heart Rate
The purpose of the target heart rate (THR) is to establish the
intensity of exercise necessary to produce a training effect on the cardiovascular system. Let‚Äôs use the Karvonen formula, which takes resting heart rate (HR
rest) into account.
Karvonen Formula for Target Heart Rate
Instructions Example
1. Start with 220*  220  220
2. Subtract age ‚Äì age ‚Äì 63
New number is the predicted age-adjusted maximum  157
3. Subtract resting heart rate (HR rest) ‚Äì HRrest ‚Äì 60
New number is the heart rate reserve (HRR)  97
4. Multiply by exercise intensity of 60-80% x 0.70 x 0.70
-Round to a whole number if needed-  68
5. Add resting heart rate back in + HRrest + 60
Target heart rate (beats/minute)  128  128
*If doing arm cycling, start with 210
Note: The maximal heart rate averages about ten beats lower for arm work than for leg work. Adjust your target heart rate for arm-crank ergometry by starting with 210.
Basic Assessment
# Unit 10 139
How to Take a Pulse
 1. Take a pulse at the thumb side of the wrist on
the palm or inner side of the forearm. Place your fingers at the area near the inside of the wrist and you will feel a throb there. Do not use your thumb because it has its own pulse. Do not take a pulse from the carotid arteries in the neck.
 2. Look at the second hand of a clock and count how many beats you feel in ten seconds. Multiply this number by six to obtain the pulse for one minute. If the pulse is weak and irregular, count for a full minute.
If an individual takes certain heart or blood pressure medications
(i.e., beta-blockers), the target rate during exercise may be lower than these calculations. Thus, you must rely on a system known as ‚ÄúPerceived Exertion.‚Äù This is called the Borg Scale. You rate how you feel during your exercise program. An overall feeling of effort is rated on a scale of 6 to 20. For a good cardiorespiratory training effect, aim for ‚Äúsomewhat hard.‚Äù
Borg Scale and Perceived Exertion
Borg Rating Perceived Exertion Examples (for most adults <65 years old)
6 None Reading a book, watching television
 7 to 8 Very, very light Tying shoes
 9 to 10 Very light Chores that seem to take little effort (e.g., folding clothes)
11 to 12 Fairly light Walking through the grocery store or other activities that require
some effort but not enough to speed up your breathing
13 to 14 Somewhat hard Brisk walking or other activities that require moderate effort. Heart rate and breathing rate increase, but you are not out of breath
15 to 16  Hard Bicycling, swimming, or other activities that take vigorous effort and get the heart pounding and make breathing very fast
17 to 18  Very hard The highest level of activity you can sustain
19 to 20  Very, very hard A finishing kick in a race or other burst of activity that you can‚Äôt maintain for long
Borg G.A. Psychophysical bases of perceived exertion. Medicine and Science in Sports and Exercise. 1982; 14:377-381.
140Senior Fitness
An even simpler way to determine exercise intensity is called the
‚Äútalk test.‚Äù If you are able to carry on a conversation while you are exercising, you are not working too hard. If you become out of breath and unable to talk, this is an indication for you to slow down.
Special Note for Clients with Spinal Cord Injuries
Due to disturbances in the sympathetic nervous system and partial paralysis of the arm and shoulder musculature, persons with spi -
nal cord lesions above thoracic nerve root 6 cannot use the target heart rate formula described above because of deficient exercise tachycardia: the heart rate reaches a ceiling between approximately 100 to 130 beats/minute. Therefore, the individual with this type of Moderate Activities:
Clients who have been inactive for a long time will need to work up to these activities gradually.Vigorous Activities:  Clients who have been inactive for a long time or those with health risks should not start with these activities.

# Unit 10 141
disability should not attempt to achieve his or her ceiling heart rate
during training bouts. Also, clients on beta blockers and pacemak -
ers should not rely on heart rates.
# Progressing
When your client is ready to progress, build up the amount of time spent doing endurance activities first; build up the difficulty of the activities later. For example, gradually increase the duration of endurance activities to thirty minutes over several days to weeks (or even months, depending on the individual‚Äôs condition) by walking longer distances, then walking up hills or walking more briskly.
A small increase in fitness can be all it takes to improve the ability
to do things like get up from a chair or climb stairs. Muscles are active even while we sleep. Cells are still doing the routine activities they need to do to stay alive. This work is called metabolism, and it uses up calories that can help keep your weight in check, even when you are asleep!

# Using Met Equivalents To
# Measure Exercise Intensity
All physical activities can be classified on the basis of their oxy -
gen requirements. The amount of oxygen your body consumes is
directly proportional to the energy you expend during the activity. At rest, your body consumes approximately 3.5 ml of oxygen per kilogram of body weight per minute. The resting level of oxygen consumption is referred to as l.0 MET. Thus, an eight-MET level would equal eight times the amount of oxygen you use at rest.
Although the MET method can be used for prescribing exercise
intensity, it has two serious limitations. First, various environmental factors such as heat, humidity, cold, wind, altitude, pollution, differ -
ences in terrain, etc., can change the way your cardiovascular system responds to a given MET level. As a result, your cardiovascular sys -
tem may be working harder at the same MET level. Also, as your level of fitness improves, different MET levels are required to ensure that the training stimulus is sufficient to produce continued improve -
ments. For these reasons, compared to MET equivalents, both THR and RPE are more commonly used to indicate exercise intensity. 142Senior Fitness

NO PAIN, NO GAIN . . . NO WAY!
The old adage‚Äî‚Äúno pain, no gain‚Äù‚Äîwhen applied to exercise‚Äîhas
often led more than one unsuspecting individual to attempt to do too much. In fact, a strong argument could be made that ‚Äúno pain, no gain equals no sense.‚Äù It is in your best interest to keep in mind that exercise does not have to ‚Äúhurt‚Äù to produce improvements in fitness level. Whether someone is new to the ‚Äújoys‚Äù of exercise or a profound fitness enthusiast, using the THR or RPE methods will make work -
outs more effective and more enjoyable.
# Senior Fitness Test
The following assessments are from ‚ÄúMeasuring Functional Fitness of Older Adults,‚Äù The Journal on Active Aging (2002): 24-30 and ‚ÄúAbout the functional fitness test,‚Äù Interactive Health Partner, Center for Successful Aging. Tests are reproduced with permission from authors Roberta Rikli, PhD and Jessie Jones, PhD.
Assessments and Equipment Needed
1. Chair Stand
Measures: lower body strength

Equipment: chair without arms, stopwatch
2. Arm Curl Measures: upper body strength

Equipment: 5-lb (women) and 8-lb (men) weight, chair without arms, stopwatch
3.
 Two-Minute Step Test Measures: physical stamina/endurance

Equipment: stopwatch, measuring tape, visible tape
4. Chair Sit and Reach Measures: lower body (hamstring) flexibility

Equipment: chair, ruler
5. Up and Go Measures: speed, agility, balance

Equipment: chair, cone (or tape), stopwatch
6. Back Scratch Test (aka Zipper Test) Measures: upper body flexibility

Equipment: ruler144Senior Fitness
Chair Stand Test
Place the chair against a wall for stability. Have your client sit in
the middle of the chair, feet flat on the floor, with arms crossed at the wrists and placed against their chest. You will time them for 30 seconds and count the number of full stands  they can complete.
A full stand is counted if the client:
‚Ä¢
 Doe
s not push off of thighs, the seat of the chair, or using any
other assistance.
‚Ä¢ Ke
eps arms against chest and does not allow them to swing up
upon rising.
Chair Stand: Norms and Standards

Two-Minute Step Test
This is a variation of the step test given in the text book. You will
not need a 12‚Äù step for this test.
To begin, measure the midway point between your client‚Äôs knee cap and iliac crest. Then, measure from the floor to the midway point. Mark this distance on the wall (chalk or tape work well). This will be the measure you will use to count the client‚Äôs steps. Each knee must be lifted to this level.
You will time your client for 2 minutes and count the number of
full steps they can complete.
A full step cycle is counted:
‚Ä¢
 Wh
en both the right foot and left foot have lifted off from and
returned to the floor. Count one step cycle when the right knee
is lifted.
‚Ä¢ If bo
th knees reach the appropriate height, as marked on the
wall.
Your client may rest as needed during this assessment.

Chair Sit and Reach
Place a chair against a wall for stability. Your client will sit down
and move forward in the chair until he/she is able to straighten one leg. The other foot should be flat on the ground. Your client will stack one hand on top of the other and try two practice reaches on each side. Your measurements will be taken on the side that is most flexible.
The stretch must be held for 2 seconds. No bouncing or jerking. No
grabbing the leg to hold the stretch.
To measure the stretch, place a ruler across the client‚Äôs toe at the 6‚Äù
mark with the zero end being closest to their body. The 6‚Äù mark will be your zero measurement. When the client reaches forward, you will mark the furthest point they reached on the ruler.
Examples: ‚Ä¢
 If t
hey reach to 3‚Äù, this is a -3.0 score because it is before the toe.
‚Ä¢ If t
hey reach to 10‚Äù, this is a +4.0 score because it is after the toe.

Up and Go
Place a chair against a wall for stability. Your client will sit down
with both feet flat on the floor and hands on their lap. Place a marker (a cone or piece of brightly colored tape) on the floor, 8 feet in front of the chair with a straight and direct path from the chair to the marker.
To score this test you will use a stop watch to time how long it takes
your client to get up from the chair, walk to and around the marker and sit back down in the chair.
You will score to the nearest 0.1 (one tenth) of a second.

Back Scratch Test
Your client will get two practice stretches on each side before you
score this assessment. Score the side that is most flexible. Record the best score of two stretches. The client will raise their left arm straight up in the air above the left shoulder and bend the elbow, reaching the finger tips toward the middle of their shoulder blades. The right arm will then be extended down, with the palm out. Bend the elbow and reach the fingers up to touch the fingers of the left hand. Finger tips just touching is a score of ‚Äú0‚Äù. A positive score is recorded if fingers overlap. A negative score is recorded if the fin -
gers do not touch.
Score to the nearest 0.5 (half) inch.

# Unit Summary
 I. Every exercise program should begin with an assess-
ment of your client‚Äôs physical fitness level. Assessment should be done during the course of your initial con
-
sultation to review in depth any medical or chronic conditions that your client presents. Request medical clearance if necessary and inquire about work parame-ters for more serious conditions.
A.
 Once you reach a decision to have your client start
exercising, his/her physician may recommend that he/she undergo a graded exercise test (GXT) before beginning an exercise regimen.
1.
 There are two types of GXTs: symptom-limited
maximal GXT and submaximal GXT.
 a. Symptom-limited: increase in exercise intensity until signs or symptoms of exertion intolerance occur
 b. Submaximal: increase of exercise intensity until a prede-termined end point or until a sign or symptom of exer
-
tion intolerance
B. Several techniques exist for the monitoring of acceptable levels of exercise intensity. The most widely used methods are: target heart rate (THR), rating of perceived exertion (RPE) and metabolic equivalent units (METS).
 1. THR range = maximal HR x 0.70 and 0.85
a.  70% and 85% of maximal heart rate is equal to approxi-
mately 60% and 80% of functional capacity VO 2 max.
2. Rating of perceived exertion (RPE) refers to the
physical strain individuals believe they are experi-encing while exercising.
 a. RPE is often one of the first readily recognizable measures
of (positive) changes in aerobic fitness.
 3. The resting level of oxygen consumption is
referred to as l.0 MET.
 a. At rest, your body consumes approximately 3.5 ml of
oxygen per kilogram of body weight per minute.
Basic Assessment
# Unit 10 151
# Unit Summary
C. The Senior Fitness Test, developed by Dr. Roberta Rikli
and Dr. Jessie Jones, is an easy-to-use battery of test items that assesses fitness levels of older adults. The Senior Fitness Test consists of six assessments used to measure aerobic fitness, strength, and flexibility. The six assessments; the chair stand test, arm curl test, 2-minute step in place test, chair sit and reach test, 8-foot up and go test, and back scratch test were developed to be safe and enjoyable for older adults, while still meeting scien-tific standards for reliability and validity.
# Cardiovascular Fitness
AND THE OLDER ADULT UNIT 11Senior Fitness
Cardiovascular Fitness and the Older Adult
# Unit 11 153
# Learning Objectivesunit Outline
 I. Frequency of Exercise
 II. Safety Considerations
 III. Cardiovascular Exercises
 a. Walking
 b. Swimming
 c. Bicycling
 d. Rehab Trainers
After completing this unit you will be able to:
 ‚Ä¢ Institute a cardiovascular training program
for older adults
 ‚Ä¢ Make recommendations for frequency, intensity, and safety in the cardiovascular training of older adults
The cardiovascular system includes the heart, lungs, and vascular
system, which are critical to a good quality of life. Part of the disuse syndrome that occurs when an individual is immobilized is the loss of cardiovascular endurance. This reduction in aerobic capacity has many health implications in regard to obesity and heart disease. Regular, moderate physical activity may aid efforts to control ciga-rette smoking, hypertension, lipid abnormalities, diabetes, obesity, and emotional stress. This unit provides some guidelines for insti-tuting a cardiovascular training program with the special needs of older adults in mind.
Cardiovascular exercise is one of the most important components
of physical fitness. Not only does it help increase the ability of the circulatory system to carry oxygen and necessary nutrients to every cell of the body more effectively, but it also helps prevent heart disease. Since heart disease is the leading cause of death in this country, cardiovascular exercise is a preventive measure everyone, including the older adult, should participate in regularly.
Cardiovascular exercises are those exercises that require the body‚Äôs
circulatory system to work harder to meet the demands of the exercise. For example, when one climbs a flight of stairs the heart will begin to beat faster, and breathing will become deeper and faster. These are cardiovascular adjustments necessary to transport more oxygen throughout the body in order to meet the demands of climbing a flight of stairs. If you were to climb consecutive flights of stairs without stopping and you were to do this for approximately twenty minutes, this would then be considered aerobic exercise. Aerobic exercise requires oxygen and sustained effort.
Aerobic exercises are those types of activities that are long, slow,
and continuous, and require the use of large muscle groups (for example, walking or swimming). In order for the cardiovascular system (heart, lungs, arteries, veins, etc.) to benefit from cardio-vascular exercise, it is important to maintain an exercise intensity that elevates the heart rate to a target zone. Both age and current level of fitness determine this target zone as discussed in unit 10.
# Frequency Of Exercise
Researchers generally agree that cardiovascular training should occur between three and five times per week. Participating in some form of physical activity every day is recommended. Cardiovascular training is especially important for the older client whose sedentary lifestyle may have already compromised cardiovascular endurance. As cardiovascular endurance improves, a progression to cardiovas-cular exercise as a part of the daily routine is recommended. 154Senior Fitness
Help your client build up his or her endurance gradually, starting
out with as little as five minutes of endurance activities at a time, if need be. Starting out at a lower level of effort and working up to a more vigorous workout gradually is especially important if an individual has been inactive for a long time. It may take months to go from a long-standing sedentary lifestyle to doing some of the activities suggested in this unit. The goal is to work your way up, eventually, to a moderate-to-vigorous level of activity that increases breathing and heart rate and feels somewhat hard.
Once you reach the goal, exercise can be divided into sessions of
no less than ten minutes at a time, as long as they add up to a total of at least thirty minutes at the end of the day. Doing less than ten minutes at a time won‚Äôt provide the desired cardiovascular and respiratory system benefits. The exception to this guideline is when an individual has first made the decision to begin doing endurance activities, and is just starting out. The goal in this case is to build up to a total of at least thirty minutes of endurance exercise on four to seven days of the week. More often is better, and every day is best. Cardiovascular Fitness and the Older Adult
# Unit 11 155

heat stroke: A life threat-
ening illness when the
body‚Äôs temperature-reg-ulating mechanisms fail. Bod temperature may rise to over 104 degrees F, skin appears red, dry, and warm to the touch. The victim has chills, sometimes nau-sea and dizziness, and may be confused or irrational. Seizures and coma may follow unless temperature is brought down to 102 degrees within an hour. Seek medical attention immediately if you or your client experiences these symptoms.
hypothermia: Body tem-
perature below normal. Usually due to exposure to cold temperatures, espe-cially after exhausting ready energy supplies. SAFETY CONSIDERATIONS
Endurance activities should not make clients breathe so hard that
they can‚Äôt carry on a conversation with you. Participation should not cause dizziness or chest pain. Have your client do some kind of light activity before and after your endurance exercise session to warm up and cool down (for example, easy walking). Have your client stretch after endurance activities when his or her muscles are warm.
As we get older, our bodies may become less likely to trigger the
urge to drink when we need water. In other words, we may need water, but we won‚Äôt feel thirsty. Be sure to encourage your client to drink fluids when doing any activity that causes loss of fluid through sweat. The rule-of-thumb is that by the time we notice we are thirsty, we are already somewhat dehydrated. This guideline is important year-round, but it is especially important in hot weath-er, when dehydration is more likely. If your client‚Äôs doctor has recommended a limit on fluids, be sure to check with him or her before recommending an increase in fluid intake while exercising. Congestive heart failure and kidney disease are examples of chronic diseases that often require fluid restriction.
Another safety consideration when working with older adults is that they can be affected by heat and cold more often than other adults. In extreme cases, exposure to too much heat can cause heat stroke, and exposure to very cold tem-peratures can lead to hypothermia
(a dangerous drop in body tem-perature). When exercising out-doors, suggest your client dress in layers so clothes may be added or removed as needed. 156Senior Fitness

Always use safety equipment to prevent injuries. Make sure your
client wears a helmet for bicycling and wears protective equipment for activities like skiing and skating. Also be sure your client wears appropriate shoes designed for the exercise they are performing.
# Cardiovascular Exercises
# Walking
Walking is the safest form of exercise and yet one of the most benefi -
cial. Walking does not require a lot of equipment, it can be done con -
tinuously at almost anytime, anywhere, and injuries are infrequent. To ensure safety, encourage your client to walk in an area that has Cardiovascular Fitness and the Older Adult
# Unit 11 157
plenty of light, especially when walking in the evening, and make sure that the walking surface is smooth and stable. Shopping malls are popular sites in which to walk. They provide plenty of space and a safe and well-lit environment and are usually kept at a constant temperature. The only equipment necessary is a good pair of prop -
erly fitted walking shoes, which provide plenty of support and trac -
tion. It is also important to wear comfortable clothing, which allows a person to move freely and doesn‚Äôt constrict bodily movement.
The treadmill provides anoth-
er convenient option for walkers. Instructions for operation are usu-ally listed on the machine. Be sure the client has read and understands the instructions prior to using the treadmill. Make sure that each per-son has the physician‚Äôs and the physical therapist‚Äôs permission before using the treadmill. Have the person straddle the belt before turning the power on. Encourage the person to walk upright and at the front of the belt. Make sure that the person slows before stop-ping, instead of just stopping abruptly.
# Swimming And Water Exercises
Considered one of the best forms of exercise, aqua-activities are ideal for those who may suffer any orthopedic difficulties. Not only does water exercise allow the orthopedically disadvantaged the opportunity to participate freely, it also provides an environment that is quite relaxing. Water exercisers are far less susceptible to injuries due to the body‚Äôs buoyancy and the resistance of the water. Water exercise requires one to use all of the major muscle groups, thus making it an excellent aerobic exercise if done continuously. 158Senior Fitness

# Bicycling
Bicycling is another excellent form of aerobic exercise if done con-
tinuously. Not only does it help increase cardiovascular endurance, but it also helps increase muscular strength and endurance of the leg muscles. For those who have difficulty with balance or lack a safe place to ride, a stationary bicycle provides the same benefits. With a stationary bicycle, one need not worry about traffic or outdoor con-ditions. It allows you the opportunity to exercise at any time inside and even in the privacy of your own home.
Whether cycling outdoors or on a
stationary bicycle, it is necessary to exercise continuously for a mini-mum of twenty minutes to achieve the greatest cardiovascular benefit. Recumbent bikes are good alter-natives for those who cannot use upright bikes because of poor bal-ance, a bad back, or some other phys-ical limitation.
When using a bike, adjust the seat so
the person will have a 15¬∫ bend in the extended leg when cycling. Check to see that the knees are aligned with the toes while pedaling. Special pedals, blocks, and straps are available for stationary bikes for persons whose legs are too short to reach the pedals.
When a person actually lacks the ability to mount the stationary
bicycle, he or she may cycle while seated in a chair or wheelchair positioned behind the bicycle (the bicycle seat is removed). If a chair is used, the chair should be backed up to a wall so that it will not tip over backward during pedaling. If a wheelchair is being used, always lock wheelchair brakes! If the bicycle slides around, place rubber pads under its legs. Cardiovascular Fitness and the Older Adult
# Unit 11 159

paraplegia: An impair-
ment in motor and/or sen-
sory function of the lower extremities. It is usually the result of spinal cord injury or a congenital condition.REHAB TRAINERS (ARM CYCLES)
Studies involving arm pedaling have primarily used interval
training for improving fitness parameters of those with lower-ex-tremity disabilities. Significant improvements in physiologic responses have occurred using three four-minute work bouts sep-arated by two-minute rest intervals. For persons with paraplegia, improvements have been achieved when alternating one-minute bouts of high and low work rates, progressing to thirty minutes of continuous high work rate by the nineteenth week of training.
Assist the person to sit with the
body lined up with the center of the machine. If in a wheelchair, lock the brakes. Adjust the machine so that the fulcrum of the crank arm is even with the shoulder height of the person. Check for possible friction spots on the person‚Äôs skin.
# Lower Body Ergometry
The usefulness of training by lower body cycling has been proven time and time again in research. While we encourage our older clients to participate in weight bearing activity to improve mobility and bone mineral density, the benefits of this low impact exer-cise should not be overlooked. Older clients who have mobility problems benefit from cycling both by developing cardiovascular endurance and leg strength.160Senior Fitness

# Unit Summary
 I. Cardiovascular training is one of the most important
components of physical fitness, and since heart dis-ease is the leading cause of death in this country, it is a preventive measure everyone, including older adults, should participate in regularly.
 II. Cardiovascular training should occur between three and five times per week, and participating in some form of physical activity every day is recommended.
 a. Older clients should build up their endurance grad-ually, starting out with as little as five minutes of endurance activities at a time if need be.
 b. The goal is to work up to a level that increases breathing and heart rate and feels somewhat hard.
 III. Trainers should always take proper safety precautions when doing cardiovascular work with older adults.
 a. Client should never be made to breathe so hard they can‚Äôt carry on a conversation.
 b. Participation should never cause  dizziness or chest pain.
 c. Encourage client to drink plenty of fluids.
 d. Beware of extreme temperature fluctuations, as this can affect older adults more easily than others.
 e. Always use proper safety equipment.
 IV. Many effective cardiovascular exercises exist that offer a vigorous and useful workout as well as safety for the older adult. Examples include walking, swimming, bicy-cling, and arm cycling.
 V. Understanding target heart rate is key, as this allows the trainer to establish the intensity of exercise

necessary to produce a training effect on the
cardiovascular system.Cardiovascular Fitness and the Older Adult

# Strength Training

Strength Training Guidelines
# Unit 12 163
# Learning Objectivesunit Outline

 b. Importance of proper technique
 c. Agonistic and antagonistic muscles
 II. Phases of a Strength Training Program
 a. ‚ÄúKnowledge is Power‚Äù phase
 b. ‚ÄúTrain Don‚Äôt Strain‚Äù concepts
 c. Going Strong and Keeping it Up
 III. Strength Exercises
 a. Lateral raise
After completing this unit you will be able to:
 ‚Ä¢ Institute a strength training program for older adults
 ‚Ä¢ Make recommendations for the safety and progression of the program b. Biceps curl
 c. Triceps extension
 d. Knee flexion
 e. Hip flexion
 f. Shoulder flexion
 g. Knee extension
 h. Hip extension
 i. Side leg raise
 IV. Frequency and Intensity
 V. Safety
While some older adults are as strong as some sedentary younger
adults, it is probably safe to assume that many of the clients you will work with will have brittle bones and weak tendons, ligaments, and muscles. The goal of most older adults is to get fit for life, and since pushing them too hard will only set them up for failure, it is best to begin slowly and progress gradually. It is critical to keep in mind that changes seen in the soft tissue and bone mineral density of older adults require that any exercises they do follow sound bio-mechanical principles. It is also important to remember that more is not necessarily better when it comes to working with the begin-ning older adult client relative to strength training.
# Goals And Techniques
When strength training with older adults, it often makes sense for
exercises to have a functional fitness goal. For example, exercises to make getting out of the chair easier or carrying the groceries easier can have a noticeable impact on clients‚Äô daily activities and highlight the benefits of the training you are doing together. That is why choosing activities that will improve your client‚Äôs quality of life is usually a good way to begin.
The first few weeks or even months of training should be dedicated
to teaching proper lifting techniques. Teaching the correct tech-nique is far more important than increasing the amount of weight lifted. Improper lifting techniques will lead to injury and to clients dropping out of their exercise program. Encourage your clients to ask you questions and make them become familiar with the mus-cles they are working in each exercise.
Many different formulas exist to determine a training load, but
this need not be a consideration until the client knows how to lift without ‚Äúcheating.‚Äù Otherwise the training load determination will be based upon bad information (i.e., clients can curl more weight when they use body momentum as opposed to standing against the wall and trying to do a curl.) Teach the client to be able to perform each movement for at least eight reps in a row without breaking form. If they can‚Äôt do eight reps correctly, the weight is too heavy.
To avoid injuries and enhance the benefits of strength training,
complete an adequate thermal warm-up before lifting and finish with a comprehensive flexibility program after lifting. After the client is warmed up, start the workout with compound exercises before you progress to isolated muscle group exercises (i.e., do bench presses before triceps extensions).
As you plan your client‚Äôs workout, keep in mind that the body has
agonistic muscles and antagonistic muscles. If you have a client do an exercise on the front side, follow with an exercise for an antag-onistic muscle group on the back side. Some trainers call this the 164Senior Fitness
‚Äúpush /pull‚Äù method (working hamstrings/quadriceps, chest/back,
etc.) It is important to note that many older adults have adaptive shortening of some muscles, for example, tight shoulder and chest muscles that cause round shoulders. In this case it would be wise to stretch the tight muscles and strengthen the weak muscles.
Remember: do it right and do it slowly! It is very important to
constantly remind the client to maintain a slow and purposeful movement, lifting the weight slowly and lowering it just as slowly. Smooth controlled movements will produce gradual but safe results.
Also make sure that your client breathes normally! Instruct your
client to breathe one inhalation and exhalation per repetition. Too many people just forget to breathe when lifting weights, especially when they start to go for heavier weights. Strength Training Guidelines
# Unit 12 165
Evaluate Programs and Participants Before Exercise
 1. While exercise benefits even the frail elderly, programs need to be carefully
tailored for seniors. A number of factors should be assessed before older patients begin unsupervised exercise programs.
2.

 Cognitive function is an important consideration. Judgment, memory and alertness should all be evaluated, and patients with impairment may need supervision while exercising.
3.

 Flexibility and manual strength should be assessed to determine where weakness and limited ranges of motion lie.
4.

 Endurance testing can indicate undiagnosed heart problems and show heart rate targets, but such testing is not possible in many frail patients.
 5.  Medications and existing diseases may limit exercise programs. Motor problems, dietary deficiencies, heart problems and pulmonary disease all indicate the need for special evaluation, as does the use of some drugs, including diuretics, psychotropics and antiarrhythmics.
From SENIOR PATIENT, Vol. 2, No. 11 PP 38-42, 4530 W. 77th St., Minneapolis, MN 55435; 612/835-3222.
# Phases Of A
# Strength Training Program
A progressive program should be broken down into phases so that
clients are safely moved along the training path towards their goals. This allows a learning process to occur for both the client and the trainer. The client learns the right way to train safely and effective-ly, and the trainer gets an opportunity to find out more about the client‚Äôs goals and physical capabilities.
Phase One‚ÄîWeeks 1 & 2: ‚ÄúKnowledge is Power‚Äù Phase
 1. The purpose of this phase is to educate your clients so
that they train smart.
 2. Teach before you train.
 3. Progressively work up to lifting one set of fifteen repeti-tions with a relatively light amount of weight. This regi
-
men continues for approximately two weeks. Use this time to teach perfect form.
 4. Instruct your client to breathe naturally during exercise.
Phase Two‚ÄîWeek 3: ‚ÄúTrain Don‚Äôt Strain‚Äù Concepts
 1. Aim to have the client work towards doing one to two sets of seven to fifteen repetitions for all the major  muscle groups.
 2. Again, remind the client to breathe; it does not matter if you exhale on effort or inhale on relaxation as long as

you don‚Äôt hold your breath. Each exercise has a different breathing pattern (i.e., the breathing pattern on a bench press is different than the breathing pattern of a lateral pull).
 3. Teach the client to be able to distinguish between good muscle pain and bad joint pain. Some muscle soreness can

be expected especially if the client was extremely seden-166Senior Fitness
tary. Teach the client to listen to his or her body and, as
their trainer, listen to what they say. The recommenda-tions outlined in this phase are not going to produce great strength gains in a short period but will be successful in keeping the older adult engaged in strength training for a longer period of time. Constantly explain that proper body mechanics are critical to prevent injury.
 4. Remind your client to check body position during each exercise: ears over shoulders, soft knees, straight wrists, neutral spine.
Phase Three‚ÄîWeek 4:  Going Strong and Keeping It Up!
 1. Vary client workouts to prevent burnout, injury, and  boredom.
 2. Individualize! There is no perfect exercise; if the client feels pain then you must adapt, modify, or eliminate until the client can do the activity without any residual discomfort.
 3. A good trainer will progressively overload the muscle. As the muscle gets stronger, challenge the muscle with addi-tional load or with a different exercise that works the same muscle group.
 4. It is very important to avoid controversial exercises with this population. While some exercises might be okay for

younger or fitter clients, they might be ill advised for some older adult clients. Overhead lifts and deep squats are examples of such exercises.
 5. As your older client becomes more advanced, use extra caution when using movements such as high intensity lifts

or isometric activities that can increase intratracheal pres-sure. Such exercises include heavy bench presses, heavy
leg presses, and overhead lifts.Strength Training Guidelines
# Unit 12 167
# Strength Exercises
Even very small changes in muscular development can make a big
difference in strength, especially in people who are already decon-ditioned and have generalized atrophy. Slightly improved muscle strength can be all it takes to improve one‚Äôs ability to do things like get up from a chair or climb stairs.
To do most of the following strength exercises, you need to
lift or push weights, and you need to keep gradually increasing the amount of weight you use. You can use the hand and ankle weights sold in sporting-goods stores, or you can use things like emptied milk jugs filled with sand or water, or socks filled with beans and tied shut at the ends. There are many alternatives to the exercises shown here. For example, you can buy a resistance band at a sporting-goods store for under $10. Stretching it builds mus-
cle and helps in other types of strength exercis-es. Or you can use the special strength-training equipment at a fitness center. Regardless of what kind of equipment you use with your cli-ents, make sure it is safe and effective.
The following exercises are among many that
you may use in getting your clients started. These exercises were drawn from Exercise: A Guide from the National Institute of Aging. You will notice that the directions are geared towards the client rather than you as the train-er; however, this makes them ideal for creat-ing instructional handouts for your client to
take home.
# Lateral Raise
Strengthens shoulder muscles. Sit in a chair, with your back straight. Your feet should be flat on the floor, spaced apart so that they are 168Senior Fitness
Figure 12-1 Lateral Raise

Strength Training Guidelines
# Unit 12 169
even with your shoulders. Hold hand weights straight down at
your sides, with your palms facing inward. Take three seconds to lift your arms straight out, sideways, until they are parallel to the ground. Hold the position for one second. Take three seconds to lower your arms so that they are straight down by your sides again. Pause. Repeat eight to fifteen times. Rest. Do another set of eight to fifteen repetitions.
# Biceps Curl
Strengthens upper-arm muscles. Sit in an arm-less chair, with your back supported by the back of the chair. Your feet should be flat on the floor, spaced apart so that they are even with your shoulders. Hold hand weights down at your side, palms facing in toward your body. Take three seconds to lift your left-hand weight toward your chest by bending your elbow. As you lift, turn your left hand so that your palm is facing your shoulder. Hold the position for one second. Take three seconds to lower your hand to the starting position. Pause, and then repeat with right arm. Alternate until you have repeated the exercise eight to fifteen times on each side. Rest, and then do another set of eight to fifteen alternating repetitions.
# Triceps Extension
(Note: If your shoulders aren‚Äôt flexible enough to do this exercise, see alternative ‚ÄúDip‚Äù exercise that follows.)
Strengthens muscles in back of upper arm. Sit in a chair, toward
the front. Your feet should be flat on the floor, spaced apart so that they are even with your shoulders. Hold a weight in your left hand, and raise your left arm all the way up, so that it‚Äôs pointing toward Figure 12-2 Biceps Curl

170Senior Fitness
the ceiling, palm facing in. Support your left arm by holding it
just below the elbow with your right hand. Slowly bend your left arm so that the weight in your left hand now rests behind your left shoulder. Take three seconds to straighten your left arm so that it‚Äôs pointing toward the ceiling again. Hold the position for one second. Take three seconds to lower the weight back to your shoulder by bending your elbow. Keep supporting your left arm with your right hand throughout the exercise. Pause, then repeat the bending and
Figure 12-3 (A) Triceps Extension (B) Alternative ‚ÄúDip‚Äù Exercise for Back of Upper ArmsA

Strength Training Guidelines
# Unit 12 171
straightening until you have done the exercise eight to fifteen times
with your left arm. Reverse positions and repeat eight to fifteen times with your right arm. Rest, and then repeat another set of eight to fifteen repetitions on each side.
ALTERNATIVE ‚ÄúDIP‚Äù EXERCISE
# For Back Of Upper Arm
Sit in a chair with arm rests. Make sure the chair is heavy enough
and stable enough to support dips. Lean slightly forward, keeping your back and shoulders straight. Hold onto the arms of the chair. Your hands should be level with the trunk of your body, or slightly farther forward. Place your feet slightly under the chair, with your heels off the ground and the weight of your feet and legs resting on your toes and the balls of your feet. Slowly lift yourself up, using your arms, as high as you can. This pushing motion will strengthen your arm muscles even if you aren‚Äôt yet able to lift yourself up off of the chair. Don‚Äôt use your legs or feet for assistance, or use them as little as possible. Slowly lower yourself back down. Repeat eight to fifteen times. Rest, and then repeat another eight to fifteen times.
# Knee Flexion
Strengthens muscles in back of thigh. Stand straight, very close to a table or chair, holding it for balance. Take three seconds to bend your left knee so that your calf comes as far up toward the back of your thigh as possible. Don‚Äôt move your upper leg at all; bend your knee only. Take three seconds to lower your left leg all the way back down. Repeat with right leg. Alternate legs until you have done eight to fifteen repetitions with each leg. Rest, and then do another set of eight to fifteen alternating repetitions. Use ankle weights when you are ready to progress.
Figure 12-4 Knee Flexion

# Hip Flexion
Strengthens thigh and hip muscles. Stand to the side or behind a
chair or table, holding it with one hand for balance. Take three sec-onds to bend your left knee and bring it as far toward your chest as possible. Stand straight throughout, without bending at the waist or hips. Hold position for one second, and then take three seconds to lower your left leg all the way down. Repeat with right leg; alter-nate legs until you have done eight to fifteen repetitions on each side. Rest, and then do another set of eight to fifteen alternating repetitions. Add ankle weights when you are ready to progress.
# Shoulder Flexion
Strengthens shoulder muscles. Sit in a chair, with back straight. Feet should be flat on the floor, spaced apart so that they are even with your shoulders. Hold hand weights straight down at your sides, with your palms facing inward. Take three seconds to lift your arms in front of you, keeping them straight and rotating them so that your palms are facing upward, until your arms are parallel to the ground. Hold the position for one second. Take three sec-onds to lower your arms so that they are straight down by your sides again. Pause. Repeat eight to fifteen times. Rest; do another set of eight to fifteen repetitions.
# Knee Extension
Strengthens muscles in front of thigh and shin. Sit in a chair, with your back resting against the back of the chair. If your feet are flat on the floor in this position, you should place a rolled-up towel under your knees to lift them up. Only the balls of your feet and your toes should be resting on the floor. Rest your hands on your thighs or on the sides of the chair. Take three seconds to extend your right leg in front of you, parallel to the floor, until your knee is straight. With your right leg in this position, flex your foot so that your toes are pointing toward your head; hold your foot in this position for one to two seconds. Take three seconds to lower your
right leg back to the starting position, so that the ball of your foot 172Senior Fitness
Figure 12-5 Hip Flexion
Figure 12-6 Shoulder Flexion
Figure 12-7 Knee Extension

rests on the floor again. Repeat with left leg. Alternate legs, until
you have done the exercise eight to fifteen times with each leg. Rest; then do another set of eight to fifteen alternating repetitions. Use ankle weights when you are ready to progress.
# Hip Extension
Strengthens buttock and lower-back muscles. Stand twelve to eigh-teen inches away from a table or chair, feet slightly apart. Bend forward from the hips, at about a 45-degree angle, holding onto the table or chair for balance. In this position, take three seconds to lift your left leg straight behind you without bending your knee, point-ing your toes, or bending your upper body any farther forward. Hold the position for one second. Take three seconds to lower your left leg back to the starting position. Repeat with right leg. Alternate legs, until you have repeated the exercise eight to fifteen times with each leg. Rest; then do another set of eight to fifteen alternating repetitions with each leg. Add ankle weights when you are ready to progress.
# Side Leg Raise
Strengthens muscles at sides of hips and thighs. Stand up straight, directly behind a table or chair, feet slightly apart. Hold onto the table to help keep your balance. Take three seconds to lift your right leg six to twelve inches out to the side. Keep your back and both legs straight. Don‚Äôt point your toes outward‚Äîkeep them fac-ing forward. Hold the position for one second. Take three seconds to lower your leg back to the starting position. Repeat with left leg. Alternate legs, until you have repeated the exercise eight to fifteen times with each leg. Rest, and do another set. Use ankle weights when you are ready to progress.
# Frequency And Intensity
Recommend to your client that strength exercises for all of the major muscle groups be within the training program, and don‚Äôt schedule strength exercises of the same muscle group on any two days in a row. Strength Training Guidelines
# Unit 12 173
Figure 12-8 Hip Extension
Figure 12-9 Side Leg Raise

Some older clients might need to start out using as little as one or
two pounds of weight, or no weight at all. The connective tissues that bind the structures of one‚Äôs body together need to adapt to strength exercises. Have your client use a minimum of weight the first week, and then gradually build up the weight. Starting out with weights that are too heavy (or, in some cases, starting with any weight at all) can cause injuries. Use this time to help your client develop perfect form and an understanding of which muscle is being trained. At the same time, remember that you have to gradually add a challenging amount of weight in order for your clients to benefit from strength exercises. If you don‚Äôt challenge their muscles, they won‚Äôt benefit from strength exercises. Stretch your client after strength exercises, when their muscles are warmed up. When stretching before strength exercise, make sure they warm up first with light cardio exercise (light walking and arm pumping, for example).
# Safety
Instruct your clients not to hold their breath. Remind them to breathe normally. Holding one‚Äôs breath while straining can cause changes in blood pressure. This is especially true for people with cardiovascular disease.

If your client has had a hip repair or replacement, check with the doctor who did their surgery before doing lower-body exercises.

Don‚Äôt allow these clients to cross their legs, or bend their hips farther than a 90-degree angle. Avoid jerking or thrusting move-ments that can cause injuries. Instead, use smooth, steady move-ments. Avoid ‚Äúlocking‚Äù the joints in arms and legs into tightly straightened position. (Tip: To have your client straighten their knees, ask them to tighten the thigh muscles; this will lift their kneecaps and protect them.)
Muscle soreness lasting up to a few days and slight fatigue is nor-
mal following muscle-building exercises; however, exhaustion, sore joints, and unpleasant muscle pulling aren‚Äôt. The latter symp-toms mean you are overworking your client. None of the exercises should cause pain. The range within which you have your clients move their arms and legs should never hurt.174Senior Fitness
Guidelines for a Safe
& Beneficial Strength Program
1.  Keep accurate, daily records
of your participant‚Äôs per-formance. This procedure includes recording the date, poundage used, and the number of sets and reps performed.
2.
 Retest every few weeks to reestablish the 1RM (if appropriate).
3.
 Supervise with a spotter (assistant) when a partici-pant is lifting weights ‚Äî no matter the poundage!
4.
 Alternate upper and lower body exercises so no two consecutive exercises involve the same prime mover.
5.
 Perform exercises that involve larger muscle  groups first. For example, perform shoulder strength
-
ening exercises before  wrist exercises.
6.
 Allow sufficient recovery time between sets (usually several minutes), especially for those with degenerative neuromuscular conditions.
7.
 Allow the client at least one day of rest between strength training sessions.
8.
 Each repetition should be performed in a smooth and controlled manner.
# Unit Summary
 I. Strength training for many older adults involves function-
al fitness, that is, developing strength that will assist the person in carrying out their daily activities. Exercises that make getting out of a chair or carrying the groceries easier can have a significant impact on the older client‚Äôs quality of life.
 II. Employing proper technique when strength training with older adults is important to help avoid injury.
 a. In the beginning, focus on teaching proper lifting  techniques.
 b. Make sure the client can do eight reps correctly before increasing the training load.
 c. Warm up before lifting and stretch afterwards.
 d. Keep in mind the need to balance working agonistic muscles with antagonistic muscles.
 e. Encourage slow, purposeful movements.
 f. Encourage the client to breathe!
 III. A strength training program should be broken down into phases so that clients are moved along safely toward their goals. In Phase One, the focus is on education. In Phase Two, the client works toward doing one to two sets of seven to fifteen repetitions for all major muscle groups. And in Phase Three, the client learns principles for main-taining a long-term pattern of strength training.
 IV. Many effective exercises exist that can be safely incorpo-rated into a strength training program for older adults. Examples include the lateral raise, the biceps curl, the tri
-
ceps extension, knee flexion, and hip flexion, to name just a few.
 V. A strength training program should include exercises for all of the major muscle groups, and working the same muscle group any two days in a row should be avoided. Clients should begin with a light weight and gradually build up.Strength Training Guidelines
