Flexibility is critical for older adults. Participating in flexibility
training allows the client to improve their level of health. The abil-ity to perform activities of daily living is greatly enhanced when a person regularly engages in a physical activity program that includes stretching.
# Optimal Conditions
# For Eliciting A Stretch
Connective tissue displays both properties of elasticity (the ability to rebound to original length) and plasticity (the ability to undergo deformation without rupture). To increase flexibility, it is import-ant to affect the plastic property of connective tissue. To achieve the optimal conditions for a permanent increase in flexibility keep the following things in mind.
Engage in Warm-Up Prior to Stretching
Increased tissue temperature will increase the viscous (plastic) property of connective tissue, resulting in a greater elongation or stretch. In other words, a warm muscle will stretch farther than a cold one! Warm-up may include easy laps around the track, walk-ing, riding a stationary cycle, or doing calisthenics until a sweat is broken (five to ten minutes). The same result may be achieved by performing calisthenics in place.
Do Not Apply Too Much Force to the Stretch
Lower amounts of force induce less injury and tearing to connec-tive tissue than high amounts of force. Vigorous or ballistic stretch-ing may cause bleeding in the joint, as well as tearing of soft tissue.
Hold the Stretch for a Sufficient Duration
Although research has not demonstrated what the optimal dura-tion for a stretch is, many believe that assisted and active stretching is best. For purposes of this manual, it is suggested that each stretch be held for a duration of thirty to sixty seconds. 178Senior Fitness
edema: Swelling from
excessive accumulation of
watery fluid in cells, tissues, or serous cavities.Perform Stretching through
the Pain-Free Range of Motion
Do not perform stretching if pain, infection, or edema (swelling) is
present. Stretching while pain is present could cause the problem to worsen, thereby delaying the healing process.
Incorporate Stretches at the End of Cool-Down
To prevent adaptive shortening and promote the relaxation of muscle, be sure to stretch after training. Muscle which has been subjected to a vigorous workout will generally be in a contracted state. Performing stretching at the end of the workout will return muscle to its resting length and promote additional elongation of connective tissue.
# Stretching Techniques
There are two basic types of stretching techniques: active and passive. Active stretching is performed without assistance, using a volitional muscle contraction, while passive stretching is performed Maintaining Flexibility As We Age
# Unit 13 179

proprioceptive
neuromuscular
facilitation (PNF):
Muscle stretches that use
the proprioceptors (muscle spindles) to send inhibit-ing (relaxing) messages to the muscle that is to be stretched. with the aid of an assistant. The range of motion of a joint is usually
greater in a passive stretch than an active one. PNF (propriocep-tive neuromuscular facilitation) Hold-Relax is a technique that utilizes both active and passive stretching.
ACTIVE STRETCHING (SLOW STATIC)
This technique involves slowly stretching a muscle for thirty to sixty seconds (or longer) by contracting the opposing muscle group. If proper stretching is to be accomplished, the lengthened muscle must be held at the point of limitation with a tension level that does not activate, to any marked extent, the stretch reflex mechanism. After holding the stretch for a period of time, the dis-comfort of tension should diminish to some degree. At this time, the individual may increase the stretch and establish a new point of limitation. This process can be repeated until no further range of motion is attainable. Discomfort that becomes greater the longer the stretch is held or quivering or vibrating of the lengthened mus-cle signals overstretching. Avoid bouncing or ballistic movements.
# Passive Stretching
This technique is applied when the individual cannot perform an active stretch. It is performed by the trainer and involves stretch-ing every affected joint to prevent contractures from developing through disuse. The trainer moves each joint slowly through the permissible range-of-motion, using one hand to stabilize the body while the other moves the limb. Hold at the point of limitation for thirty to sixty seconds. Prolonged, moderate stretching is more effective than momentary, vigorous stretching. Two to five repeti-tions are usually sufficient for passive range of motion.
PNF (PROPRIOCEPTIVE NEUROMUSCULAR
FACILITATION) HOLD-RELAX
PNF hold-relax is a relaxation/lengthening technique in which the
individual isometrically and maximally contracts a muscle group 180Senior Fitness
Golgi Tendon Organs:
Nerve sensors (‚Äúproprio-ceptors‚Äù), located at the junction of muscles and tendons, that pick up mes-sages of excess stress on the muscle and cause the brain to shut off muscle contraction. The purpose may be to protect against separating the tendon from bone when a contraction is too great. prior to stretching it to a new point of limitation. The contraction
is held for six seconds. When the maximal number of motor units is contracting simultaneously, the Golgi Tendon Organs within that muscle will fire, causing the muscle to relax. This phenome-non is known as autogenic inhibition. During this post-contrac-tion depression, lasting for about six seconds, the Golgi Tendon Organs override the reflex activity of the muscle spindles. The individual or assistant then moves the limb to a new point of lim-itation. The sequence is repeated until no new further range of motion is obtainable.
Hold-Relax Sequence:
 1. The trainer (passively) or the participant (actively)
moves the limb to the point of limitation. Active move-ment should be encouraged whenever possible. The participant then isometrically and maximally contracts the muscle being stretched against a resistance provid
-
ed by the trainer for six seconds . This is the hold period
of the technique, and the limb should not be allowed to move. The maximal contraction should be brought on gradually, not suddenly.
 2. The participant then relaxes the stretched muscle and moves the limb to a new point of limitation, either actively or passively, with help of the trainer. This
relaxation  period for the stretched muscle should last
six seconds . Repeat the sequence of holds and relax-
ation until no further range of motion is obtained.
# Isokinetic Exercise
The participant contracts the agonist muscle group through the
range of motion against a lever of constant speed, thereby achiev-ing a maximal effort at every point in the range. Because it accom-modates the strength changes that occur as the joint angle changes, isokinetic exercise theoretically enables one to perform more work than with isotonic methods. Only reciprocal movements with con-centric contractions occur with isokinetic exercise. The exerciser does not lift weight but pushes against a lever that moves at a fixed Maintaining Flexibility As We Age
# Unit 13 181
isokinetic exercise:
Exercise equipment using
accommodating resistance technology. For example, Keiser equipment uses compressed air to provide accommodating resistance. With this form of isokinet-ic movement, the harder you push the harder the machine resists, providing the net effect of controlling the speed of movement.
speed in both directions (e.g., flexion/extension). Thus, it allows
the instructor to measure strength imbalances about a joint (e.g., knee flexors should be approximately 60% the strength of the knee extensors at slower velocities of movement). Isokinetic devices offer a range of speeds to select for training.
# Contraindications For
# Performing Stretching
 1. Infections around a joint.
 2. Exacerbation (attacks) of inflammatory disease, especially when pain is present.
 3. Edema‚Äîjoint capsule is subject to tears.
 4. Functional contractures (e.g., finger flexors, elbow flexors, and pronators may actually assist the par-ticipant to pull objects toward him or her).
# Performance Requirements For Stretching Techniques
 1. Know the anatomical motions that occur at each
joint. (Review Unit 4 of Fitness: The Complete Guide
from your CFT course.)
 2. Stabilize the extremities at the joint, for example at
the elbow or wrist. For someone with a painful joint, such as in arthritis, support the extremity in the mus-cular area.
 3. Try to avoid touching the muscle or tendons being stretched as this may trigger unwanted reflex activity.
 4. Stretch in opposition to the line of pull of the muscle.
 5. Use a firm but comfortable grip.182Senior Fitness

# Stretches
The following stretches, among others, should
be incorporated into the fitness plan. As your client makes progress, the stretching tech-niques you use may change to accommodate improvements in flexibility. The following list of safe stretches is also taken from Exercise: A Guide from the National Institute on Aging.
# Hamstring Stretch
Stretches muscles in back of thigh.
 1. Sit sideways on bench or other hard
surface (such as two chairs placed side by side.
 2. Keep one leg stretched out on bench, straight, toes pointing up.
 3. Keep other leg off of bench, with foot flat on floor.
 4. Straighten back.
 5. If you feel a stretch at this point, hold the position for 10 to 30 seconds.
 6. If you don‚Äôt feel a stretch, lean for-ward from hips (not waist) until you feel stretching in leg on bench, keeping back and shoulders straight. (Omit this step if you have had a hip replacement, unless your surgeon or physical therapist says otherwise.)
 7. Hold position for 10 to 30 seconds.
 8. Repeat with other leg.
 9. Repeat 3 to 5 times on each side.186Senior Fitness
Figure 13-4 (A) Hamstring Stretch (B) Alternative
Hamstring Stretch
A
B
# Alternative Hamstring
# Stretch
Stretches muscles in the back of the thigh.
 1. Stand behind chair, holding the back of it with both hands.
 2. Bend forward from the hips (not waist), keeping back and shoulders straight at all times.
 3. When upper body is parallel to floor, hold position for 10 to 30 seconds. You should feel a stretch in the backs of your thighs.
 4. Repeat 3 to 5 times.
# Calf Stretch
Stretches lower leg muscles in two ways: with
knee straight and knee bent. 1. Stand with hands against wall, arms
outstretched and elbows straight.
 2. Keeping your left knee slightly bent, toes of right foot slightly turned

inward, step back 1 to 2 feet with right leg, heel, and foot flat on floor. You should feel a stretch in your calf mus-cle, but you shouldn‚Äôt feel uncomfort-able. If you don‚Äôt feel a stretch, move your foot farther back until you do.
 3. Hold position for 10 to 30 seconds.
 4. Bend knee of right leg, keep heel and foot flat on floor.
 5. Hold position for another 10 to 30 seconds.
 6. Repeat with left leg.
 7. Repeat 3 to 5 times for each leg.UNIT 13 187Maintaining Flexibility As We Age
Figure 13-5 Calf Stretch

# Ankle Stretch
Stretches front ankle muscles.
 1. Remove your shoes. Sit toward the
front edge of a chair and lean back, using pillows to support your back.
 2. Stretch legs out in front of you.
 3. With your heels still on the floor, bend ankles to point feet toward you.
 4. Bend ankles to point feet away  from you.
 5. If you don‚Äôt feel the stretch, repeat with your feet slightly off the floor.
 6. Hold the position for 1 second.
 7. Repeat 3 to 5 times.TRICEPS STRETCH
Stretches muscles in back of upper arm.
 1. Hold one end of a towel in the  right hand.
 2. Raise and bend right arm to drape towel down back. Keep your right arm in this position, and continue holding onto the towel.
 3. Reach behind your lower back and grasp bottom end of towel with the left hand.
 4. Climb left hand progressively higher up towel, which also pulls your right arm down. Continue until your hands touch, or as close to that as you can comfortably go.
 5. Reverse positions.
 6. Repeat each position 3 to 5 times.188Senior Fitness

# Wrist Stretch
Stretches wrist muscles.
 1. Place hands together, in the
praying position.
 2. Slowly raise elbows so arms are  parallel to floor, keeping hands flat against each other.
 3. Hold position for 10 to 30 seconds.
 4. Repeat 3 to 5 times. 4. Reach back and grab heel of that leg. If you can‚Äôt reach your heal with your hand, loop a belt over your foot and hold belt ends.
 5. Gently pull that leg until the front of  thigh stretches.
 6. Hold position for 10 to 30 seconds.
 7. Reverse position and repeat.
 8. Repeat 3 to 5 times on each side. If the back of your thigh cramps during this exercise, stretch your leg and try
again, more slowly.UNIT 13 189Maintaining Flexibility As We Age

# Quadriceps Stretch
Stretches muscles in front of thighs.
 1. Lie on side on the floor. Your hips
should be lined up so that one is directly above the other one.
 2. Rest head on pillow or hand.
 3. Bend knee that is on top. Figure 13-9 Quadriceps Stretch
# Double Hip Rotation
Stretches outer muscles of hips and thighs.
Don‚Äôt do this exercise if you have had a hip replacement, unless your surgeon approves.
 1. Lie on floor on your back, knees bent
and feet flat on the floor.
 2. Keep shoulders on floor at all times.
 3. Keeping knees bent and together, gently lower legs to one side as far as possible without forcing them.
 4. Hold position for 10 to 30 seconds.
 5. Return legs to upright position.
 6. Repeat toward other side.
 7. Repeat 3 to 5 times on each side.
# Single Hip Rotation
Stretches muscles of pelvis and inner thigh.
Don‚Äôt do this exercise if you have had a hip replacement, unless your surgeon approves.
 1. Lie on your back on floor, knees bent
and feet flat on the floor.  2. Keep shoulders on floor throughout exercise.
 3. Lower one knee slowly to side, keep-ing the other leg and your pelvis in place.
 4. Hold position for 10 to 30 seconds.
 5. Bring knee back up slowly.
 6. Repeat with other knee.
 7. Repeat 3 to 5 times on each side.190Senior Fitness

A
B
# Shoulder Rotation
Stretches shoulder muscles.
 1. Lie flat on floor, pillow under head,
legs straight. If your back bothers you, place a rolled towel under your knees.
 2. Stretch arms straight out to side. Your shoulders and upper arms will remain

flat on the floor throughout this exer-cise.
 3. Bend elbows so that your hands are pointing toward the ceiling. Let your arms slowly roll backwards from the elbow. Stop when you feel a stretch or slight discomfort, and stop imme-diately if you feel a pinching sensation or a sharp pain.  4. Hold position for 10 to 30 seconds.
 5. Slowly raise your arms, still bent at the elbow, to point toward the ceiling again. Then let your arms slowly roll forward, remaining bent at the elbow, to point toward your hips. Stop when you feel a stretch or slight discomfort.
 6. Hold position for 10 to 30 seconds.
 7. Alternate pointing above head, then toward ceiling, then toward hips. Begin and end with pointing-above-head position.
 8. Repeat 3 to 5 times.UNIT 13 191Maintaining Flexibility As We Age
Figure 13-11 Shoulder Rotation

# Neck Rotation
Stretches neck muscles.
 1. Lie on the floor with a phone book or
other thick book under your head.
 2. Slowly turn head from side to side, holding position each time for 10 to 30 seconds on each side. Your head should not be tipped forward or back-ward, but should be in a comfortable position. You can keep your knees bent to keep your back comfortable during this exercise.
 3. Repeat 3 to 5 times.192Senior Fitness
Figure 13-12 Ankle Stretch
Make sure your clients stretch after regularly
scheduled strength and endurance exercis-es. If your client is unable to do endurance or strength exercises for some reason, and stretching exercises are the only kind they are able to do, make sure they do them at least three times a week, for at least twenty min-utes each session. Do each stretching exercise three to five times at each session.
# Safety
If your client has had a hip replacement, check with the doctor who did the surgery before doing lower body exercises. Clients who have had a hip replacement should not do any stretches that require them to cross their legs or bend their hips past a 90-degree angle.
Always have your client warm up before
stretching exercises. Have clients stretch after endurance or strength exercises, for example; or, if only performing stretching exercises on a particular day, do a little bit of easy walking and arm-pumping first. Stretching prior to a warm up may result in injury.
Stretching should never cause pain, espe-
cially joint pain. If stretching is painful, you FREQUENCY AND INTENSITY
are stretching the client too
far and need to reduce the stretch so that it doesn‚Äôt hurt. Mild discomfort or a mild pulling sensation is normal. Instruct your client to never ‚Äúbounce‚Äù into a stretch, but to make slow, steady move-ments instead. Jerking into position can cause muscles to tighten, possibly result-ing in injury. Tell your cli-ents to avoid locking joints into place when straighten-ing them during stretches. Arms and legs should be straight when stretched, but not locked into a tightly straight position. Always make sure your client has a very small amount of bending in their joints while stretching.
# Progressing
You will know how to progress by communicating with your cli-ent, stretching should never hurt. It may feel slightly uncomfort-able, but not painful. Push your client to stretch farther, but not so far that it hurts. Maintaining Flexibility As We Age
# Unit 13 193

# About Floor Exercises
Some stretches that are done on the floor stretch very important
muscle groups. If your client is afraid to lie on the floor to exercise because they fear that they won‚Äôt be able to get back up, consider using the buddy system to do some of these exercises.
Knowing the right way to get into a lying position on the floor and
the right way to get back up also may be helpful to your clients. Those people who have had a hip replacement should check with their surgeon before using the following method. The same advice goes for clients with osteoporosis‚Äîcheck with the doctor first.
To get into a lying position:
 1. Stand next to a very sturdy chair that won‚Äôt tip
over (put chair against wall for support if you need to). Put your hands on the seat of the chair.
 2. Lower yourself down on one knee.
 3. Bring the other knee down.
 4. Put your left hand on the floor and lean on it as you bring your left hip to the floor.
 5. Your weight is now on your left hip.
 6. Straighten your legs out.
 7. Lie on your left side.
 8. Roll onto your back.
Note: You don‚Äôt have to use your left side. Reverse positions, if you like.194Senior Fitness
Maintaining Flexibility As We Age
# Unit 13 195
To get up from a lying position:
 1. Roll onto your left side.
 2. Use your right hand, placed on the floor at about
the level of your ribs, to push your shoulders off the floor.
 3. Your weight is on your left hip.
 4. Roll forward, onto your knees, leaning on your hands for support.
 5. Lean your hands on the seat of the chair you used to lie down.
 6. Lift one of your knees so that one leg is bent, foot flat on the floor.
 7. Leaning your hands on the seat of the chair for support, rise from this position.
Note: You don‚Äôt have to use your left side. Reverse positions, if you like.Figure 13-13 Getting Up From Lying Position.
# Unit Summary
 I. Since flexibility is important for safe training as well as
for the performance of routine daily activities, atten-tion to flexibility should be included in any fitness

program for older adults.
 II. An awareness of the proper conditions and techniques for stretching is key and should be conveyed to  the client.
 a. Optimal conditions for eliciting a stretch include warming up muscles before stretching, avoiding the application of too much force to the stretch, and holding the stretch for a duration of approximately thirty to sixty seconds.
 b. The two basic stretching techniques are active stretching and passive stretching.
 III. Safe stretches for older adults include specific stretches for major muscle groups as well as wrists, ankles, hips, shoulders, and neck.
 IV. Stretching should never cause pain, especially in  the joints.
 V. Clients who are nervous about doing floor stretches because they are afraid they won‚Äôt be able to get up can use the buddy system and can also be instructed in proper methods for getting into and out of a lying position on the floor.196Senior Fitness

After completing this unit you will be able to:
 ‚Ä¢ Have the basic information necessary to work
safely with individuals with cardiac and pulmo-nary conditions
 ‚Ä¢ Help individuals recovering from a cardiac event bridge the gap between rehabilitation and gen-eral fitness programming
 ‚Ä¢ Identify warning signs that indicate danger for an individual with a cardiac or pulmonary con-dition
 ‚Ä¢ Understand how exercise can influence out-come for individuals with cardiac or pulmonary conditions
When done properly and prudently,
exercise will normally have a positive outcome. Exercise is therapy for the mind and the body. However, it is the trainer‚Äôs job to make sure it is done within the standards and prac-tices recommended for older adults. It is also important to remember that the doctor‚Äôs or physical thera-pist‚Äôs protocols supercede any exer-cise program you have developed. If you have concerns or disagreements about the therapy prescribed for the client, call the doctor or physical therapist to discuss their rationales. As we said earlier, your role is to be a team player in the client‚Äôs continu-um of care.
Exercise is often prescribed for orthopedic conditions, heart health, and general health issues, and we know that many of these issues become more common, sometimes chronic, with age. In this sec-tion, we will examine some of these conditions and explore the things that every trainer needs to know when working with clients who are dealing with them. Be sure to keep in mind, though, that your client is an individual to be respected and cared for, and not just someone with a bad back or knee. Our mission is to make the client function at optimum efficiency.
This section will address working with individuals with cardiac and
pulmonary conditions, chronic conditions seen frequently in older adults. The pathology of numerous conditions will be explained, as will the ways in which exercise can influence outcome. Corrective exercises will also be explained and illustrated. Our approach will be therapeutic in nature; however, we will work from a wellness model rather than a model of sickness. 200Senior Fitness

myocardial infarction
(MI): Destruction of
heart tissue resulting from obstruction of the blood supply to the heart muscle. Commonly known as a heart attack.
angioplastie surgery:
Surgery to repair a dam-aged blood vessel or unblock a coronary artery.
bypass surgery: Surgery
that reroutes blood around clogged coronary arteries and improves the supply of blood and oxygen to the heart muscle.CARDIOVASCULAR CONDITIONS
# Cardiac Rehabilitation
Following a major cardiac incident like a myocardial infarction
(MI) or ‚Äúheart attack,‚Äù most patients will participate for twelve to thirty weeks in a cardiac rehabilitation program. Cardiac rehabil-itation programs also serve clients who have undergone cardiac surgeries to prevent major events like angioplasties and bypass surgeries from happening.
There are generally four phases of cardiac rehabilitation. The first
two stages involve acute care in the days and weeks immediately following the event or surgery. During this time the primary goals are functional abilities like sitting up, standing, walking (stop and go), and flexibility. Phases three and four include walking pro-grams of greater intensity than the previous stages and light resis-tance exercises. Once the patients have graduated to phase three or four, they approach a period where they are not quite ready to be training on their own, yet they no longer require clinical monitor-ing. During this time physicians will strongly encourage their older clients to continue participating in an outside exercise program to further the gains that have been made in rehabilitation and to pre-vent any further cardiac incidents from occurring.
As a fitness professional, you can bridge the gap between rehabili-
tation and general fitness programming. Working with physicians and therapists, you can play a significant role in providing life-en-hancing activity programs to those people who have experienced cardiac difficulties. Exercise plays a key role in the return to a normal lifestyle following a heart attack, angioplasty, bypass sur-gery, or other trauma to the heart. The continued progress made through ‚Äúafter care‚Äù exercise programs has been shown to reduce the rate of subsequent cardiac events and improve quality of life.Chronic Conditions I: Cardiovascular and Pulmonary Conditions
# Unit 14 201
Indicated Exercise Program
In addition to any specific recommendations the physician or ther-
apist has made, regular participation in cardiovascular exercise is a must. Consistency is essential in cardiovascular workouts. Your client should do aerobic activity for at least fifteen to twenty min-utes, three times a week. Be sure to start with whatever the client can comfortably do and work up to this min-imum. As physical condition improves, consider gradually increasing the workouts to thirty to sixty minutes five or more times a week.
The type of cardiovascular
activity that will be chosen depends on your client‚Äôs per-sonal preferences and the options available. Walking is a popular low impact activ-ity to start with, especially for those people who have not been active. Cycling and water exercise programs are equally as effective in getting the heart working and are also excellent low impact choices.
Strength training is another component that should be added to
the exercise program. Light dumbbells and resistance bands can be used to develop major muscle groups until the client is ready to begin work on strength-training machines and heavier free weights. Two to three days per week of resistance training is rec-ommended for optimum gains. As the client progresses, you can work them up to eight to ten strength sessions for two to four sets of eight to twelve repetitions.202Senior Fitness

Contraindications of Exercise
 1. Sharp pain or pressure in chest, back, jaw, or arm
 2. Shortness of breath
 3. Abnormal heart rhythm
 4. Nausea
 5. Dizziness or feeling light-headed
Importance of Exercise
 1. Increases functional efficiency of the
cardiovascular system
 2. Improves venous return
 3. Restores quality of life following major surgery
 4. Prevents further cardiac incidents from occurring
When to perform exercise
 1. Three to five days per week
 2. When no illness or pain is present
 3. When exercise can be performed without  causing pain
 4. At a time of day that is convenient and will  promote regular participation
Precautions
 1.  Consult with the client‚Äôs physician to find out exactly what the physical and medical condition of your client is and how serious it is.
 2. Find out what medications your client is taking and their affect on exercise capacity and heart rate.Chronic Conditions I: Cardiovascular and Pulmonary Conditions
# Unit 14 203
# Hypertension
Many adults have high blood pressure. It is believed that hyper-
tension is the third most common chronic condition in the United States, right behind sinusitis and arthritis. Generally, people with high blood pressure will get cardiovascular disease after sixty-five years of age. Normal blood pressure is considered a measurement (in millimeters of mercury) of 120mmHg/80mmHg or lower. Hypertension is characterized by resting blood pressure measur-ing greater than 140mmHg systolic and/or 90mmHg diastolic. Although both measurements may be elevated, having one or the other elevated still classifies as hypertension. Elevated blood pressure is characterized as increased pressure against the arterial wall during heart contraction (systole) and during heart relaxation (diastole). Medical experts diagnose 90 percent of hypertension as coming from an unknown cause. Speculations have been made about possible causes ranging from excess sodium intake to elevat-ed stress levels. Hypertension is usually characterized in stages as shown in Table 14-1.hypertension: Persistent
high blood pressure. 204Senior Fitness
Table 14-1 Stages of Hypertension
Blood Pressure Category Systolic (mmHg) Diastolic (mmHg)
Normal <130 <85
High Normal 130 - 139 85 - 99
Stage 1 (Mild) 140 - 159 90 - 99
Stage 2 (Moderate) 160 - 179 100 - 109
Stage 3 (Severe) 180 - 209 110 - 119
Stage 4 (Very Severe) ‚â•210 ‚â•120
Indicated Exercise Program
Participation in regular exercise can help to bring blood pressure
levels back within the normal range. The level or intensity of exer-cise at which you will train your client will vary depending on their level of hypertension and their physician‚Äôs recommendation. For stage one hypertensives, low intensity cardiovascular training may Source: National High Blood Pressure Education Program
be used to alleviate high blood pressure, often without the client‚Äôs
having to be placed on medication. Both cardiovascular and resis-tance training may be used with this client, paying special attention to keeping the resistance low and the repetitions high. Only those stage one hypertensives that are borderline or have their hyperten-sion under control should participate in resistance/weight training. Be aware that at this stage, more people are taking prescription medications. Blood pressure medications can influence resting and exercise heart rate levels, so be sure to keep informed of any and all medications your client is taking. Exercise is generally not recom-mended in the treatment plan for stage three hypertensives due to the sensitive nature of the condition.
Contraindications
 1.  Avoid training when your client has a resting systol-
ic BP >175mmHg unless you have the physician‚Äôs consent.
 2. Avoid heavy resistance training.
 3. Instruct the client to avoid holding his or her breath, as that increases blood pressure.
Precautions
 1.  Emphasize warm-up and stretching portions of the workout and include a prolonged cool-down ses-sion.
 2. Never have clients stop suddenly; always keep them moving after vigorous exercise until heart rate is close to their pre-exercising resting level.
 3. Focus on exhaling with each repetition of an exer-cise
 4. Exercising with light weights may be acceptable, but check with the physician before starting. Emphasize muscular endurance versus strength. Strive to perform a minimum of ten to fifteen repe-titions of each exercise with a lighter weight.
 5. Concentrate more of the program on lower-ex-tremity exercises. Upper body work is good, but pressing too much weight overhead tends to ele
-
vate the blood pressure.Chronic Conditions I: Cardiovascular and Pulmonary Conditions

hyperlipidemia:  Higher
than normal fat and cho-
lesterol levels in the blood

(cholesterol).
high-density lipoprotein
(HDL): A type of lipopro-tein that seems to provide protection against the buildup of atherosclerotic fat deposits in the arteries. Exercise seems to increase the HDL fraction of total cholesterol. HDL contains high levels of protein and low levels of triglycerides and cholesterol.
low-density lipopro-
tein (LDL): A lipoprotein
carrying a high level of cholesterol, moderate levels of protein and low levels of triglycerides. Associated with the building of other sclerotic deposits in the arteries. HYPERLIPIDEMIA
Hyperlipidemia (also sometimes referred to as hypercholester-
olemia) is another way of saying high cholesterol. Elevated blood plasma cholesterol levels can lead to chronic medical problems. Total blood cholesterol levels are ideal when they stay below 180mg/dl (milligrams per deciliter of blood). When the total blood cholesterol level rises above 240 mg/dl a person is diagnosed with hyperlipidemia. High-density lipoproteins (HDL‚Äôs) actually have
a protective effect against coronary artery disease; a level >65mg/dl is considered good even when total cholesterol is slightly elevated. Low-density lipoproteins (LDL‚Äôs) are the end product of triglycer-ide metabolism and are considered the bad guys in the cholesterol group. An LDL level of <100mg/dl is considered good or ‚Äúlow risk.‚Äù206Senior Fitness

Indication for Exercise
Exercise can help to alleviate hyperlipidemia by increasing blood
plasma levels of HDL‚Äôs, the good cholesterol. Recommendations from experts in the field include participating in an exercise pro-gram in conjunction with a reducing dietary fat. There are some people who eat very little dietary fat and foods containing choles-terol but still have hyperlipidemia; however, this is not the norm.
Contraindications
 1. Those people who are significantly deconditioned
should consult a physician prior to beginning an exercise program.
 2. Anyone using a medication that would significantly
decrease exercise capacity should consult a physi-cian prior to beginning an exercise program.
Importance
 1. Reduces blood plasma cholesterol without the use
of drugs.
 2. Improves dietary habits.
 3. Increases exercise capacity.
 4. Reduces risk of chronic diseases associated with hyperlipidemia.
When to Exercise
 1.  Three to five days per week.
 2. When no illness is present.
 3. When exercise can be performed without causing undue fatigue.
 4. At a time of day that is convenient and will pro-mote regular participation.
Precautions
 1.  Stop smoking.
 2. Lose excess body fat and reduce dietary fat.
 3. Limit alcohol intake.
 4. Always consult a physician.Chronic Conditions I: Cardiovascular and Pulmonary Conditions
# Unit 14 207

peripheral vascular dis-
ease (PVD): Also known as peripheral artery disease (PAD) or peripheral artery occlusive disease (PAOD), includes all diseases caused by the obstruction of large arteries in the arms and legs. PVD can result from atherosclerosis, inflam-matory processes leading to stenosis, an embolism, or thrombus formation. It causes either acute or chronic ischemia (lack of blood supply), typically of the legs.
lactic acid: A by-product
of glucose and glycogen metabolism in anaerobic muscle energetics. A min-ute accumulation
causes muscular fatigue and pain, and retards contraction. Lactic acid is carried by the blood to the liver, where it is reconvert-ed to glucose and returned as blood glucose to the muscles. It is this eleva-tion of blood lactic acid in sustained strenuous exer-cise, such as in marathon running, which results in muscle fatigue and pain. Recovery follows when enough oxygen gets to the muscle, part of the lactic acid being oxidized and most of it then being
built up once more into glycogen. PERIPHERAL VASCULAR DISEASE
Peripheral vascular disease (PVD) is a common name shared by
a collection of vascular problems that involve an atherosclerotic narrowing of the vessels. PVD usually involves the occlusion or blockage of large to medium sized arteries of the lower extremities by hardened fatty plaques. PVD is usually associated with other chronic conditions like high cholesterol (hyperlipidemia) or hypertension. Most people with PVD will experience pain during exercise as a result of inadequate blood flow or ischemia. The damage to the ves-sels prevents the necessary amount of oxygen from being delivered to the working muscle. The muscular ischemia results in a painful cramping sensation or claudication in the calves during activity with the feeling subsiding when the activity is stopped. The lack of oxy-gen leads to excessive accumulation of lactic acid and other waste products in the muscle. This is what causes the pain. If the exercise is continued, the build up can eventually cause shortness of breath. If the disease progresses, so does the severity of the symptoms. The complete obstruction of blood flow in an affected vessel can lead to necrosis (death) of the tissues supplied by that vessel.
Indications for Exercise
Although medication and surgery are common treatments for PVD, exercise is recommended to help improve functional change. Most physicians and therapists recommend weight-bearing cardiovascular exercise. Weight bearing exercise may not be tolerated well by some clients with advanced disease, so non-weight bearing exercise can be used to develop some conditioning and tolerance. A progressive pro-gram will eventually ease the client into weight bearing exercise. Always obtain physician‚Äôs concent prior to beginning an exercise program.
Interval training (e.g., alternating two-minute walks with one-min-
ute rests), swimming, stationary cycling, walking on a slow tread-mill at one mph, and chair exercises are all options for persons with PVD. Those who are unable to use the lower extremities due to PVD and claudication can perform chair exercises or upper body exercises; recumbent and non-weight-bearing activities are also good choices. In terms of aerobic activity, a clinical recommenda-208Senior Fitness
tion is for the participant to establish an exercise intensity that is
balanced between his or her target heart rate and moderate discom-fort or pain from which the person‚Äôs attention can be diverted by a number of stimuli such as conversation. This level of discomfort is classified as ‚ÄúGrade II.‚Äù (See Table 14-3) If a participant cannot achieve the minimum target heart rate because it elicits discomfort above Grade II, then lower the target heart rate until Grade II dis-comfort is achieved. Discontinue any exercise that provokes pain at Grade III or above (intense pain from which the person‚Äôs attention cannot be diverted except by catastrophic events).
Training should be performed in intervals, alternating periods of
exercise and periods of rest. An optimal training program of five days per week, maintaining twenty to thirty minutes of cardiovas-cular exercise should be the goal. The intensity and duration of the exercise periods can be increased as the client‚Äôs tolerance level increases. It is useful to use a subjective pain-rating scale like Table 14-3 to help patients rate their pain and become more in tune with their condition. Remember that clients should never be allowed to work beyond a Grade II.Chronic Conditions I: Cardiovascular and Pulmonary Conditions
# Unit 14 209
Grade I Initial Discomfort (established, but minimal)
Grade II Moderate Discomfort (attention can be diverted)Grade III Intense pain (attention can not be diverted)Grade IV Excruciating and Unbearable PainTable 14-3 Subjective Rating of Claudication Discomfort
Source: American College of Sports Medicine Guidelines for Exercise Testing and Prescription
Contraindications
 1.  Do not exercise when claudication pain is present
at rest.
 2. Avoid weight bearing exercise if pain can‚Äôt be kept below a Grade II rating; use a low impact mode  of exercise.
 3. Do not train without a physician‚Äôs consent.
chronic obstructive pul-
monary disease (COPD): Refers to chronic bronchitis and emphysema, a pair of two commonly co-existing diseases of the lungs in which the airways become narrowed. This leads to a limitation of the flow of air to and from the lungs caus-ing shortness of breath.Importance of Exercise
 1.  Improves peripheral circulation without medica-tion.

 2. Improves tolerance to pain.
 3. Improves muscle metabolism and oxygen  utilization.
When to exercise
 1.  At least five days a week is the goal.
 2. When no signs or symptoms of severe occlusion are present.
 3. When medication can be avoided by participation.
 4. At a time of day that is convenient and will pro-mote regular participation.
Precautions
 1.  Most people with PVD also have a high prevalence of heart disease. Watch for signs and symptoms during exercise.
 2. Clients with PVD may experience an abnormal rise in blood pressure due to the compromised vessels.
# Pulmonary Conditions
# Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease
Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) is a term utilized
for both chronic bronchitis and emphysema since most persons will exhibit characteristics of both bronchitis (airway disease) and emphysema (alveolar disease). Chronic bronchitis is generally defined as a condition where a cough develops as a result of an over-secretion of mucus and difficulty expelling air. The overpro-duction of mucus that narrows the airways is caused by chronic irritation of the airways. The most common irritant is cigarette 210Senior Fitness
smoke. Air pollution, secondhand smoke, and exposure to occu-
pational hazardous materials/dusts are among other common irritants. Emphysema is characterized by the abnormal and perma-nent enlargement of air spaces due in part to the destruction of the alveolar walls. The structural damage causes the walls to collapse on exhalation, which leads to the obstruction of airflow.
COPD is diagnosed by pulmonary function testing. The measure-
ment of FEV1, the amount of total lung volume that can be forcibly exhaled in one second, should be above 65 percent. A value that falls below this level is considered a positive diagnosis.
Symptoms
 1. Chronic cough
 2. Increased mucus or sputum production
 3. Wheezing and difficult exhalation
 4. Fever
 5. Increased respiration rateChronic Conditions I: Cardiovascular and Pulmonary Conditions
# Unit 14 211

asthma: A long-term,
reversible respiratory condi-
tion, in which the airways may unexpectedly and suddenly narrow, often in response to an allergen, cold air, exercise, or emo-tional stress. Symptoms include wheezing, short-ness of breath, chest tight-ness, and coughing.Emergency Procedures
 1.  Have an individual sit and attempt to relax
 2. Provide a glass of warm water to break down  the mucus
 3. Remind client of his/her prescription medication  (if applicable)
 4. Seek medical attention
The following information should be in the cli-ent‚Äôs medical file:
 1.  Type and frequency of medication
 2. Possible side effects of drugs
 3. Procedures to follow during an attack
 4. Substances that trigger the attack (allergies).
 5. Detailed record of all attacks
Physical Characteristics of Individuals  with COPD
 1.  Decreased lung capacity
 2. Weak upper back muscles (rhomboids)
 3. Reduced FEV1 during and after cessation  of exercise
# Asthma
Asthma is classified as a reversible (spontaneously or therapeuti-
cally) obstructive airway disease, resulting in the sudden onset of muscle spasms, swelling, and the presence of mucous in the tra-cheobronchial tree. It is caused by a hyperirritability to a variety of stimuli, such as inhalants, ingestants, environment, and exercise. Exercise-induced asthma (EIA) is brought on by sustained exercise such as running or bicycling for longer than six minutes.212Senior Fitness
Symptoms of Asthma
 1.  Cough or hack (due to increased mucus)
 2. Wheezing and dyspnea (difficulty breathing),
leading to difficult exhalation
 3. Severe bronchial obstruction during which the indi-vidual becomes cyanotic, constituting an extreme medical emergency
Emergency Procedures
 1.  Have the client sit and attempt to relax
 2. Provide a glass of warm water to break down the mucus
 3. Remind client of his/her prescription medication  (if applicable)
 4.  If symptoms persist seek medical attentionChronic Conditions I: Cardiovascular and Pulmonary Conditions

# Neurological Disorders
# The Organization Of The Brain
In order to understand a client with a brain injury, it is critical
to understand the basic functional components of the brain. The brain is the control board for all of the body‚Äôs functions, including thinking, moving, and breathing. It receives messages, interprets them, and then responds to them by enabling the person to speak, move, or show emotion.
The brain is protected by a thick layer of bone (the skull) and is
surrounded by cerebrospinal fluid that allows the brain to ‚Äúfloat‚Äù slightly within the skull. This fluid also fills the open areas within the brain (the ventricles).
The brain is comprised of the following:
 ‚Ä¢ Cortex - most thinking functions occur
 ‚Ä¢ Cerebellum - coordinates movement
 ‚Ä¢ Brain Stem - controls consciousness, alertness, and
basic bodily functions such as breathing, respira-tion, and pulse.
The cortex is the largest part of the brain and is divided into four
‚Äúlobes,‚Äù each of which specializes in particular functions and skills.
 ‚Ä¢ Frontal lobe - emotional control, motivation,
social functioning, expressive language, inhibi
-
tion of impulses, motor integration, and voluntary movement.
 ‚Ä¢ Temporal lobe - memory, reception, language, sequencing, and musical awareness.
 ‚Ä¢ Parietal lobe - sensation, academic skills such as reading, and awareness of spatial relationships.
 ‚Ä¢ Occipital lobe - visual perception.218Senior Fitness
The cortex is divided into two hemispheres.
The dominant hemisphere (usually the left hemisphere) controls verbal functions includ-ing speaking, writing, reading, and calculating. The right hemisphere generally controls func-tions that are more visual-spatial in nature like visual memory, copying, drawing, and rhythm.
Individuals with head injuries often show
changes in personality and emotional control regardless of which side the injury occurred.
# Acquired Brain Injury
One of the most challenging aspects of being a fitness educator of older adults is addressing neurologic dysfunction. The focus of this sec-tion will be on Acquired Brain Injury (ABI). Very few disabilities present the vast combi-nation of cognitive, physical, psychosocial, and emotional problems that ABI does. ABI is a fancy term that means that the individual suffered an injury to the brain from either an internal or external source and that this insult to the brain occurred after maturation. As you can imagine, the causes are numerous and varied.
Causes of ABI
Some of the most common causes are (1) head injury from a fall or accident, and (2) cerebrovascular accident (CVA) which in every-day language means ‚Äúa stroke.‚Äù Other common causes include drug overdoses, brain tumors, and hypoxia (a deficiency of oxygen in the tissues). While it is important to understand the cause and pathology of ABI, it is critical to understand that damage to the parietal lobe of the brain will be the same regardless of the cause. If you plan to work with the ABI individuals, it would be wise to study the basics of brain pathology. (The textbook, Adapted Physical Education for Adults with Disabilities, contains a useful section on ABI by Lasko/Knopf; for more information, call FEOAA.).Chronic Conditions II: Neurological and Metabolic Conditions

Definition of Acquired Brain Injury
An acquired impairment of brain function resulting in the loss of
cognitive, motor, linguistic, psychosocial, sensory, and perceptual abilities that may display dysfunction in the following domains:
Cognitive: Impairment of memory function, attention, concentra-
tion, judgment, organizational thinking skills, spatial orientation, and information processing.
Psychomotor: Involvement of neuromotor system. Impairment of
motor tone, balance, coordination, and strength.Linguistic: Impairment of speech, language, and related commu-
nication skills.
Psychosocial: Impulsiveness, disinhibition, denial, poor social
judgment, emotional disability, initiation, and in some cases untoward social behavior.
Sensory/Perceptual: Visual, auditory, and tactile perceptual deficits.
Other disorders that are secondary to head injury are:
 1. Spasticity (flexion and extension synergies)
 2. Joint Contractures
 3. Bowel and Bladder Dysfunction
 4. Pneumonia
 5. Seizures
 6. Depression
 7. Loss of Judgment
 8. Headaches
 9. Memory Loss
 10. Impulsive Behavior220Senior Fitness
aphasia: An inability to
interpret or execute spoken language (receptive and expressive, respectively) which is not related to dis-eases of the vocal cords or ears.Many characteristics are common among persons with injuries on
the same side of the head. Damage to the right side of the brain often results in impaired perceptions and paralysis on the left side of the body (i.e., left hemiplegia). Persons with injury to the left side of the brain manifest problems in communication related to speech, comprehension, reading, writing, along with paralysis of the right side of the body.
Many times the head-injured person will suffer from aphasia.
This condition results in the inability to comprehend spoken language (receptive aphasia) and/or execute speech (expressive aphasia). These symptoms are due to a lesion in the auditory cor-tex of the brain.
Cerebral Vascular Accident (Stroke)
Strokes are the third leading cause of death and a leading cause of disability in the United States. Men experience strokes more fre-quently than women and more African Americans incur strokes than Caucasians do. The incidence of stroke increases dramatically with age and doubles with every decade after age 55. Because there are approximately three million people alive in the U.S. who have incurred a stroke, it is essential that fitness educators of older adults have some working knowledge of how to teach this population.
Due to the large number of clients enrolled in adaptive physical
fitness programs who have had a stroke, it is very important to understand as much as possible about this neurological disorder, and how best to assist them in regaining normal function.
A stroke occurs when a blood vessel bringing oxygen and nutri-
ents to parts of the brain bursts or becomes clogged. When that happens, the nerve cells in that part of the brain die, resulting in a disturbance to the opposite side of the body. A right-sided stroke in the brain results in left-sided paralysis, known as ‚Äúhemiplegia.‚ÄùChronic Conditions II: Neurological and Metabolic Conditions
# Unit 15 221
hemiplegia: One sided
paralysis.
The nerve cells of the brain control how we receive and interpret
sensations. They also control most of our body movements. When some nerve cells in the brain cannot function, then the part of the body controlled by them cannot function either. This can result in difficulty speaking, inability to walk, loss of memory, lack of bowel or urinary control, or other symptoms. The effects of a stroke may be very slight or severe, temporary or permanent. This depends on which brain cells have been damaged, how widespread the damage is, and how quickly other areas of brain tissue take over the work of the damaged cells.
Spasticity (abnormal increases in muscle tone) interferes with the
ability to move normally. Spasticity develops insidiously a few days after the stroke has occurred and gradually replaces the initial com-plete paralysis of the muscles of the affected side of the body.
A newborn has little control over his body, and his movements
are dictated by reflexes, referred to as primitive reflexes. These reflexes are with us throughout our lives. As the brain develops, it overrides, or inhibits, these reflexes. When a person suffers brain damage, these primitive reflexes are considered abnormal when they re-emerge. Careful positioning, as well as encouragement to use the affected limbs in a functional manner throughout all daily activities, can help to prevent or inhibit spasticity (tone) and abnor-mal primitive reflexes. This will help to ensure that the likelihood of regaining normal functional movement is maximized.
Research shows that the stroke survivor can benefit from contin-
ued exercise even after his or her initial rehabilitation. Therefore, fitness educators of older adults will be seeing more and more stroke survivors in their classes and should be prepared to meet the demands of these clients. Returning to the community and to mainstream life can be difficult for survivors and family alike. Family dynamics will change. Issues arising after the return home include safety, fall prevention, health promotion, and appropriate leisure time activities. Participating in a well-designed exercise class that is therapeutic in nature can contribute greatly to the stroke survivor‚Äôs physical and mental health.222Senior Fitness
Recommendations for Exercise
Often, persons with ABI are resistant to physical activity, expecting
that it will aggravate headaches, cause dizziness, or fatigue them. Generally, exercise will not aggravate any such conditions. It is rec-ommended that the individual spend at least 30 to 60 minutes daily participating in activities that will develop muscular strength and endurance, cardiovascular endurance, balance, and coordination. Small-group activities teaching balance and range-of-motion have been found to be successful and beneficial, providing that progress is monitored regularly. The time spent partaking in exercise, swim-ming, and/or sports activities is not only valuable physically, but is also psychologically stimulating.
Goals of the Exercise Routine
A well-designed program will focus on trying to reduce contrac-tures and improve functional fitness skills. It would be prudent to improve the ABI client‚Äôs ability to perform weight shifts, in addition to maintaining balance and coordination. It is unwise to Chronic Conditions II: Neurological and Metabolic Conditions

Range-of-motion exercises that were prescribed by the client‚Äôs

Muscular Strength: This area is still controversial, depending on
the physical therapist‚Äôs frame of reference. Some therapists who utilize the Bobath approach maintain that strength training caus-es spasms and contractures. Other therapists believe that in order to maintain greater independence, one must develop enough strength to meet the demands of daily living. Any loss of muscle strength will manifest itself in a greater loss of functional ability. (Always consult the client‚Äôs physician and physical therapist). If your client notices loss of flexibility, either decrease the amount of strength training (if indicated), or spend more time participat-ing in stretching, range-of-motion activities. Also, be mindful of increases in blood pressure!
Balance/Coordination: For persons with a head injury, a signifi-
cant portion of time should be devoted to safe and functional bal-
ance activities. Always make sure your clients are safe when doing balance exercises.
Cardiovascular Exercise: This activity is not only useful to increase
aerobic capacity; it helps maintain proper blood pressure. Aerobic exercise also encourages reciprocal movements of the arms and legs, which helps coordination. Always consult the client‚Äôs phy-sician prior to incorporating aerobic exercise and ask for specific guidelines relevant to work load, intensity, and training heart rates.224Senior Fitness
Parkinson‚Äôs Disease: A
degenerative disorder of the central nervous system that often impairs the suf-ferer‚Äôs motor skills, speech, and other functions.PARKINSON‚ÄôS DISEASE
Parkinson‚Äôs Disease usually occurs between the ages of 40 to 80.
Parkinson‚Äôs is caused by damage to specific regions of the brain. Symptoms appear over a period of months to years. Parkinson‚Äôs Disease affects motor function but not cognitive abilities. The pri-mary characteristics include:
 ‚Ä¢ Muscle rigidity and weakness
 ‚Ä¢ Gait/walking changes, as seen in shuffling
 ‚Ä¢ Posture change, as witnessed by hunched-over
posture
 ‚Ä¢ Hand tremors, present at rest
Exercise is very helpful for persons with Parkinson‚Äôs Disease.
Exercise not only improves their physical health, it improves their emotional health. Exercise allows them to feel good about the fact that they are actively having a positive influence on the disease. Exercise allows people to gain self-esteem, which may lead to expanding social contacts. Many communities have Parkinson‚Äôs support groups that provide exercise classes specifically designed for persons with the disease.Chronic Conditions II: Neurological and Metabolic Conditions
# Unit 15 225

Indicated Exercise Program
 ‚Ä¢ Emphasis should be placed on stretching the tight
muscles.
 ‚Ä¢ Focus should be placed on mild strength-training exercises done slowly and under control, facilitating coordination and strength.
 ‚Ä¢ Always do each exercise through the complete
range of motion to keep the joint supple.
 ‚Ä¢ Work on breathing exercises to keep lungs as healthy as possible.
 ‚Ä¢ A well-designed and supervised water exercise pro-gram can be helpful. Look for a class where the water is as warm as possible to encourage flexibility
and reduce spasticity.
Exercise Precautions
 ‚Ä¢ Do not allow the client to become fatigued. Teach
clients to start slowly and listen to their bodies. It is OK to back off if necessary.
 ‚Ä¢ Work closely with the client‚Äôs physical therapist and
physician regarding the exercise plan.226Senior Fitness

# Exercises For The
# Neurologically Impaired
Arm/Hand Stretch
Starting Position: Sitting. Assistant sits on affected side.
Movement: Assistant straightens affected elbow, wrist, and fingers,
holding this position as long as tolerated. If thumb is stretched out
first, hand and fingers will open more easily.
Elbow Flexion/Extension
Starting Position: Lying on back, affected shoulder flexed to ninety degrees, elbow straight. Assistant should stabilize upper arm in this position, supporting at the hand and the elbow.
Movement: Bend elbow, bringing hand toward opposite shoulder,
then straighten. Assistant may help to complete each movement
only as needed. With improvement, hand may be brought to mouth, chin, nose, etc. This is a slow process, requiring patience.
Weight-Bearing on Forearms
Starting Position: Lying on stomach. Place forearms under chest. May clasp or cross hands to keep in place.
Movement: Push up and support weight on forearms, keeping
shoulders directly in line over elbows. Shift weight toward affected
side, holding to tolerance. Attempt to place affected hand flat on mat with fingers outstretched to reduce tone.
Weight-Bearing on Hands and Knees
Starting Position: Lying on stomach, bearing weight on forearms, affected hand flat.
Movement: Lift hips off of mat (assistant may have to help with this),
and rise up onto hands and knees. Attempt to straighten elbow, shift-
ing weight toward affected side. With practice, you can shift weight forward and backward. Attempt to bend and straighten elbows.Chronic Conditions II: Neurological and Metabolic Conditions
# Unit 15 227
Pelvic Mobility
Starting Position: On hands and knees.
Movement: Isolated movement of pelvis: move buttocks side to side.
Trunk Stabilization
Starting Position: On hands and knees.Movement: Assistant applies resistance against outside of one
shoulder and side of opposite hip. Client pushes against both
points of resistance at the same time.
Prone Knee Bends
Starting Position: Lying on stomach.
Movement: Slowly bend knee, bringing heel toward buttock. Bring
the leg to a ninety-degree angle, and stabilize it in this position. Hold as long as tolerated, and then slowly lower leg back down. Assistant may provide support as needed.
Achilles Tendon Stretch
Starting Position: Stand with support. Step forward with stronger leg, keeping affected leg back.
Movement: Bend the front leg and lean for-
ward until a stretch is felt in the calf of the
rear leg. Hold as tolerated (up to 60 seconds).
Heel Slides
Starting Position: Lying on back.
Movement: Bend and then straighten the
affected hip and knee, always keeping the
heel in contact with the mat. Perform a slow, controlled motion
throughout the stretch.228Senior Fitness
Figure 15-1 Achilles Tendon Stretch
Standing Up
Starting Position: Scoot buttocks to front edge of chair. Place feet
apart with stronger leg back and under you. Clasp hands together, fingers interlaced, and reach forward with the arms.
Movement: Lean forward, with body weight balanced equally over
both legs. Stand up straight.
Weight-Shifting, Forward and Backward
Starting Position: Stand with support. Step forward with affected
leg, keeping stronger leg back.
Movement: Bend front leg, shifting weight forward; hold as tolerat-
ed, then shift weight backward. Repeat several times.
Weight-Shifting, Side-to-Side
Starting Position: Stand with support, feet evenly apart with toes pointing forward.
Movement: Shift weight onto affected leg; hold as tolerated, then
shift weight onto other leg. Repeat several times.
# Metabolic Disorders
# Diabetes
Diabetes mellitus is a metabolic disease in which the body is
unable to use food properly. It is caused by an insufficient supply of insulin. Insulin is produced in the pancreas. The function of insulin is to regulate the rate at which the body uses or stores sugar. Much of the food we eat is converted into a form of sugar called glucose or stored as glycogen. Glycogen is stored in the liver and muscles and may be broken down into glucose when needed. Insulin is needed by the body to burn glucose, which provides energy for the muscles. If the pancreas fails to produce enough insulin, glucose cannot be used by the muscles or stored, so glucose accumulates in the bloodstream and causes blood sugar levels to rise above normal. Two signs of diabetes are excessive amounts of sugar in the blood (hyperglycemia) and excretion of sugar in the urine (glycosuria).Chronic Conditions II: Neurological and Metabolic Conditions
# Unit 15 229
diabetes mellitus: A
group of disorders of
the body‚Äôs carbohydrate (sugar) metabolism charac-terized by elevated blood glucose (blood sugar) levels and glucose in the urine resulting from inadequate production or use of insulin (eg hyperglycemia).
The symptoms of diabetes are related to the increased amount of
sugar in the blood. Diabetes may be without symptoms; such may be the case with elderly weakness. Other symptoms include pain, numbness, tingling in the hands and feet, disturbances in vision, irri-tability, and nervousness. In children, the classic symptoms manifest themselves, as weight loss, polyuria, polydipsia, and fatigue.
There are two types of diabetes mellitus: Type I and Type II. Type
I is insulin-dependent diabetes (formerly called juvenile diabetes), where the body‚Äôs immune system destroys insulin-producing cells, and insulin needs to be injected into the bloodstream.
Type II is known as non-insulin-dependent diabetes (formerly
called adult-onset diabetes), where the body does not produce enough insulin. Type II diabetes is the type you will encounter most frequently in older adults. With Type II, diet, exercise, and oral medications usually work well, so insulin injections are not needed to control blood glucose levels.  230Senior Fitness

Regular exercise can help a person with Type II diabetes to stabilize
the condition by losing excess fat and weight, decreasing the need for insulin, lowering cholesterol, and avoiding further complica-tions. Always consult your client‚Äôs physician for specific recom-mendations and precautions relevant to his or her condition.
Diabetes can lead to:
 1. Cardiovascular Disorders: A person with diabetes
is two times more likely to develop Coronary

Artery Disease.
 2. Macrovascular Disease: Diabetes contributes to  the development of vascular problems requiring amputation.
 3. Microvascular Disease: Diabetic retinopathy results when tiny blood vessels in the retina break and cause little hemorrhages on or near the retina. (See Unit 17 for more on diabetic retinopathy.) Cataracts also seem to be more prevalent in per-sons with diabetes.
 4. Neuropathy: Destruction of nerves caused by excessive blood glucose and poor blood supply. (See Unit 17 for more on neuropathy.)
 5. Diabetic Nephropathy: Chronically high blood sugar overworks the kidneys and causes them to stop working properly.
# Treatment
Diabetes cannot be cured, but can be controlled. Treatment may
include medication (insulin), daily exercise, and diet therapy. Exercise in consistent amounts is very important in managing Type II diabetes. Blood glucose levels should be checked prior to exercise to determine any adjustments in food intake. If blood sugar levels are 300 mg or greater, do not begin exercise. Table 15-2 summarizes guidelines for adjusting food intake according to blood sugar levels. This table is for reference only. The Specialist in Senior Fitness credential does not qualify you to act as a Registered
Dietitian or Nutritionist, nor does it qualify you to dispense med-ical nutrition therapy.Chronic Conditions II: Neurological and Metabolic Conditions

insulin for when exercising

 ‚Ä¢ Exercise at predictable times to minimize blood sugar fluctuation
 ‚Ä¢ Exercise heart rates should be between 50% and 70% of maximum heart rate

UNIT SUMMARYExercise with Renal Nephropathy
Because of the many complications of diabetes and renal disease,
thorough screening prior to the initiation of an exercise program is essential. No exercise program should begin until the person is sta-bilized on a therapeutic program of medication, dialysis, and diet. Individuals on hemodialysis benefit from aerobic exercise such as brisk walking, cycling, and swimming. The program should begin gradually due to the likelihood of anemia, cardiovascular dysfunc-tion, and low physical work capacity, using an interval training program (five minutes work, five minutes rest). Intensity of exer-cise should range from 60 to 80 percent of the person‚Äôs maximum heart rate. Fluid replacement is warranted if exercise is performed in heat or following dialysis treatment.

a fall or accident, and (2) cerebrovascular accident (CVA) also known as a stroke.
a.
 Research shows that the stroke survivor can benefit
from continued exercise even after his or her initial rehabilitation. Therefore, fitness educators of older adults will be seeing more and more stroke survivors in their classes or appointment books and should be pre-pared to meet the demands of these clients.
b.
 It is recommended that the individual spend at least 30 to 60 minutes daily participating in activities that will develop muscular strength and endurance, car-diovascular endurance, balance, and coordination. A well-designed program will focus on trying to reduce contractures and improve functional fitness skills.
c.
 The water is a great place to work on recuperating skills

This unit will focus on orthopedic issues which, for a variety of rea-
sons, tend to occur frequently in older adults. As in the last unit, the pathology of numerous conditions will be explained, and the ways in which exercise can influence outcome will be examined.

# Arthritis
Arthritis is one of the most common conditions that you will encounter while working with an older clientele. More than 36 mil-lion people in the United States have some form of arthritis. Arthritis
literally means inflammation of
a joint. The term arthritis is used to describe over one hun-dred joint/connective tissue disorders. Several of the most common forms of arthritis are osteoarthritis, rheumatoid arthritis, and gout. Sometimes fibromyalgia is also listed as an arthritic condition.
# Osteoarthritis
Osteoarthritis is caused by the breakdown of the smooth hya-line cartilage inside the joint. Without a smooth articulating
surface movement in or about the joint, movement becomes pain-ful. Osteoarthritis is commonly referred to as the wear-and-tear arthritis because it is frequently seen in the weight-bearing joints such as the knees, hips, ankles, and spine. The prognosis is usually good; the condition progresses slowly and usually is not crip-pling. The use of medications and gentle exercise is very helpful. (Osteoarthritis is a less severe inflammatory condition than rheu-matoid arthritis.)
RHEUMATOID ARTHRITIS (RA)
Rheumatoid arthritis is a systemic autoimmune condition. RA often begins before the age of forty. Symptoms include fatigue, tenderness on or around joints, inflammation, pain, stiffness, and swelling. This condition follows an unpredictable course of exac-erbations and remissions. Many persons with RA notice a loss in appetite, sometimes fever, and the development of rheumatoid nodules under the skin. This condition entails much more than simple joint pain; it is much more like a chronic illness.238Senior Fitness

uric acid: A byproduct
of the purine metabolism
which in its turn is a prod-uct of nucleic acid break-down. The majority of uric acid is synthesized in the liver. Elevated levels of uric acid (hyperuricemia) and its salts are associated with formation of urate crystals causing gout and kidney stones.GOUT
Gout is caused by a metabolic disorder that allows uric acid to
accumulate in the blood. Crystals of uric acid are deposited within the joints, causing inflammation. The most common site is the big toe, but other joints can be affected. Gout is very painful. People used to believe that only the affluent, like kings and queens, suf-fered from this condition; unfortunately, that is simply not true.
No matter what type of arthritis your client has, one of the major
problems is combating the chronic pain cycle. When moving a joint causes pain, there is a tendency to not move the joint. This causes more stiffness and weakness, which ultimately leads to less ability and desire to move and, consequently, more disability. This downward cycle must be broken, and sensible exercise is a major component in reversing it. Today, most enlightened therapists and doctors recognize the importance of sensible and regular exercise in the care of arthritis. Research now shows that if you can increase the strength of muscles around the affected joint, these muscles will aid in supporting the load placed on that joint.Chronic Conditions III: Orthopedic Conditions
# Unit 16 239

Reasons Exercise Is Important for People
with Arthritis
 ‚Ä¢ Increases strength and flexibility around joints
 ‚Ä¢ Helps maintain or increase bone strength
 ‚Ä¢ Provides nourishment and lubrication to joints
 ‚Ä¢ Prevents disuse syndrome
 ‚Ä¢ Provides feelings of control and self-worth
How Often and When Should Exercise  Be Performed?
 ‚Ä¢ On a daily basis
 ‚Ä¢ When there is the least amount of pain and  stiffness
 ‚Ä¢ When the individual is least tired
 ‚Ä¢ When the individual receives the maximum benefit from medications during the day (generally 10:00 to 11:00 a.m.). However, instruct client not to over-medicate to mask pain.
Important: Prepare for exercise by thermally warming up the
body: use massage, a heating pad, a warm shower, or gentle rhyth-
mic movements.
Precautions
 ‚Ä¢ Find out what type of arthritis the client has.
 ‚Ä¢ Minimize stress to joints. Learn about appropriate
activities such as swimming, water exercise, and walking.
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid vigorously exercising an inflamed joint; rather, gently put the joint through its range of motion.
 ‚Ä¢ Do not allow the client to increase medications without his or her physician‚Äôs approval.240Senior Fitness
 ‚Ä¢ Have client wear warm clothes and peel off layers.
 ‚Ä¢ Modify the exercise routine depending on how the
client feels.
Contraindications
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid positions of extreme weight-bearing flexion or hyperextension.
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid jarring movements or quick directional changes.
 ‚Ä¢ Never exercise a ‚Äúhot‚Äù joint‚Äîa joint that is actively inflamed and causing severe pain.
 ‚Ä¢ Never allow client to mask pain with medication.
Indicated Exercise Program
For persons with osteoarthritis, proper body weight is paramount.
Obesity accelerates the damage to diseased weight-bearing joints; thus a program of walking, water exercise, or mild stationary biking, in combination with limiting the intake of food can help facilitate the goal of losing weight. Be sure to teach proper body mechanics. Researchers, therapists, and doctors now realize that exercise plays an important role in the health of people with arthri-tis, and as trainers our job is to help our clients get the greatest benefit possible from their training.
The Goals of an Exercise Program for
People with Arthritis Should Include:
 ‚Ä¢ Using non-weight-bearing activities like swimming,
water exercise, chair series, and stationary biking
 ‚Ä¢ Always doing a thermal warm-up for five to ten minutes prior to exercising, and always stretching at the end of the program for five to ten minutes
 ‚Ä¢ Doing exercises that improve functional fitness, e.g., chair squat/leg curb squats
 ‚Ä¢ Not ‚Äúmaking‚Äù pain. Always follow sound biome-chanical techniques, and encourage the client to listen to his or her body. If it hurts, don‚Äôt quit; just back off!Chronic Conditions III: Orthopedic Conditions
# Unit 16 241
FIBROMYALGIA SYNDROME (FMS)
FMS is a chronic and often disabling condition, characterized by
widespread body pain and severe fatigue. Many times it is accom-panied by other problems such as irritable bowel, headache, and sleep difficulties. Other conditions that many times are grouped into this same category are chronic fatigue syndrome and myofasical pain. Persons with fibromyalgia suffer from chronic pain that is often very debilitating. A person suffer-ing from chronic pain not only suffers from the pain itself, but also suffers psycho-logically. Loss of self-esteem, which leads to depression and isolation, often triggers more pain that leads to further dis-ability; a vicious downward spiral of pain and disability can be the result.
# Chronic Pain Cycle
For exercise to be effective for persons with chronic muscle pain, these persons must first recognize that proper exercise is critical to maintaining the strength and mobility that will allow them to func-tion more fully. As good as that sounds, a person with chronic pain is often afraid to exercise, for fear that the pain will be exacerbated. The first key to a sensible exercise program is to start very, very slowly! If the person can do minimal exercise without significantly increasing the pain level, that is real progress.
Indicated Exercise Program for FMS
 ‚Ä¢ A person with any chronic painful condition must
be active in his or her own care. The more time and effort clients take to learn how they react to exercise, the better will be their progress. Clients 242Senior Fitness

need to learn to listen to their body and heed what
it says.
 ‚Ä¢ Learning and maintaining proper body mechanics is key. Using proper body mechanics while per-forming activities will help to prevent further pain.
 ‚Ä¢ Learning to relax periodically throughout the day is also important. This will help to break the cycle of muscle tension that often increases the pain level.
 ‚Ä¢ The individual should stretch often to keep muscles supple and flexible.
 ‚Ä¢ An effort should be made to increase the client‚Äôs aerobic fitness. Use low- or no-impact activities such as walking or even strolling at first, and then increase the pace, as the person feels bet-ter. Stationary biking is useful‚Äîmany people like recumbent bikes because they can sit and be more comfortable. Swimming and water exercise are also good ways to get fit! (Try to find a warm therapeu
-
tic pool). Always stretch after the exercise session.
 ‚Ä¢ Encourage the client to learn techniques to man-age pain such as heat, ice, or whirlpool baths. Therapists may employ such things as electrical stimulation and ultrasound. The client should not purchase over-the-counter devices without the physician‚Äôs approval, however.
 ‚Ä¢ Make staying as fit as possible the goal. Research shows that conditioning muscles through mild to moderate aerobic exercise improves the symptoms of FMS. Of course, start very slowly! Start with a one- to three-minute walk followed by one to three minutes of stretching. If the pain is tolerable, then add two to five minutes each week of additional walking and continue to progress slowly until you can do three bouts of ten minutes, with the goal of thirty minutes of non-stop walking.
 ‚Ä¢ Set clients up to be successful. Establish goals that they can achieve, and when they surpass them, it will improve their self-esteem.
 ‚Ä¢ Don‚Äôt ever overdo it!
 ‚Ä¢ Don‚Äôt mask pain with pills and drugs.Chronic Conditions III: Orthopedic Conditions
# Unit 16 243
# Neck Problems
Many adults experience a loss in flexibility of the neck as they
age. Often, neck pain is the result of poor body mechanics when seated or lying. Other neck problems result from arthritis of the upper spine or disc problems. Home exercise programs are a crit-ical component for persons with cervical strains and other mus-culoskeletal conditions of the neck. Many times a conservative approach of exercises and improved body mechanics, along with heat packs and recommendations from the client‚Äôs physician can result in significant improvement.
Contraindications
People with neck problems should avoid movements that hyper-extend the neck and thus aggravate neck problems that can further cause impingement of the neck arteries that supply blood to the brain. Reduced blood flow to the brain can result in serious med-ical problems.
Activities to Avoid
 ‚Ä¢ Fast or jerky neck movements‚Äînever do these!
 ‚Ä¢ Sitting with head too far forward
 ‚Ä¢ Lying down on the sofa or bed with head propped
up high on a pillow
 ‚Ä¢ Head circles
Recommendations
 ‚Ä¢ Always follow the physician‚Äôs advice
 ‚Ä¢ Try doing gentle range-of-motion exercises in  the shower or with warm, moist towels around

the neck
 ‚Ä¢ Start very, very slowly
 ‚Ä¢ When sitting, sit up straight periodically or get up and walk around
 ‚Ä¢ Always avoid the ‚Äúturtleneck‚Äù position where the head is forward244Senior Fitness
Indicated Exercise Program
 ‚Ä¢ Turn head left to right
 ‚Ä¢ Empire State Building Watch ‚Äîlook up and
down
 ‚Ä¢ Shoulder Blade Squeeze ‚Äîpull shoulder blades
together and hold and repeat
 ‚Ä¢ Head tilts ‚Äîtilt head from left to right
 ‚Ä¢ Shrug shoulders and make circles backwardsChronic Conditions III: Orthopedic Conditions
