# Low Back Problems
If you live long enough, you are likely to suffer from some form
of back problem. Over 80 percent of Americans have experienced backache. Back problems rank second to the common cold for time loss from work.
Dr. Herring of the Puget Sound Sports & Rehabilitative Center
believes that only about 1 percent of patients with back problems require surgery. For the other 99 percent of us with chronic low-er-back problems, this means that sensible exercise and proper body mechanics could lessen our pain. A person who has had a backache is ten times more likely to have another episode. Therefore, an exercise program that includes flexibility exercises, general overall conditioning, and abdominal strengthening, along with condition-ing of the muscles that stabilize the low-back area, is critical.
There are many causes of back pain, from tumors and disc ruptures
to poor body mechanics to facet joint problems, not to mention muscle spasms. Before starting any exercise program, it is wise to consult the client‚Äôs physician for a proper diagnosis and specific exercise recommendations. If the client experiences leg pain and backache, along with the following, see a physician immediately:
 ‚Ä¢ High fever
 ‚Ä¢ Loss of bowel and bladder function
 ‚Ä¢ Rapid weight loss
 ‚Ä¢ Back pain lasting more than three days
 ‚Ä¢ Numbness in the pelvis and extreme weakness in
the leg
A sound exercise program is designed to provide support and
strength to the spine and to develop a better awareness of proper body mechanics. Trunk stabilization is essential for preventing injury to the lower back while performing any movement or daily activity. Many of these exercises are also suggested for any person who is recovering from a back injury. Always begin these exercis-246Senior Fitness
es slowly and carefully, e.g., begin by holding for a count of five,
allowing the muscles to loosen up gradually. Don‚Äôt be alarmed if the exercises cause some mild discomfort, which lasts a few min-utes. However, stop doing any exercise that causes pain until you have checked with your doctor.
Common Sense Ways to Reduce Back Pain
 ‚Ä¢ Make sure home and car are set up to minimize
further pain
 ‚Ä¢ Have knees higher than hips when sitting
 ‚Ä¢ Move driver‚Äôs seat closer to steering wheel
 ‚Ä¢ Tighten abdominal muscles whenever you are about to lift anything
 ‚Ä¢ Use leg muscles when lifting by bending the knees. Keep the object close to the body. If you must bend to reach the object, bend at the hip joint.
 ‚Ä¢ Do flexibility exercises daily
 ‚Ä¢ Do back exercises regularly
 ‚Ä¢ Never twist forcefully
 ‚Ä¢ Push, don‚Äôt pull‚Äîbetter yet, ask for help!
 ‚Ä¢ Strengthen core muscles of torso
Tips for Proper Standing
 ‚Ä¢ Stand with weight evenly distributed over left and right foot
 ‚Ä¢ Stand with weight evenly distributed over front and back of feet
 ‚Ä¢ Keep the ears over the shoulders and chin parallel to floor
 ‚Ä¢ Keep knees soft (do not lock knees)
 ‚Ä¢ Keep abs and glutes firm to support the low backChronic Conditions III: Orthopedic Conditions
# Unit 16 247
# Shoulder Problems
The design of the shoulder is remarkable: it allows baseball pitchers
to throw baseballs at 90 mph, it allows us to reach and grab things high or low, as well as to hug our loved ones. Shoulder problems can be the result of many different causes, such as osteoarthritis, trauma, bursitis, tendinitis, or may even be idiopathic (i.e., having no known cause). Many times the corrective exercises are the same, regardless of the cause. Prior to doing these exercises, make sure that the physician has approved them.
# The Rotator Cuff
The shoulder is a ball-and-socket joint much like the hip joint, except that the hip joint is a much deeper joint and thereby pro-vides a lot more support (although with more limited mobility.) The shoulder joint is a flatter and smaller joint. If you can visualize a golf tee with a golf ball on it, you begin to sense the design of the shoulder joint. While this design affords us a great deal of move-ment, we pay the price for it with a joint that is at risk for misuse and abuse. Spring is the time of year you begin to hear a lot about shoulder problems‚Äîbaseball is in full swing (pun intended), folks are returning to lap swimming, and recreational tennis players are doing all those overhead smashes.
Some experts believe that when you move your arm, as many as twenty-six muscles are engaged in the movement. Go to any gym and you will see men and women doing all kinds of over-head presses to build up the ‚Äúshow‚Äù muscles. However, they often neglect the very important SITS muscles. The rotator cuff musculature is made up of these SITS muscles. Research suggests that the rotator cuff muscles display the greatest electrical activity during the eccentric phase of the follow-through of major arm movements.248Senior Fitness

Causes of Rotator Cuff Injuries
Three major contributors to rotator cuff problems are described below:
 ‚Ä¢ Age: The risk for a rotator cuff injury increases with
age. Normal wear and tear, an increase in calcium
deposits within the joint, and bone spurs can irritate the rotator cuff.
 ‚Ä¢ Trauma:  An injury to the shoulder‚Äîwhether it is from
a fall or any other kind of trauma‚Äîis another source of problems for the shoulder joint.
 ‚Ä¢ Overhead activities:  The outcome is likely to be the
same whether the excessive overhead activity stems from playing too much tennis or simply from lifting too many boxes. Be especially careful in water exercise classes where there are often a lot of overhead move-ments and activities that involve bringing the arms out from under the water.Chronic Conditions III: Orthopedic Conditions
# Unit 16 249
Figure 16-1  The ‚ÄúSITS‚Äù MusclesT
Corrective Exercises
After a trained medical professional has made a diagnosis and has
given your client permission to exercise, the following exercises might be appropriate. Many times, rest is the best thing for your client to do for the first couple of days. However, remind him or her not to allow the joint to freeze up. Six basic shoulder exercises are described below.
The V Soda Can Lift
Starting Position: Stand erect with arms alongside the body.
Movement: Then, as if holding a soda can with the thumbs facing
down, slowly lift the arms outwards as if pouring the soda on the floor. Raise the arms to the height of the shoulders with hands slightly behind the back.
Internal Rotator
Starting Position: Stand erect with the elbow bent, the elbow and upper arm against the body, and the hand straight ahead.
Movement: Then slowly allow the hand to move toward the abdo-
men. As the client improves, resistance can be added (via a hand paddle in the pool or an elastic exercise band on land).
External Rotator
Starting Position: Have the client stand as in the internal rotator exercise just described.
Movement: Assume the same basic position, except this time slow-
ly allow the hand to move outward and then return to the starting position.
Forward Cane Lift
Starting Position: Standing upright with optimal posture, use both hands to hold onto a towel or broom handle. (The towel should 250Senior Fitness
be in a horizontal position across the front of the body; the hands
should be placed a comfortable distance apart with the palms up).
Movement: Slowly lift the arms as high as possible without arching
the back or causing pain. Then return to starting position.
Rear Cane Lift
Starting Position: Standing erect, hold onto a dowel or broom han-dle behind the buttocks.
Movement: Straighten both arms and very slowly lift them as far
away from the buttocks as is possible to do comfortably.
Choker Stretch
Starting Position: Stand erect with the arms out at 90Àö angle to
the body.
Movement: Gently pull the arm across the front of the body until
a pleasant stretch is felt in the back of the arm and in the shoulder muscles. Chronic Conditions III: Orthopedic Conditions
# Unit 16 251

# Hip Problems
The hip joint is the workhorse of the lower body, and while it is able
to move in many different directions, it is also designed for bearing weight. The hip joint is referred to as a ball-and-socket joint. The joint structure is built for stabili-ty, and it gains additional stabil-ity from the muscles positioned around it.
Hip problems are caused by
unequal leg lengths, hip mus-cle imbalances, and poor body mechanics, as well as by the cumu-lative effects of overuse, misuse, and arthritis. Many adults who have suffered from pain and dis-ability may opt for a total hip replacement after more conserva-tive approaches have failed. As with all exercises, it is prudent to always let the physician and/or physical therapist know what exer-cises you have planned and get their approval and input, especially after hip-replacement surgery.
Contraindications
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid full flexion, i.e., deep squats or pulling knee
to the chest.
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid crossing legs when exercising or moving either leg past mid-line.
Recommendations
 ‚Ä¢ Because a tight musculature is often implicated in hip problems, a gentle stretching and flexibility program would be prudent.
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid high-impact activities.
 ‚Ä¢ A well-designed water exercise program would be useful for clients with hip problems.252Senior Fitness

# Knee Problems
Knee problems are common in adults. The causes are many and
range from rheumatoid arthritis, osteoarthritis, chondromalacia patellae, and joint trauma to joint replacement and reconstructive surgery of the knee. If the client is recovering from knee surgery, be sure to check with the physician to make sure that these exercises are appropriate for your client‚Äôs specific condition. If your client has been told to wear a knee brace, make sure he or she has it on when exercising.
Contraindications
 ‚Ä¢ Never go past a safe range of motion.
 ‚Ä¢ Do not use additional medication to mask the pain.
 ‚Ä¢ Do not sit with knee bent underneath you. Try to sit
with the knee almost straight whenever possible.
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid squatting, kneeling and stair climbing when-ever possible.
Recommendations
For people who have pain around the knee, it is important to keep
the thigh muscles as strong as possible.
 ‚Ä¢ Be faithful to and regular with the exercise pro-
gram and your joint will say, ‚ÄúThank-you!‚Äù
 ‚Ä¢ Do gentle range-of-motion exercises within your safe range.
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid full squats.
 ‚Ä¢ Use ice when appropriate.Chronic Conditions III: Orthopedic Conditions
# Unit 16 253

 ‚Ä¢ If pain increases significantly, speak with the client‚Äôs
physician and rest the joint until advised to  resume exercise.
 ‚Ä¢ Don‚Äôt ‚Äúmake‚Äù pain.
Indicated Exercise Program
 ‚Ä¢ Quad setting
 ‚Ä¢ Wall squats
 ‚Ä¢ Halfway-down chair squats
 ‚Ä¢ Closed-knee extension
 ‚Ä¢ Terminal leg extension
 ‚Ä¢ Seated leg extension (with permission)
 ‚Ä¢ Forward lunges with knee over foot
 ‚Ä¢ Leg curls
# Ankle Problems
Ankle sprains are very common. Ankle sprains occur when a
ligament connecting the bones or cartilage of the ankle is ruptured
or torn.
Factors that contribute to ankle problems are:
 ‚Ä¢ Previous injury
 ‚Ä¢ Weak or imbalanced muscles
 ‚Ä¢ Inappropriate/worn-out shoes
 ‚Ä¢ Walking on uneven surfaces
 ‚Ä¢ Being overweight
 ‚Ä¢ History of weak/unstable ankles254Senior Fitness
The two keys to improving your ankle health are:
 ‚Ä¢ Maintaining and improving range of motion
 ‚Ä¢ Improving strength and muscular endurance of the
ankle joint
Indicated Exercise Program
 ‚Ä¢ Heel cord stretch
 ‚Ä¢ Seated gas pedal

# Osteoporosis
Some of the leading causes of osteoporosis include family histo-
ry, lack of regular weight-bearing exercises, inadequate calcium and vitamin D, and low levels of estrogen following menopause. Osteoporosis is a loss of normal bone-density mass, which leads to increased porosity of the bone, which in turn makes the bone more vulnerable to fractures. Osteoporosis is more common in women, but does affect men later in life. Women who smoke and are thin and have fair complexions are more susceptible.
If you suspect that your client has osteoporosis, alert him or her
to the following:
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid all situations that could possibly lead to a fall.
 ‚Ä¢ Get rid of throw rugs and light up dark hallways to
prevent falls.
 ‚Ä¢ Quit smoking and drinking an excessive amount of soft drinks.
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid caffeine.
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid quick twisting movements.
 ‚Ä¢ Avoid poor posture when sitting, standing, and  lifting.
 ‚Ä¢ Be careful of drugs that cause you to get dizzy or that could cause you to fall.
 ‚Ä¢ Get involved in a safe exercise program, especially including weight bearing exercises
Topics for the client to discuss with the physician:
 ‚Ä¢ Discuss whether calcium supplements would  be helpful.
 ‚Ä¢ Ask if hormone replacement therapy would  be useful.
 ‚Ä¢ Find out what type of exercise is permissible.
 ‚Ä¢ Find out whether or not a bone scan is needed.256Senior Fitness
Indicated Exercise Program
 ‚Ä¢ Start a slow-walking program. If unstable, try sta-
tionary biking or using a cane while walking.
 ‚Ä¢ Strengthen the muscles of the upper back and stretch the chest muscles. A recent study showed that by doing strength and flexibility exercises, the effects of the ‚Äúdowager‚Äôs hump‚Äù were diminished.
 ‚Ä¢ Swimming and water exercise are good for cardio-vascular exercise, but the verdict is still out as to whether these really increase bone density. A study at the Jewish Hospital in Israel showed that water exercise, not swimming, did improve bone density, but until more research confirms that fact, be sure to combine water exercise with regular walking to increase bone density. Be careful not to slip and fall when in the locker room or around the pool.
 ‚Ä¢ Do not bend over to lift anything. This places undue stress on the bones of the lower back. Instead, bend the knees when lifting.
 ‚Ä¢ Start doing some basic strength-training exercises.
# Amputations
Amputation refers to the loss of an upper or lower extremity. The
term includes both acquired and congenital limb losses.
Etiology
 ‚Ä¢ Trauma
 ‚Ä¢ Peripheral vascular disease
 ‚Ä¢ Diabetes
 ‚Ä¢ Frostbite
 ‚Ä¢ Chronic infection of bone
 ‚Ä¢ Tumor
 ‚Ä¢ Congenital deformity where existing limb is not
capable of functioning properlyChronic Conditions III: Orthopedic Conditions
# Unit 16 257
The phantom limb syndrome is a frequent complaint of the person
with an amputation. This syndrome refers to the persistent aware-ness of the removed limb. This pain may occur shortly after trauma or surgery. Generally, these sensations are temporary, becoming less common once a prosthesis is applied.
Indicated Exercise Program

 ‚Ä¢ Balance activities
 ‚Ä¢ Ambulation
 ‚Ä¢ Pivoting
 ‚Ä¢ Stair climbing
 ‚Ä¢ How to fall to and rise from the ground258Senior Fitness

The aerobic capacity of the person should be maintained as much

Many older adults exhibit physical and sensory disabilities. The
following unit has been excerpted and edited from the textbook, Adapted Exercises for Adults with Disabilities, by Knopf and Lask. It will focus on both hearing and visual impairment. As an effective educator of older adults, it is important to understand the pathol-ogy of your clients‚Äô special conditions. However, sometimes it can be even more important to know how to teach and convey essential information to your clients. This is ever so evident when teaching the client who has difficulty hearing or seeing you.Sensory Problems in Older Adults
# Deafness And Hearing Loss
Hearing impairment or deafness may result in loss of amplitude
(decibels) and pitch (hertz). Each individual is different. Some peo-ple lose their hearing at birth or at early age and others lose it later in life. The term ‚Äúdeaf‚Äù can signify different things. It can indicate that a person cannot hear speech even when amplified, or it can indicate that a person doesn‚Äôt speak and uses sign language or fin-gerspelling as their primary language.
It is estimated that 10% of the population are hearing impaired or
deaf in some form in the United States. The challenge for individ-uals who lose their hearing is adjusting to communicate in a new way. Methods of standardized sign language, fingerspelling, and lip reading allow more persons with hearing impairments to partici-pate in regular as opposed to adapted physical education programs.
Rapid technological advances in the last 30 years‚Äîincluding digital
hearing aids, surgical cochlear implants, and in-ear hearing aids‚Äîhave allowed many people to regain some form of hearing. But even with these technologies, it is important to understand the range of frequency or sound is not the same as before. Even with advanced technology, many older adults experience an adjustment period.
# Types Of Hearing Impairment
Congenital hearing impairment refers to the medical condition‚Äôs having been present since birth. Adventitious hearing impairment refers to the condition‚Äôs having been acquired later on in life.  ‚ÄúLate deaf‚Äù or ‚Äúdeaf become‚Äù are terms commonly used for those with adventitious hearing impairments.
Conductive deafness occurs when sound waves cannot be trans-
mitted through either the outer or middle ear. The causes of this type of deafness could be the result of congenital atresia; middle ear infection due to upper respiratory infection or diseased tonsils or adenoids; childhood diseases; otitis media; or osteosclerosis. Sensorineural deafness occurs when sensory nerves of the inner ear are irreversibly damaged. A partial loss of equilibrium response is frequently associated with sensorineural hearing losses.262Senior Fitness
# Characteristics Of Persons
# With Hearing Impairments
Balance problems can be observed in people with sensorineural
(inner ear) loss. However, the person may compensate through the use of visual and kinesthetic cues. Hyperactivity is present in some, stemming from the need to maintain visual contact with all action occurring in the environment.
# Communicating With A
# Hearing Impaired Person
Before employing teaching strategies, it is important to be able to
develop a communication method with the individual. Every indi-vidual is different. Do not assume or generalize an individual‚Äôs abil-ity to understand you. You may not be aware that your client has a hearing impairment, as hearing aids can be invisible. Other clients may not even be aware they have a hearing impairment. The very best strategy is to inquire if your client has a hearing impairment and then ask how you can accommodate them.
Below are some strategies to keep in mind when communicating
with a hearing impaired or deaf person.
 1. Get the person‚Äôs attention before speaking. Make eye
contact, tap him on the shoulder, wave, turn the lights on/off, or use another visual signal.
 2. Key the person into the topic of discussion. People with hearing impairments need to know what the subject of discussion is so that they may pick up words that help them follow the conversation. This is especially important for people who depend on oral communication.
 3. Speak slowly and clearly, but do not yell, exaggerate, or over-pronounce. It is estimated that only three out of ten spoken words are visible on the lips. Exaggeration and overemphasis of words distort lip movements, mak-ing speech-reading more difficult. Try to enunciate each word, without force or tension. Short sentences are easier to understand than long ones.UNIT 17 263Sensory Problems in Older Adults
264Senior Fitness
 4. Look directly at the person when speaking. Even a
slight turn of your head can obscure their speech-read-ing view.
 5. Do not place anything in your mouth when speak-ing. Mustaches that obscure the lips, smoking, pencil chewing, and putting your hands in front of your face make it difficult for persons with hearing impairments to follow what is being said.
 6. Maintain eye contact. Eye contact conveys the feel-ing of direct communication. Even if an interpreter is present, continue to speak directly to the person with a hearing impairment. He or she will turn to the inter
-
preter as needed.
 7. Avoid standing in front of a light source, such as a window or bright light. The bright background and shadows created on the face make it almost impossi-ble to speech-read.
 8. First repeat, then try to rephrase a thought rather than again repeating the same words. If the person missed only one or two words the first time, one repetition usually will help. Particular combinations of lip move-ments sometimes are difficult for persons to speech-read. Don‚Äôt be embarrassed to communicate by paper and pencil if necessary. Getting the message across is more important than the medium used.
 9. Use gestures, body language, and facial expression to help communicate. A lively speaker is always more interesting to watch.
 10. Be courteous during conversation. If the telephone rings or someone knocks at the door, excuse yourself and tell the person that you are answering the phone or responding to the knock. Do not ignore the person and carry on a conversation with someone else while the person waits.
 11. Use open-ended questions that may be answered by more than ‚Äúyes‚Äù or ‚Äúno.‚Äù Do not assume that the per-son has understood your message if he or she nods in acknowledgment. Open-ended questions ensure that your information has been communicated.
Sensory Problems in Older Adults
# Unit 17 265
# Teaching Strategies
 1. Do not have the client facing the sun while you are instructing.
 2. Do not talk while facing away from person.
 3. Make eye contact before and when speaking to the person.
 4. If the client uses sign language and you know sign language, use
it.
 5. Ask if the client would prefer communication via pencil and paper.
 6. Keep hearing aid or cochlear implants away from excessive mois-ture. Many clients will need to remove their hearing aids during

exercise due to excess moisture. Ask your client ahead of time how you can best communicate during this time.
 7. Go over the exercise schedule and plan in advance (especially since many individuals will remove their hearing aids.)
 8. Use pictures and charts to reinforce demonstrations.
 9. Do not exaggerate or raise your voice when speaking to someone with a hearing aid (causes tension headaches). Use your typical

speaking voice.
 10. Assign a teammate to act as a partner and inform participant of changes in the environment during group sports and activities.
 11. Use visual cues (red flag, turn off lights) to get attention during activities.
 12. Ask clients if they have a balance dysfunction. If so, supervise climbing and apparatus work and teach principles of equilibrium:
 a. Broad base of support
 b. Lower center of gravity
 c. Use of arms out to side
 d. Falling properly
 e. Use of kneeling and sitting positions
 f. Falling outside base of support
# Indicated Exercise Program
Seniors who lose their hearing may withdraw socially from games,
dances, and group activities; thus, a fitness trainer or instructor may want to include activity choices that facilitate interaction and maintenance of social skills. Hearing loss can be a difficult to adjust to, but it need not prevent an older adult from maintaining good physical health.
# Visual Impairments
All definitions of visual impairments refer to how well the individual can see even with the best of corrective lenses. Normal vision is tech-nically referred to as 20/20. This numerical ratio is interpreted as the ability to see at twenty feet what the normal eye can see at twenty feet. A person is considered partially sighted or visually impaired if visual acuity is 20/70 or less with correction‚Äîinterpreted as the ability to see at twenty feet what the normal eye can see at seventy feet. A per-son is considered legally blind if visual acuity is 20/200 or less. Total blindness is the inability to detect a bright light that is shown directly into the eye. Visual acuity is measured with a Snellen Chart (lines of letters which become smaller with each line). Over 75 percent of individuals with visual impairments in the United States have some usable vision.
Visual impairments take many forms. Some may see a tiny spot of
the visual field; but even though the spot of vision is 20/20, they may be considered legally blind. Fuzzy vision, peripheral vision, and tunnel vision make the description of visual impairment difficult to understand. The amount of light and contrasts in the environment influence visual ability in persons with impairments. For example, a person may not need a guide during daylight hours because contrasts are sufficient to permit independent movement. However, during the evening or in a dark room such as a theater, a sighted guide may be necessary because no contrasts are available. Most visually impaired individuals can perform most activities once they are oriented to the facility. Some visually impaired people swim, golf, lift weights, ride tandem bikes, etc., as long as they do not have any other issues to contend with.266Senior Fitness
Sensory Problems in Older Adults
# Unit 17 267
# Etiology
Macular Degeneration: The macula is located in the central por-
tion of the retina. Degeneration affects central vision, but the per-son maintains good peripheral vision. Usually this condition does not progress to total blindness.
Glaucoma: Excessive high pressure within the eyeball, creating
tunnel vision.
Cataracts: A clouding of the cornea. The incidence of blindness
from cataracts has decreased due to surgical techniques.
Retinitis Pigmentosa: An inherited condition in which the rod-
shaped cells in the retina degenerate. This disorder leads to total blindness.
Diabetic Retinopathy:  Because diabetes can induce vascular
changes, the retina is particularly susceptible to hemorrhage, scar-
ring, and loss of vision.
Stroke: Blindness can be caused by lack of blood supply to the visu-
al cortex located in the occipital lobe. Blindness occasionally occurs in stroke cases where the person is only capable of seeing out of one side of each eye (hemianopsia).
Refractory Errors: This includes farsightedness, nearsightedness,
and astigmatism.
Other Causes: Blindness can be a result of venereal disease, the
aging process, trauma causing detached retina, tumors, or exposure to bright light (e.g., sun, welding light).
# Characteristics
The functional ability of a person who is blind or visually impaired varies, depending upon age of onset and whether the impairment is total or partial. A person who is visually impaired or has some perception of form and light may be completely independent with full use of his or her other senses. With proper training in the use of Braille, large type books, tape recorders, new technological
equipment, and good mobility skills, individuals who are blind
can be as functionally independent as their community will allow. Individuals who have been totally blind since birth do not have any visual memory and therefore learn to use their intact senses to perceive what others see. Some individuals with visual impairments may display mannerisms such as eye-poking or rocking, usually due to a need for physical stimulation.
# Techniques For Guiding A Person
# With Visual Impairment
 1. Make contact: Lightly brush forearms with the per-son so he or she can find the back of your arm, just behind the elbow. Keep your elbow flexed to 90 degrees. This position enables the person to be at your side but about a half-step behind.
 2. Familiarize the person with the environment. When you give directions, give them according to the way the person is facing‚Äîleft or right. When enter-ing a room, indicate number of people
 present,
size of room, and general description.
 3. Pause before any stairs or curbs. Describe the height of the step up or down.
 4. When approaching a door, inform the person whether the door will swing ‚Äúaway‚Äù or ‚Äútoward‚Äù him or her and if it opens to the left or right. Swing the door open as far as possible and whisk the person through before it swings shut.
 5. When sitting, deposit the person at the back of the chair and allow him or her to seat himself or herself. Alternate method: back person up into the seat until the calves touch.
 6. If going through a narrow passageway, keep your elbow flexed, but internally rotate your shoulder, placing your arm behind your back. The person will then slide the hand from the back of your biceps to the middle of the forearm and step directly behind you.268Senior Fitness
# Indicated Exercise Program
It is recommended that each person with visual impairments have
a medical verification completed by an ophthalmologist. These doctors have differing philosophies regarding physical activities for visually impaired individuals (e.g., regarding bending over during exercise, putting the face in the water when swimming, etc.). Therefore, a phone call to the attending eye care specialist would be useful, especially in the case of diabetic retinopathy.
The mode of exercise may vary depending on the degree of vision
remaining and risk of injury to the eye. If an individual has no functional vision, his or her choice of physical activities may actu-ally expand since there is no fear of further deterioration.
Encourage cardiovascular endurance. The person with visual
impairments rarely gets an opportunity to engage in aerobic activi -
ties due to the amount of supervision or guiding required and thus often has low endurance. Utilize stationary cycling, low-intensity rowing on a row machine, tandem cycling, folk and square dance, aerobic dance, swimming, and jogging. If an indoor track is avail -
able, a guide wire can assist with independent locomotion. On an outdoor track, a sighted guide can assist with walking or jogging by simply touching forearms occasionally as the two move side-by-side; another method is to extend a short rope between the two persons to maintain contact and control. Swimming is an ideal activity because it builds strength and cardiovascular endurance while requiring minimal assistance from a partner. Swimming is easily adaptable as buoys and rope lanes prevent an individual from straying out of a lane while swimming laps. A ‚Äúbonker‚Äù (soft sponge ball attached to a long wooden dowel) may be used to tap the swim -
mer on the head and signal the edge of the pool and end of the lap.
Develop individual sport activities and leisure skills that have car-
ry-over value. Because of equipment adaptations in sports such as archery, persons with visual impairments are able to participate and compete alongside sighted persons. Teaching adaptations have Sensory Problems in Older Adults
# Unit 17 269
also broadened sports participation by persons with visual impair-
ments; for example, snow skiing may be accomplished through the use of a sighted guide and auditory input. Mingling sighted part-ners with non-sighted partners allows folk dancing to be enjoyed by non-sighted people. Circle formations are especially helpful in providing spatial orientation during dance steps.
Postural exercises may need to be prescribed for lordosis, ptosis,
and kyphosis. These will be prescribed by the client‚Äôs attending physician and/or physical therapist.
Considerations for Diabetic Retinopathy
Aerobic exercise for persons with retinopathy includes stationary cycling, low-intensity rowing on a rowing machine, swimming, walking, and jogging. Adaptations for accomplishing these activities can be found elsewhere in this unit. Greenlee (1987) recommend -
ed that the heart rate not exceed that which elicits a systolic blood pressure of 170 mm Hg. The blood pressure should be monitored during every exercise session and intensity adjusted accordingly. This method is used because moderate exercise can raise systolic blood pressure to levels above 200 mm Hg, thereby risking further damage to the retina. A submaximal pretest should be conducted to establish initial exercise intensity. Resistive exercise using standard weight-lifting equipment is not recommended (unless a person is totally blind); however, if a weight-lifting regime is strongly desired by the participant, physician approval is requisite. For each resistive exercise, the load to be lifted should be such that the person can lift a minimum of fifteen repetitions per set with a resistance that does not cause undue fatigue. Exhaling on the lift/effort and inhaling on the return to starting position will prevent a Valsalva maneuver.
At present, there is no clear evidence that intensive physical train -
ing programs accelerate the progression of diabetic retinopathy.
However, certain types of exercise result in large increases of systolic blood pressure with concomitant increases in intraocular pressure; 270Senior Fitness
neuropathy: a disease of,
or damage to nervesthese exercises are contraindicated. Specifically, activities should be
avoided that involve (1) bending over so that the head is positioned lower than the waist, (2) Valsalva-type maneuvers that raise blood pressure, (3) any near maximal isometric contractions, (4) weight lifting with high resistance and low repetitions, (5) vigorous bounc -
ing such as high impact aerobics, (6) rapid eye-head movements found in contact sports, and (7) strenuous upper extremity exercise, which increases peripheral resistance to blood flow resulting in higher blood pressure. Jogging can produce irregular head move -
ments that may potentially aggravate an eye condition. Additional activities that are contraindicated include parachuting, scuba div -
ing, and yoga. The latter uses postures that increase intraocular pressure. Exercise is contraindicated if the person has recently undergone retinal photocoagulation treatment or eye surgery.
SENSORY DISORDERS: NEUROPATHY
Neuropathy is a disease of or damage to nerves that can be caused by various chronic conditions. When nerve damage or disease occurs outside of the brain or spinal cord, it is called a peripheral neuropathy.
# Autonomic Neuropathy
Disease or damage specifically to the nerves that regulate blood pressure, heart rate, bowel and bladder emptying, digestion, and other body functions is known as autonomic neuropathy.
Causes: chronic alcoholism, diabetes, disorders involving scarring
and hardening (sclerosis) tissues, Guillain Barre syndrome or other diseases that inflame nerves, HIV and AIDS, inherited nerve disor-ders, Parkinson‚Äôs disease, surgery or injury to the nerves.
Exercise considerations:  Stationary cycling or water exercises are
suggested; the latter is particularly beneficial for persons with ortho -
static hypertension because pressure of the water surrounding the Sensory Problems in Older Adults
# Unit 17 271
autonomic neuropathy:
disease or damage specifi-cally to the nerves that reg-ulate blood pressure, heart rate, bowel and bladder emptying, digestion, and other body functions
272Senior Fitness
body helps to maintain normal blood pressure. Sitting or semi-re -
cumbent exercises (which assist in maintaining blood pressure) are
also recommended for developing fitness levels. Strenuous exercise involving rapid changes in body position should be avoided as the concomitant changes in heart rate and blood pressure may trigger a hypotensive episode following exercise.
# Sensorimotor Neuropathy
Neuropathy can affect nerves that provide feeling (sensory neu-ropathy) or cause movement (motor neuropathy). It can also affect both, in which case it is called a sensorimotor neuropathy.
Causes: chronic alcoholism, cancer (called a paraneoplastic neurop-
athy), chronic inflammatory neuropathy, diabetes, Guillain-Barre syndrome, hereditary neuropathy, vitamin deficiency (vitamins B12, B1, and E)
Exercise considerations: Persons with sensorimotor neuropa -
thy are more dependent on vision when performing motor skills
because proprioception is diminished in the extremities. The fol-lowing list provides strategies to facilitate movement in persons with sensorimotor neuropathy (Graham & Lasko-McCarthy, 1990):
 1. Use mirrors to facilitate body awareness of the
lower extremities.
 2. Enhance muscle tone by rubbing and tapping the dermatome (skin) of the muscle to be contracted.
 3. Use visual aids such as footprints placed on the floor during exercise.
 4. Teach principles of equilibrium such as (a) hands out to side, (b) lowering the center of gravity by bending knees, and (c) focusing on stationary object at eye level.


 5. Increase proprioception and stimulate joint reflexes by adding resistance to the movement (e.g., wrist weights or manually applied resistance).
 6. Use non-weight bearing activities such as arm exer-cises, swimming, and cycling for persons with loss of sensation to the feet.sensorimotor neurop-athy: disease or dam-
age to nerve cells, nerve fibers, and nerve coverings throughout the body, affecting both sensory and motor nerves
Sensory Problems in Older Adults
# Unit 17 273
 7. Inspect the feet before and after exercise for swell-
ing, heat, redness, or ulceration. Change shoes every five hours.
 8. Use towels or canes to facilitate stretching  exercises.
 9. Use external support such as a chair during
balance exercises.
# Conclusion
Communication is always an important component between client
and trainer and is even more crucial when working with individuals with sensory disabilities and individuals who may be returning to exercise after an injury, illness, or other absence. A common mis-take people make is beginning an exercise program without first consulting their physician or physical therapist. A doctor or physi-cal therapist can help set any movement limitations for your client as well as prescribe appropriate exercises and set realistic goals. It is then the responsibility of the client to communicate movement limitations and goals to you, the exercise leader or personal trainer. Once you are aware of the condition, you should follow up with a phone call to the doctor or physical therapist. This way, everyone involved is aware of all conditions and is working together to pro-vide your client with the safest and most effective program.
Proper progression is another component that will assure success
and safety. Often people try to return to their previous level of fit-ness too quickly which may result in re-injury. The goals, which you and your client set, should be both attainable and reasonable. As your client achieves new fitness goals, progressively increase the frequency, intensity, and duration of their activity over weeks and months. Advise clients to be ‚Äúprotective‚Äù of their bodies and to pay attention to warning signs. Follow the ‚Äú2-hour rule‚Äù: If your client reports feeling unusual pain during or within two hours after exercise, they probably worked too hard, too fast, or mechanically incorrectly. You then should re-evaluate the program and make the necessary adjustments.
# Unit Summary
 I. Many older adults experience hearing and visual dis-
orders, so understanding how to communicate with these individuals is vital for the trainer of older adults.
 II. Hearing disorders are one of the most common chronic physical impairments in the United States.
 a. Common characteristics of people with hearing loss include difficulty with balance, hyperactivity, and socially immature behavior in children.
 b. Teaching strategies for working with individuals with hearing loss include facing the person when talking, using pictures and charts to reinforce demonstrations, assigning a teammate to act as a partner, and teaching principles of equilibrium.
 III. Visual impairments take many forms, but it is import-ant to remember that most visually impaired people can perform most activities once they are oriented to the facility.
 a. Causes of visual impairment include glaucoma, cat-aracts, retinitis pigmentosa, and diabetic retinopa
-
thy.
 b. Individuals with visual impairments should have medical clearance to participate in an exercise  program because doctors have differing philoso
-

# Business Basics
Many college graduates face the prospect of finding employment
after they receive their diplomas. In many professions, jobs are waiting if preparation is done well in advance of graduation. This assurance is not so well defined in fitness. From coaching to con-sulting, there are many avenues for new professionals to seek, but automatic job placement cannot be taken for granted.
# Employment By Gym
Upon becoming certified as a personal trainer, many individuals
elect to work as for organizations as employees. Gyms and health clubs, corporate wellness centers, resorts and hotels, hospitals, and retirement facilities all have been known to hire personal trainers able to work with older adults. The advantages of being an employ-ee include the security of a regular paycheck, employee benefits such as medical insurance and paid vacations, freedom from record keeping and income tax withholding, and above all, freedom from the necessity of going out and finding clients.
Advantages of Gym Employment
 ‚Ä¢ Regular paycheck.
 ‚Ä¢ Employee benefits.
 ‚Ä¢ Freedom from record keeping and tax details.
 ‚Ä¢ Do not have to find clients.
# Working For Yourself
The alternative to employee status is pursuing independent con-
tractorship or building your own personal training business. Independent contractors are independent business people who are hired to perform specific tasks. They are just like any other vendor, except they perform services rather than providing tangible prod-ucts. Independent contractors are not eligible for unemployment, disability, or workers‚Äô compensation benefits because they are not the hiring firm‚Äôs employee. They are in business for themselves. The hiring firm does not have to pay employee-employer taxes or provide workers‚Äô compensation insurance, and usually is not liable for the independent contractor‚Äôs actions. (For this reason, it is essential that you carry your own liability insurance if you plan 278Senior Fitness
to function as an independent contractor.) If you wish to form an
independent contractor relationship with a hiring firm, you should remember the following characteristics that must be satisfied to retain independent contractor status:
Characteristics of Independent Contractors
 ‚Ä¢ Hired on a job-to-job basis.
 ‚Ä¢ Operate as a separate business.
 ‚Ä¢ Offer their services to the general public.
 ‚Ä¢ Hired to perform a specific task.
 ‚Ä¢ No ongoing relationship or obligation to the hiring firm.
 ‚Ä¢ No control of the contractor‚Äôs work by the hiring firm.
The I.R.S. and local taxing authorities can assess severe penalties
upon businesses that improperly classify employees as indepen-dent contractors, so you should consult a knowledgeable attorney or accountant when forming such a relationship as an indepen-dent contractor.
Advantages of Independent Contractorship
 ‚Ä¢ You set your own hours.
 ‚Ä¢ You decide what rates to charge.
 ‚Ä¢ You can grow, expand, and progress based on your
abilities and efforts rather than an employer‚Äôs dictates.
Often, trainers begin their careers by working for a gym or health
club for a few months to build confidence and experience. Once they develop a private clientele, they gradually phase out of the relationship with the gym and operate as independent trainers.Business and Marketing Basics
# Unit 18 279
Where to Train
Many very highly paid trainers perform their services at their cli-
ents‚Äô homes or at private exercise facilities owned by the trainer. It is easy and inexpensive to obtain portable equipment such as dumbbells, rubber-band resistance devices, and Swiss exercise balls that can be transported from session to session in the trunk of the car. If you wish to bring your private clients to a gym to train them, you can usually make such an arrangement with the gym owner or manager by explaining that some of your clients will likely even-tually become members of the gym. Usually the gym owner will require that you submit proof of your certification and liability insurance coverage, and that you pay a nominal fee (such as $10.00 per session) to the gym. This fee will normally be a small fraction of the fee you charge your clients.280Senior Fitness

Getting Started
It is normal to feel uncomfortable or even intimidated when con-
templating starting a fitness business. Anyone starting a new ven-ture feels the same way. Not to worry, however; as your experience in the field accumulates, you‚Äôll gain confidence and your comfort level will improve. But before you actually begin to work with cli-ents, you‚Äôll need to attend to business procedures. Preparation is the key to developing a successful personal training business.
Choose where and how you will work. It‚Äôs a good idea to start
your business in your hometown and the area that surrounds it.
Sometimes trainers move to another area (such as a large city) purely because of better business prospects, but usually you‚Äôll be happier if you work where you want to live. Once you know where you want to work, you need to determine how you want to work. You can work as an independent contractor from your home, or you can subcontract your services with a local gym or club. At first you may want to pursue both avenues to allow yourself the great-est possible exposure to prospective clients.
Decide where you will set up your office. It may be in a separate
room at home, or you may obtain the privilege of using an existing room or office at the gym or club you have an agreement with. In either case, you must have all of the necessary forms, business cards, client logs, etc., in an accessible location. Another important reason to have an office is to have a location to interview prospec-tive clients, collect fees, and so on. Even if you don‚Äôt have an actual office, it‚Äôs important to have a consistent spot in the gym or health club where you leave your briefcase and other necessities during a session. A simple table and chair will do the job if nothing else is available. Important point: Use your office to showcase your diplo-mas, photos of clients you have trained, articles you have written, etc. They‚Äôre great credibility builders. Almost everyone who enters an office spends time looking at items on the wall while they‚Äôre waiting for you.Business and Marketing Basics
# Unit 18 281
Establish policies and communicate them to your client at the
outset. Outline how you want to handle late clients, no shows, late payments, bounced checks, and other problems that may arise in a written policy. Being upfront about your policies will prevent mis-understandings and ensure a fair business relationship for all parties involved. If you have a written policy that late payments will result in a 10% service charge, you won‚Äôt feel uncomfortable charging it if a client does not pay on time. Avoid having to create a policy ‚Äúon the spot.‚Äù You‚Äôll feel uncomfortable, and your clients will sense a lack of professionalism. Be prepared and organized‚Äîit pays off!
Keep thorough client records. Thorough client records are vital
when working with older clients who have chronic conditions. Keep a file for each client including, at a minimum, a Health History Questionnaire, emergency contact information, and a training contract. Also use some type of attendance sheet, as well as a daily log. Use the log to track what exercises and training was done during the session, how well the client did, any unusual events (or injuries) that may have occurred, and anything else that you may feel to be noteworthy. After each training session, immediately fill out the client‚Äôs training log while the experience is still fresh in your memory. Not only will this practice keep you focused better on your client‚Äôs progress, but it will also be a valuable asset should a lawsuit develop. You‚Äôll be able to demonstrate that you‚Äôre a pro-fessional who keeps accurate and detailed records on each client.
# Marketing Basics
In order for you to use all of this valuable information about how to best train the older adult, you‚Äôll need to find a way to market your services to older adults. For many, this is the hardest part. This unit is designed to provide you with some suggestions about how to find clients so you can use the knowledge you have learned. And make no mistake, this important and expanding segment of the world‚Äôs population is out there waiting for you. Dr. Peterson, in his book Gray Dawn, states that global aging will become the most crucial 282Senior Fitness

WHO IS AN OLDER ADULT?
Currently, the population is divided into five generations:
 ‚Ä¢ Matures, born between 1900 and 1945
 ‚Ä¢ Baby Boomers, born between 1946 and 1964
 ‚Ä¢ Generation X, born between 1965 and 1979
 ‚Ä¢ Generation Y, born between 1980 and 1995
 ‚Ä¢ Generation Z, born between 1995 and the presentBusiness and Marketing Basics
# Unit 18 283

Trainers interested in pursuing fitness education with older adults
will find clients who are either Matures or Baby Boomers. The life experience of each group will influence their opinions on health and fitness, the amount they are willing to pay for your services, and the value they attach to personal training. Given these vari-ables, it will be important for you to learn what kind of individual your client is, what your client wants, and what your client is will-ing to pay, and to develop a clear and concise message that takes all of these things into account.
Once you develop a philosophy and your mission is clear, you are
on your way.
Remember that ‚Äúmarketing‚Äù is anything and everything you do,
whether informally or formally, to recruit and retain clients. If you rely on the sales staff at the gym or fitness center to ‚Äúmarket‚Äù your training, you risk losing clients. You will need to learn how to nurture clients yourself to grow your base. How you interact with each of the subgroups mentioned above will be different, but to be 284Senior Fitness

successful in working with any older adult, you must be adaptable,
respectful, and resourceful.
It is critical that you learn as much as possible about your market
and discard any preconceived ideas about how people will behave. One recent study debunked many myths about the buying behavior of older women. Marketers who understand and appreciate the real motivations of older shoppers will have an enormous opportunity to grow their business. Keep in mind that most older adult shop-pers want to ‚Äúbuy‚Äù a product, not be ‚Äúsold‚Äù a product. Older adult shoppers typically won‚Äôt fall for the gimmicks used with younger and more naive shoppers. The paradigm of ‚Äúpushing‚Äù sales will not work with this audience. What will work is offering services that will ‚Äúpull‚Äù the client to you. Developing a referral base is one exam-ple. Your reputation will bring in more clients than an expensive ad in the yellow pages.
# Reaching Older Adults
If you desire to work with older adults, begin marketing your ser-vices in places where older individuals are likely to be on a regular basis. Recreation centers that offer senior services and events that foster socialization are great places to meet older adults who are interested in staying fit. Retirement communities are also ideal places to begin marketing your services. Such places aren‚Äôt neces-sarily staffed with nurses and therapists who can work with resi-dents on fitness. You may offer your services once or twice a week to work one-on-one with seniors or teach small groups. A letter to the facility director, followed up with a phone call, may prove to be all you need to get your foot in the door if there are no rehabilita-tion or exercise programs in place. Recreation and senior centers also often have bulletin boards and newsletters to keep members updated on upcoming events and services for seniors. Place an ad in one of these places or do an interview for the newsletter to pro-mote senior fitness and your training services.Business and Marketing Basics
# Unit 18 285
Also keep in mind that a high percentage of seniors are taking pre-
scription drugs for various chronic conditions. Post a flyer adver-tising your services at your local drugstore. Or consider providing a brown bag lecture series at the drugstore. Older adults tend to trust their pharmacist‚Äôs advice, so telling the pharmacist about your business and getting his or her support can do a lot to generate clients.
Becoming familiar with your local area physician‚Äôs network is
another smart idea. Find out which doctors in your area are doing the most work with older adults. Getting a physician‚Äôs referral can be a great way to get your business rolling. A good reputation with-in the medical community is priceless when working with senior clients. Many doctors are becoming more receptive to the idea of utilizing exercise as a means to control or reduce the symptoms of existing conditions in their older clients. Doctors also recognize the quality-of-life improvements seen in older adults who are already participating in regular exercise. Present yourself to physicians as a qualified trainer desiring to work as a part of the treatment team. Even if some doctors don‚Äôt refer any patients directly, they may let you advertise in their clinic or office.
Each year there are many events sponsored locally and nationally
that will draw senior participation. Be there too! Walk for a cause, attend a health fair, or set up your own booth. Contact the local chamber of commerce or visit your city‚Äôs website to find out when these events will take place in your area. These events also lend themselves to networking with other people in the health and fit-ness field.
Don‚Äôt forget about your local gyms and fitness centers either. Some
of them may already have a senior fitness program in place, but oth-ers may desire to get one started. Speak with the manager and find out if you can post a business flyer on the bulletin board. Managers rarely refuse an opportunity to increase their club membership! Focus on the fitness centers close to local retirement communities 286Senior Fitness
Business and Marketing Basics
# Unit 18 287
or those serviced by public transportation. Many seniors use cabs,
busses, and community center van services to get around town.
Finally, if you are currently training athletes or general fitness cli-
ents, let them know that you are interested in expanding your busi-ness to accommodate an older population. Your clients may have family, neighbors, or friends who could benefit from your services. Word of mouth is one of the best ways to get new clients.

# Basics Of Assisting A  Person In A Wheelchair
Many people are unsure how to act when confronted by a person
in a wheelchair. A wheelchair is part of the user‚Äôs body space and should be respected. Always ask whether the wheelchair user needs assistance. Always explain what you are doing and where you are going before starting to push a chair. Never surprise a person from
behind by moving his or her chair. Wheelchairs can be tricky to
handle, so here are some points to remember:
 ‚Ä¢ Be aware that the wheelchair user might be able
to walk with the assistance of a cane, crutches,

or braces.
 ‚Ä¢ Don‚Äôt try to maneuver a wheelchair alone unless you are sure you can handle the weight and move-ment safely.
 ‚Ä¢ Ask how the chair operates. Are the armrests, leg rests, and body supports secure? Is there a seat belt? Are the brakes working properly? The brakes are located on both sides of the chair, just above the large wheels, and can be operated by a lever. Locate the brakes before you attempt to move the chair. Learn how to safely tip a chair back.
 ‚Ä¢ Be sure to lock the brakes when leaving the client even for a moment. Be sure to lock the brakes when transferring in or out of the chair.
 ‚Ä¢ Plan a route for where you are taking the client in the chair. Avoid narrow doorways and openings, stairs, bumpy surfaces, and wet floors. Be alert for objects in your path. Ask the client if there is a pre-ferred path to take.
 ‚Ä¢ If you are assisting by pushing a wheelchair over rough terrain, the chair will ride easier and jolt its occupant less if it is tilted back on its rear wheels.
 ‚Ä¢ While descending a curb with a wheelchair, the pusher should be behind the chair, tilting it back on its rear wheels. The pusher can then control  the descent by the use of pressure against his

own legs. It is important that both wheels touch the lower level simultaneously to avoid jolting  the occupant.
 ‚Ä¢ If assisting while ascending a curb, tip the chair back, and place the front or small wheels on the upper level; then, using the rear wheels, ride on to the upper level or sidewalk, using your legs to pro-vide pressure. 292Senior Fitness
transfer: The relocation
of an individual from one surface to another; can be classified as either ‚Äústand-ing‚Äù or ‚Äúsitting.‚ÄùTRANSFERS
Transfer refers to the relocation of an individual from one surface
to another and can be classified as either ‚Äústanding‚Äù or ‚Äúsitting.‚Äù Variations on these two types of transfers depend on the capabili-ties of the client and the type of surface to which the person is trans-ferring. Many individuals have developed a personal technique for executing a transfer, and this preference should be discussed before the assistant or instructor attempts to help. It is paramount that the safety of client and transfer be of utmost concern. Allow the client to do as much as is safely possible to do.
# Techniques For Assisting
# A Person To Transfer
Students coming to training in a wheelchair may need to transfer
into a regular chair or onto a mat to better perform the exercise program. Other students who walk with a cane or walker may also need guidance when standing up, sitting down, or getting on or off of a piece of exercise equipment. The inabilities and abilities that are identified at the time of assessment will determine the amount and kind of assistance required by a person during a transfer. The person should only be given the assistance actually needed. The principal guideline for assistance is to provide safety and protection during the transfer. In the case of an especially obese person, two assistants may be required. Do not attempt any activity beyond your capacity. The following basic concepts will help you to trans-fer anyone:
 ‚Ä¢ Stand as close to the client as possible. This
means standing in front of the client to assist with
a sitting transfer. For standing transfers that require extensive assistance, stand in front of the client so that you can actually provide support and protec-tion. If only minimal assistance is needed in the standing transfer, you may stand by the client‚Äôs side, preferably by the weaker side. Lightweight chairs need to be stabilized as well; either hold on to the back of the chair, or place it against a solid wall or table.Transfers and Ambulation Aids
# Appendix A 293
 ‚Ä¢ Stand with a broad base of support. In other
words, your feet should be kept apart, with one
foot slightly ahead of the other. This will improve your balance and also permit a shift of weight with greater ease.
 ‚Ä¢ Assist the
client at the waist rather than
pulling his arms or shoulders. The use of the belt of the pants or a transfer belt allows a good grip without causing the client pain.
 ‚Ä¢ Bend your
hips and knees, keeping your
back straight, while actually assisting the client to move from one surface to another.
 ‚Ä¢ Make sure
that the client can see the sur-
face to which he or she is transferring.
 ‚Ä¢ Always move
your body in the direction in
which the transfer is taking place.
 ‚Ä¢ Make sure
that the client is wearing shoes
(and a brace if this has been prescribed), in order to prevent possible slipping, foot injury, or turning of an ankle.
 ‚Ä¢ Be sure
your client understands when you
are transferring him or her. Count out loud so you both move together.294Senior Fitness

sliding board: Support
board used to facilitate a
transfer by bridging the gap between two areas.SITTING TRANSFERS‚ÄîUNASSISTED
Any sitting transfer, assisted or unassisted, has the following
basic elements:
 ‚Ä¢ The person should wear a transfer belt around the
waist in case assistance might be needed.
 ‚Ä¢ The wheelchair should be positioned at a 45¬∞ angle to the opposite surface if the armrests are not removable from the chair. If the armrests are removable, the chair can be positioned directly parallel to the opposite surface.
 ‚Ä¢ Brakes must be locked.
 ‚Ä¢ Footrests should be moved away in most cases.
 ‚Ä¢ If a sliding board  is used, it must rest securely on
both surfaces, i.e., the surface to which the client is transferring and the surface from which the client is transferring.
 ‚Ä¢ It is recommended that the hemiplegic person learn to transfer toward the uninvolved side.Transfers and Ambulation Aids
# Appendix A 295
Figure APa-1  Sliding Board
SITTING TRANSFERS‚ÄîASSISTED
The basic elements of unassisted transfers also apply to assisted
transfers. In an assisted sitting transfer, the client comes to the edge of the bed or mat as in the unassisted transfer. Bend your hips and knees to lower yourself to the level of the client. Grip the transfer belt from underneath, or support the client under the armpits, and 296Senior Fitness
Figure APa-3  Sitting transfer, one man front-facing
Figure APa-2  Pull to stand in parallel bars
Figure APa-1  Stand and pivot
assist as the client angles toward the chair. As the client moves
from one surface to another, support the client‚Äôs knees with your own knees. Using the transfer belt, you are able to lift the buttocks up and onto the other surface. Throughout the transfer, the client leans forward in order to maintain trunk balance. Once in the chair, you may have to help to position the client by pushing the knees back by pressure from your own. This is done while leaning the trunk forward or doing a push-up. You can also move the client back into the chair by standing behind the chair and utilizing the client‚Äôs transfer belt.
STANDING TRANSFERS‚ÄîASSISTED
When you use a transfer belt around the client‚Äôs waist, stand directly in front of the client with your feet slightly apart. You may also support under the shoulders. Bend your hips and knees to the level of the client and assist by pulling him or her to the standing position by grasping the belt at the waist from underneath. If the client has weakness at the knee, you can brace your knee against the client‚Äôs weak knee in order to stabilize it. Once he or she is in a standing position, you can assist the client to pivot. The person should lean forward and gently lower into the chair. Transfers and Ambulation Aids
# Appendix A 297
Figure APa-4  Transfer Belt
SUMMARY: A DOZEN TIPS FOR
# Performing Transfers
 1. Reduce the distance between the transfer surfaces.
Removing arm rests and detachable footrests will permit closer positioning of the client to the trans-fer surface.
 2. Always secure wheelchair brakes. They are essential for safety and stability.
 3. Transfer to a surface of equal height if possible. A sliding board may be used to eliminate the gap between the two surfaces. Be sure to stabilize the sliding board on both transfer surfaces.
 4. When transferring an individual with one-sided involvement (e.g., hemiplegia), position the wheel-chair alongside the table on the individual‚Äôs stron
-
ger side. Provide assistance from the  weaker side.
 5. Keep a wide base of support. Placing one foot ahead of the other allows you to shift your weight more easily.
 6. Keep your back straight while flexing at the hips and knees during the transfer. Hold the client  as close as possible and lift with the thigh

muscles, extending at the knees and hips, not  with your back.
 7. If possible, allow the individual to view the surface to which he or she is being transferred.
 8. To increase the stability of the wheelchair, place the casters (i.e., the front wheels) in a forward posi-tion.
 9. Give clear instructions regarding the transfer to  the client.
 10. Count ‚Äú1-2-3-go,‚Äù so that you and the client  move together.
 11. Get additional assistance if necessary.
 12. Always protect your back! Consider using back- support braces.298Senior Fitness
# Progressive Mobilization
Progressive mobilization is the term used to describe the gradual,
step-by-step development of a person‚Äôs ability to move around and gain independence. The following activities are designed to build on one another.
# Mat Or Bed Activities
These are generally accomplished in the hospital as bed activities, and include bed positioning, range-of-motion exercises, moving up and down in bed, moving sideways, and rolling over in bed. However, as clients prepare to perform exercises lying down on mats, we frequently need to remind them of the proper techniques involved in moving around as well as getting up and down.
 1. Moving up, down, or sideways: instruct your
client as follows‚Äî‚ÄùBend your knees and put your
feet flat on the mat.‚Äù You can assist by holding down one or both knees and feet if they slip. Instruct, ‚ÄúLift your hips off the mat by pushing with your feet. Move your hips in the desired direc-tion (up, down, or sideways) before putting them down.‚Äù You may also have to assist by putting your arm or arms under the buttocks. Remember your proper body mechanics.
 2. Rolling Over:
 If your client needs to roll to the
right side, instruct them as follows‚Äî‚ÄùMove side-ways to the left to ensure that there is enough room to roll to the right‚Äù (many people become frightened when they get too close to the edge). ‚ÄúCross your left leg over the right leg as far as pos-sible. Cross the left arm over your chest. Pull on the mat or railing as needed. If you need momentum, swing the arm or leg across your body.‚Äù
 3. Assuming a
Sitting Position: Instruct the cli-
ent‚Äî‚ÄùFrom a side-lying position, slide your arm underneath you with your elbow bent. Now do these two things at the same time: drop your legs off the mat and start pushing up with your elbow. Transfers and Ambulation Aids
# Appendix A 299
After your weight has been transferred onto your
elbow, shift your weight to your hand and keep pushing until you are upright.‚Äù If the client needs help, assist as necessary, but encourage them to do as much as possible by themselves. Watch for dizzi-ness when upright.
 4. Si
tting (sitting down is addressed after
Standing): Sitting tolerance (length of time out
of bed) is accomplished during the hospital reha -
bilitation phase. Sitting balance is an activity often practiced as part of an adaptive physical fitness program. This helps develop trunk stability for standing and walking. The person should ultimate -
ly be able to sit unsupported, even against resis-tance. Instruct the client to: ‚ÄúSit level on the mat or chair‚Äîweight even, on both buttocks. Position feet flat on the floor‚Äîwide base of support. Place arm or arms where you can push to maintain a good, erect position.‚Äù
 As sitting balance improves, the client should be able to sit without holding on to the edge of the mat and should be able to move his or her arms around at will. The client should practice reaching above the head, out to the side, and out in front. A mirror may be helpful. After the client can do the aforementioned, add resistance to the activity by pushing on the client from either side, from the front, or from the back. (Let the client know that you are going to do this exercise, and get permis -
sion first!) Good sitting balance is achieved when the client can do all of this.
 5. Standing: The ‚Äústand-up‚Äù is a necessary exercise even if the client cannot yet walk, because it facili
-
tates pulling up pants, going to the bathroom, and many other functions. Strength and balance are increased, as is overall circulation. A person will feel tremendously successful when he or she is able to stand up alone. However, don‚Äôt let the client rush to do it; make sure the correct steps are followed so that it will be done properly and efficiently. Give the following instructions: ‚ÄúScoot your hips/but-tocks to the front edge of the chair. Put your feet flat on the floor, feet apart, with your stronger leg 300Senior Fitness
a little further back and under you. The legs will do
the work. Use your hands only for balance (on the table, chair, or bars). Lean your head and shoulders forward and stand on the count of 1-2-3.‚Äù
 6. Si
tting down: Sitting down is accomplished in
the reverse of standing. Instruct the client to lean forward while bending the knees and to touch down slowly with the buttocks. If a person is too weak to perform the stand-up exercise alone, adjust the chair as high as possible and provide assistance until the individual can stand alone. To evaluate when a person is able to stand, test quadriceps strength. If the person can hold against some resistance, he or she can probably stand.  Also, try to help the client stand. If the client can stand, it is obvious that he or she can perform the exercise. If the client cannot stand, use resistive exercises until leg strength increases sufficiently to enable standing.
 If balance is very poor, have the client assume a wide base of support on the bars with the hands. Then have the client lift his or her hands off the bars and raise them sideways and overhead while maintaining balance. The client‚Äôs ability to complete this exercise will increase with practice and gradual gain in strength. Later, resistance can be given by pushing the client unexpectedly from side to side, forward and back. This will stress balance and will increase ability. (Again, let the client know that you are going to do this exercise, and get permission!)
# Ambulation Aids
People with a lower-extremity disability usually require some form
of assistive device during ambulation. Canes, crutches, and walkers serve as extensions that permit the upper extremities to transmit force to the floor, providing support for the lower extremities and improving balance. Because of the diversity of ambulation aids, their prescription and fit should be carefully evaluated by a phy-sician or licensed physical therapist. The person with a disability Transfers and Ambulation Aids
# Appendix A 301
should receive instruction in their use, including proper gait pat-
tern, ascending and descending stairs, and sitting and arising. Pre-ambulation exercises and training are often necessary for persons with severe disabilities (Jebsen, 1967).302Senior Fitness
# Walkers
Types of Walkers
 1. Walkerette. This model has runners attached to
the bars and is pushed forward along the floor. It is not lifted by the user like a standard walker.
 2. Roller Walker.
 This model has wheels on the
front legs so that the individual can raise the rear legs off the floor and roll the walker forward.
 3. Crutch Walker. This model has crutches attached
to the horizontal bars to support body weight. It also has a seat for the individual to rest on when fatigued. The crutches can be removed or draped to the sides when not in use.
 4. Walk-abouts.
 A walkabouts open front design
assists persons with limited weight bearing abili-ties. A walkabout walker is comprised of a frame with casters at the base that is open in the front. A support frame is located in the back with a support system that wraps around the torso with straps or buckles and has a seat that fits between the legs to partially support the weight of the body.

# Crutches
The use of crutches requires more balance, strength, and coordina-
tion than a walker. One or two crutches may be used, depending upon the extent of support needed.
Types of Crutches
 1. Axillary (armpit) crutch.
 2. Canadian, Lofstrand, or Elbow Extension Crutch.
This crutch has no shoulder rest and is usually pre-scribed for persons who can ambulate using the four-point crutch gait and need support for weak arm musculature.
 3. Gutter Crutch. This crutch has been designed for persons with a significant flexion deformity at the elbow, painful wrist, or very poor hand function. The forearm may be secured to the crutch by a Velcro strap or other fastening.Transfers and Ambulation Aids
# Appendix A 303
1. Auxillary Crutch
2. Canadian Crutch
3. Gutter Crutch
Figure APa-5  Types of crutches: Auxillary Crutch, Canadian Crutch,
and Gutter Crutch.
Crutch Adjustment
The crutch height should always be adjustable. It is also prefera-
ble to use crutches that are adjustable with respect to length and position of the arm support (Cash, 1976). The length of the axil-lary crutch should extend from a point two inches below the axilla (armpit) to a point near the foot indicated in the illustration below. The height of the handgrip should be positioned so that the elbow is flexed between 15¬∞ to 30¬∞. The wrists should be hyperextended and the weight borne on the palms. When fitting axillary crutches, it is essential that the user be instructed not to bear weight on the axillary bar. This may cause compres-sion of the radial nerve, resulting in paral-ysis, which may take months to resolve. The true purpose of this bar is to provide lateral stability of the crutch via pressure against the chest wall. Regularly check crutch tips for worn areas, cracking, or plugging of the grooves with lint and dirt.
# Canes
Canes are generally prescribed when some weight bearing can be taken on the affected extremity or when only mild balance deficits exist. It is difficult for a person to develop a normal walking pattern with one cane. Because a cane supports approximately 20-25 per-cent of the body weight, the tendency is to lean the body over the cane and shorten the stride on that side.
Types of Canes
 1. Standard Cane. This model is made of wood or alu-
minum with a C-curved handle. A telescoping cane is available that can be adjusted to between 22 to 38 inches.304Senior Fitness
6‚Äù
2‚Äù
 2. Tripod Cane. This model has three prongs at the end
of the shaft with flexible rubber sockets, allowing for

movement of the shaft while the prongs remain in contact with the ground during ambulation.
 3. Quad Cane. This model has four prongs that come in contact with the floor. It is adjustable in length and provides maximum support and balance with four contact points on the ground. This device is frequently prescribed for those with athetoid cere-bral palsy.
Cane Adjustment
The length of the cane is determined by measuring the vertical dis-
tance from the greater trochanter (the top of the femur) to the floor. The elbow should be flexed approximately 15¬∞ to 30¬∞. The cane should be held in the hand opposite the affected leg. During ambulation, the cane should be held fairly close to the side to avoid leaning.
# Wheelchairs
The market currently offers a wide selection of wheelchairs, from heavy-duty chairs to light sport models with racing tires. In addi-tion, a variety of sizes are available such as Adult, Narrow-Adult, Tall, Junior, and Children‚Äôs sizes. Some companies will even cus-tomize the size of a chair to the individual. Sport and Spokes, a magazine for athletes with disabilities, publishes an annual review of the latest models of sport wheelchairs. A physician or physical therapist familiar with the sizes, models, and components of wheel-chairs will prescribe a suitable chair for an individual.
Assistants should become familiar with the basic components of
the wheelchair and their operation. The following page contains a description of the most basic components of a typical wheelchair. There are many additional accessories, which can be attached to the wheelchair to enhance its effectiveness to the user. Transfers and Ambulation Aids
# Appendix A 305
 1. Wheels. For ease of ride over outdoor terrain (soft,
sandy or rough ground), pneumatic tires (i.e., air-
filled, like bicycle tires) are recommended.
 2. Handrims. Handrims are connected to the wheels
to allow the user to move the wheelchair without injuring the hands. Handrims come in a variety of sizes and types, depending on the sport and the ability of the individual. Rubber-coated handrims are available to persons with quadriplegia. For the client with severe limitations of grip, a handrim consisting of eight rubber-tipped vertical projec
-
tions is available.
 3. Backrest. The height of the backrest depends on

the height of the user and the degree of trunk sta-bility. Reclining backrests are available for those who need to be in a partially or fully reclined position.
 4. Armrest.
 Armrests may be either detached or
fixed. Detachable armrests easily lift off to allow the convenience of side transfers. The height of the armrests can usually be adjusted to accommodate the changes created by a wheelchair cushion.
 5. Wheel locks
(brakes). These brakes prevent the
wheelchair from rolling forward or backward on inclines and during transfers.
 6. Casters.
 These small wheels sit towards the front
of the wheelchair. Casters are most stable when they have anti-flutter caster bearings. Casters also come in the following styles: heavy-duty, light-weight, and pneumatic (for a cushioned ride with a freer roll).
 7. Front rigging.
 This consists of a footrest or leg-
rest. Legrests are used with those who need the legs elevated. Both types of rigging typically have a swing-away feature, which allows for a close approach to transfer surfaces. Heel-loops help pre-vent feet from sliding off of the footplate.306Senior Fitness
Transfers and Ambulation Aids
# Appendix A 307
# Unit Summary
 I. A wheelchair is part of the user‚Äôs body and should be
respected as such. Always ask if the user needs assis-tance and be familiar with the basics of assisting a person in a wheelchair before attempting to move the chair.
 II. A ‚Äútransfer‚Äù is the relocation of an individual from one surface to another, and there are many variations on the basic ‚Äúsitting‚Äù and ‚Äústanding‚Äù transfer.
 a. Many people have developed a personal technique for doing a transfer, and this preference should always be discussed before an instructor attempts to help.
 b. Always allow clients to do as much of the transfer for themselves as is safely possible.
 III. Progressive mobilization is the gradual development of a person‚Äôs ability to move around and gain indepen-dence. Many mat and bed activities can be used to assist with this process.
 IV. People with a lower-extremity disability usually require some sort of ambulation aid such as a cane or crutch-es. Pre-ambulation exercises and training can be nec
-
essary for people with severe disabilities.
